Razi's Refutation: Proofs Imply Infinite Parts
Razi exposes the fallacy by showing the proofs imply infinite actual parts, an impossible conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi turns the philosophers' own proofs against them to show they imply an absurd conclusion of infinite actual parts.

Core Idea
Razi uses the proof's own logic to force an impossible infinite regress.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi examines the very proofs that have been offered for atomism. He notes that no one has raised a valid objection, but he finds a fundamental flaw. He demonstrates that if the proofs were sound, they would require a body to be composed of an actual infinity of parts—a result universally rejected. This lesson walks through his refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for its overall flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the confident tone: Razi declares he will show the proof leads to a false conclusion.
The Arabic text ends abruptly; focus on the part we have.
2
Identify the false conclusion
Find the sentence where Razi states the conclusion that would follow if the proofs were sound. That conclusion is: 'the body is composed of parts infinite in actuality.' Why is this considered false?
This is the key claim that Razi will try to prove follows from the proofs.
3
Follow the first step of the argument
Razi argues that if a body meets something with one end and something else with the other end, those two meetings are actually distinct. That implies the loci (ends) are also actually distinct.
This sets up the need for actual division.
4
Trace the reasoning: accidents and division
If the loci are accidents, the same reasoning applies to them, leading to infinite division. But Razi says the chain ends when multiplicity occurs in the body's essence, resulting in actual halves.
The argument then repeats: each half meets a half, suggesting infinite regress.
5
Understand the overall point
Razi has shown that the philosophers' proofs force an actual infinite division, which is impossible. Therefore, the proofs are fallacious.
This refutes the original arguments for atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اعلم : أن أحداً من المتكلمين ما أورد على شيء من هذه البراهين : كلاماً ، يصلح لأن يلتفت إليه العاقل . إلا أنا نقول : للبحث فيه مجال . وتقريره : أنا نبين أن هذه الحجة : تنتج نتيجة باطلة بالاتفاق [ وإذا ثبت هذا ، ظهر لنا : أنها حجة مغالطية ، وليست حجة حقيقية. أما بيان أنها تنتج نتيجة باطلة بالاتفاق (۲) ) فهو أنا نقول : إن هذه الحجة لو صحت ، لأنتجت كون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل. ومعلوم : أن هذه النتيجة باطلة أما بيان الأول . فلأن الجسم إذا لقي بأحد طرفيه شيئاً ، وبالطرف الثاني منه شيئاً آخر، فهاتان الملاقاتان متغايرتان بالفعل ، فوجب أن يكون محلاهما متغايرين بالفعل . ثم نقول : هذان المحلان إن كانا عرضين عاد التقسيم فيه ، ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي آخر الأمر إلى وقوع الكثرة في ذات الجسم ، فحينئذ ينتصف ذلك الجسم بالفعل . ثم إن كل واحد من ذينك النصفين يلقى النصف
And know: That no one among the mutakallimun has raised against any of these proofs a statement worthy of the intelligent person's attention. Except that we say: there is room for investigation in it. And its establishment: that we show that this proof produces a false result by agreement. [And if this is established, it becomes clear to us that it is a fallacious proof, not a real proof. As for the demonstration that it produces a false result by agreement, it is that we say: If this proof were sound, it would produce that the body is composed of parts infinite in actuality. And it is known that this result is false. As for the demonstration of the first: Because if a body meets something with one of its ends, and with the second end meets something else, then these two meetings are actually distinct, so their loci must be actually distinct. Then we say: These two loci, if they are accidents, division recurs in them, and it does not regress endlessly, but ends ultimately to the occurrence of multiplicity in the essence of the body, so then that body is actually divided into two halves. Then each of those two halves meets the half
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage provides a critique of the four proofs just presented. The author (presumably Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) asserts that no mutakallim has offered a valid objection to these proofs, but he finds room for investigation. He sets out to show that these proofs produce a false conclusion by consensus. The false conclusion is that if the proofs were sound, they would entail that a body is composed of an actual infinity of parts — a claim universally rejected. To demonstrate this, he argues: when a body meets one thing at one end and another thing at the other end, those two meetings are actually distinct, so their loci (the ends) must be actually distinct. If those loci are accidents (i.e., non-substantial), the division continues until multiplicity arises in the body's essence, resulting in an actual division into halves. The argument then begins to repeat (each half meets a half), suggesting an infinite regress. The text cuts off before completing the reasoning, but the overall point is clear: the philosophers’ proofs, if valid, lead to an impossible infinite division, so they must be fallacious.

The False Premise of Contiguity in Substances
The author identifies the false premise as the assumption of real contiguity and redefines it as mere absence of gap.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the assumption that substances are contiguous in a real sense is a false premise in kalam arguments, and how the author redefines contiguity as mere absence of a gap.

Core Idea
Contiguity is not a real relation; it is merely the absence of a gap between substances.

In this passage, the author continues his refutation of an earlier proof by pinpointing its false premise. He argues that the proof assumes substances are connected by a real relation of contiguity, which his school denies. Instead, he offers a precise definition: contiguity is only the state where no third substance can intervene. Any additional notion stems from imagination, which is not a valid source of knowledge in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the false premise
Read the opening lines where the author states that a false conclusion indicates a false premise. He proposes two possibilities, though only the first is discussed here. Focus on the first possibility: the proof assumes parts are meeting and contiguous.
The author says 'there are two possibilities' but only details the first.
2
Understand the school's position on contiguity
The author explains that every substance occupies its own space with no real relation to another. Contiguity is defined merely as the absence of a gap between two substances. Note the key terms: mumāssa and mulāqā.
3
Examine the rejection of an extra concept
The author argues that if contiguity means only the absence of a gap, then the proof's claim about meeting with faces adds nothing new. If the opponent means something else, it is denied. This is a crucial logical move.
The author is denying any additional ontological reality to contiguity.
4
Consider the imagination argument and analogies
The author asserts that any supposed extra concept comes from imagination (wahm) and fancy (khayāl), which his school rejects as unreliable. He gives two analogies: the person at the edge of the world and the falling mountain. Reflect on why these analogies support his point.
The analogies show that intuition often contradicts demonstration, and when conflict occurs, demonstration takes precedence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : كذب النتيجة يدل على اشتمال القياس على مقدمة كاذبة . فليطلب أن المقدمة الكاذبة ما هي ؟ فنقول : فيه احتمالان : الأول : أن يقال : هذه الحجة مبنية على أن تلك الأجزاء متلاقية متماسة ، وذلك باطل عندنا . فإن الحق عندنا : أن كل جوهر فهو مختص بحيز نفسه ، ولا تعلق له البتة بالجوهر الآخر لا بالمماسة ، ولا بالملاقاة . بل الحق : أن الجوهرين إذا وقعا بحيث لا يمكن أن يتخللهما ثالث ، سميناهما بالمتلاقين وبالمتماسين وبالمتجاورين وبالمتصلين. وإن وقعا بحيث يمكن أن يتخللها ثالث ، سميناهما بالمتباعدين وبالمفترقين وبالمتباينين . فأما أن يحصل للملاقاة وللمماسة : مفهوم آخر سوى ما ذكرناه ، فهذا ممنوع ولا يقال : إن صريح العقل يحكم بأن الجوهرين إذا وجدا ، بحيث لا يمكن أن يتخللهما ثالث ، فإنه يكون أحدهما ملاقياً للآخر، ومماساً له . لأنا نقول : إن عنيتم بالملاقاة والمماسة ، نفس كونهما واقعين، بحيث لا يبقى بينهما فرجة ، ولا شيء مغاير . فهذا معقول . إلا أن على هذا التقدير لا يبقى لقولكم : إنه يحاس بأحد وجهيه ما على يمينه ، وبالوجه الثاني ما على يساره : مفهوم زائد. وإن عنيتم به أمراً آخر وراء ذلك ، فهو ممنوع . ولا ينكر أن الوهم والخيال ، يحكم فيه بحصول أمر زائد . إلا أن مذهبكم : أن حكم الوهم والخيال غير مقبول . ألا ترى أن صريح العقل يحكم بأن الواقف على طرف العالم ، لا بد وأن يميز الجانب الذي يحاذي وجهه من الجانب الذي يحاذي قفاه . ثم إنكم ذكرتم أن ذلك من عمل الوهم والخيال ، وأنه لا عبرة به البتة . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر ههنا أيضاً كذلك ؟ . وأيضاً : فأنتم لما زعمتم : أنه لا بد وأن يحصل بين صعود الحجر بالقسر ، ونزوله بالطبع : سكون . فإذا قبل لكم : لو قدرنا أن جبلاً عظيماً ، كان ينزل من السماء إلى الأرض في تلك اللحظة . فلو كان ذلك السكون واجباً ، لزم أن يكون سكونه موجباً لسكون ذلك الجبل المتناول . ثم إنكم التزمتم ذلك ، وقلتم هذا ، وإن كان الوهم لا يقبله ، والخيال لا يساعد عليه ، إلا أن البرهان اليقيني لما ساقنا إليه ، وجب التزامه . فكذا ههنا :
Then we say: The falsity of the conclusion indicates that the syllogism contains a false premise. So let it be sought what the false premise is. We say: There are two possibilities. The first: it is said that this proof is based on those parts being meeting and contiguous, and that is false according to us. For the truth according to us is that every substance (jawhar) is specific to its own space, and it has no relation at all to another substance, neither by contiguity (mumāssa) nor by meeting (mulāqā). Rather, the truth is that when two substances occur such that no third can interpose between them, we call them meeting, contiguous, adjacent, and connected. And if they occur such that a third can interpose between them, we call them distant, separate, and distinct. That there should occur for meeting and contiguity another concept besides what we have mentioned—this is denied. And it is not said that the clear intellect judges that when two substances exist such that no third can interpose between them, then one of them meets the other and is contiguous to it. For we say: If by meeting and contiguity you mean precisely their occurring such that no gap remains between them and no distinct thing, then this is intelligible. However, on this supposition, there remains no additional meaning for your statement: 'It meets with one of its faces what is on its right, and with the second face what is on its left.' And if you mean something else beyond that, it is denied. And it is not denied that imagination (wahm) and fancy (khayāl) judge that there occurs an additional thing. However, your school is that the judgment of imagination and fancy is unacceptable. Do you not see that clear intellect judges that one standing at the edge of the world must distinguish the side facing his face from the side facing his back? Then you mentioned that that is from the work of imagination and fancy, and that it is of no account at all. So why may the matter here not also be like that? Also: When you claimed that between the rising of a stone by force and its falling by nature there must be a state of rest. If it is said to you: Suppose a great mountain were descending from the sky to the earth at that moment. If that rest were necessary, then its rest would necessitate the rest of that descending mountain. Then you accepted that, saying this, even if imagination does not accept it and fancy does not support it, but since certain demonstration (burhān yaqīnī) led us to it, it must be accepted. So too here:
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the false premise in the previously refuted proof. The author states that the falsity of the conclusion implies that the syllogism contains a false premise. He proposes two possibilities (though only the first is fully discussed here). The first possibility is that the proof assumes that the parts are meeting and contiguous (mutalāqiya mutamāssa), but according to the author's school, this assumption is false. The author explains his school's position: every substance (jawhar) occupies its own space and has no relation of contiguity or meeting with another. Instead, what they call 'meeting' or 'contiguity' is merely that two substances are placed such that no third can interpose between them—there is no additional real quality. If the opponent means by meeting just that absence of gap, then the argument's claim that 'it meets with one face what is on its right' adds nothing. If they mean something else, it is denied. The author then argues that the supposed extra concept comes from imagination (wahm) and fancy (khayāl), which their own school rejects as unreliable. He gives two analogies: (1) The person at the edge of the world must distinguish sides, but they acknowledge this is imaginary and negligible. (2) The case of a stone's forced ascent and natural descent—they accepted an unintuitive conclusion about a necessary state of rest for a falling mountain because certain demonstration led them. So likewise here, if proof leads to a conclusion that contradicts imagination, it must be accepted. The passage ends mid-sentence, suggesting a complete analogy.

Correction: The author argues that contiguity is merely the absence of a gap; any extra notion is imaginary and rejected by sound demonstration.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The false premise in arguments about bodily composition is the assumption that substances have a real relation of contiguity; the correct view is that contiguity is merely the absence of a gap.

Refuting the Objection: Contact is Only a Conventional Term
Author refutes objection by defining contact nominally and citing proof over imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that the atomism proof depends on contact, by redefining contact as a nominal term and appealing to the supremacy of certain proof over imagination.

Core Idea
Contact is not a real relation; certain proof overrules imagination.

In this passage, the author faces an objection that his proof for the atom assumes that parts are in contact. He responds by redefining contact and meeting as merely the absence of intervening space, denying any extra-mental reality. Then he argues that even if imagination suggests otherwise, certain proof must be accepted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the author's dialectical style: he acknowledges the objection before refuting it.
2
Identify the objection and the false premise
The objector argues that since the conclusion is false, the syllogism must have a false premise. The author identifies two possibilities and focuses on the first: the premise that parts are in contact.
The false conclusion is not explicitly stated but refers to the impossibility of infinite division.
3
Understand the nominal definition of contact
The author defines contact and meeting as when two substances have no third substance between them. This is a conventional label, not a real relation. He denies any additional concept beyond this spatial description.
This is a key move: redefining terms to avoid the objection.
4
Examine the analogies from imagination
The author gives two analogies (man at world's edge, stone's rest) to show that your own school rejects imagination when it contradicts intellect or certain proof. Therefore, you should accept the negation of contact even if imagination objects.
The analogies are meant to show consistency in method.
5
Conclude with the certainty of atom proofs
The proofs for the indivisible atom are certain and necessitate denying contact in an extra-mental sense. Imagination must be set aside when faced with demonstrative proof.
This reinforces the overall argument for atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : كذب النتيجة يدل على اشتمال القياس على مقدمة كاذبة . فليطلب أن المقدمة الكاذبة ما هي ؟ فنقول : فيه احتمالان : الأول : أن يقال : هذه الحجة مبنية على أن تلك الأجزاء متلاقية متماسة ، وذلك باطل عندنا . فإن الحق عندنا : أن كل جوهر فهو مختص بحيز نفسه ، ولا تعلق له البتة بالجوهر الآخر لا بالمماسة ، ولا بالملاقاة . بل الحق : أن الجوهرين إذا وقعا بحيث لا يمكن أن يتخللهما ثالث ، سميناهما بالمتلاقين وبالمتماسين وبالمتجاورين وبالمتصلين. وإن وقعا بحيث يمكن أن يتخللها ثالث ، سميناهما بالمتباعدين وبالمفترقين وبالمتباينين . فأما أن يحصل للملاقاة وللمماسة : مفهوم آخر سوى ما ذكرناه ، فهذا ممنوع ولا يقال : إن صريح العقل يحكم بأن الجوهرين إذا وجدا ، بحيث لا يمكن أن يتخللهما ثالث ، فإنه يكون أحدهما املاقياً للآخر، ومماساً له . لأنا نقول : إن عنيتم بالملاقاة والمماسة ، نفس كونهما واقعين، بحيث لا يبقى بينهما فرجة ، ولا شيء مغاير . فهذا معقول . إلا أن على هذا التقدير لا يبقى لقولكم : إنه يحاس بأحد وجهيه ما على يمينه ، وبالوجه الثاني ما على يساره : مفهوم زائد. وإن عنيتم به أمراً آخر وراء ذلك ، فهو ممنوع . ولا ينكر أن الوهم والخيال ، يحكم فيه بحصول أمر زائد . إلا أن مذهبكم : أن حكم الوهم والخيال غير مقبول . ألا ترى أن صريح العقل يحكم بأن الواقف على طرف العالم ، لا بد وأن يميز الجانب الذي يحاذي وجهه من الجانب الذي يحاذي قفاه . ثم إنكم ذكرتم أن ذلك من عمل الوهم والخيال ، وأنه لا عبرة به البتة . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر ههنا أيضاً كذلك ؟ . وأيضاً : فأنتم لما زعمتم : أنه لا بد وأن يحصل بين صعود الحجر بالقسر ، ونزوله بالطبع : سكون . فإذا قبل لكم : لو قدرنا أن جبلاً عظيماً ، كان ينزل من السماء إلى الأرض في تلك اللحظة . فلو كان ذلك السكون واجباً ، لزم أن يكون سكونه موجباً لسكون ذلك الجبل التنازل . ثم إنكم التزمتم ذلك ، وقلتم هذا ، وإن كان الوهم لا يقبله ، والخيال لا يساعد عليه ، إلا أن البرهان اليقيني لما ساقنا إليه ، وجب التزامه . فكذا ههنا : الدلائل التي ذكرناها في إثبات الجوهر الفرد : دلائل يقينية لا تقبل الشك . ثم إن تلك الدلائل توجب القول بنفي الملاقاة ، ونفي التماس . فهذا وإن كان على خلاف حكم الوهم والخيال . إلا أنه لا بد من المصير إليه ، والاعتراف بحصوله .
Then we say: The falsity of the conclusion indicates that the syllogism contains a false premise. So let it be sought what the false premise is. We say: There are two possibilities: First: It might be said that this argument is based on the fact that those parts are meeting and in contact, and that is false according to us. For the truth according to us is that every substance (jawhar) is special to its own space, and it has absolutely no connection to the other substance, neither by contact (mumāssa) nor by meeting (mulāqāt). Rather, the truth is that when two substances occur such that no third can intervene between them, we call them 'meeting' (mutalāqiyayn), 'in contact' (mutamāssayn), 'adjacent' (mutajāwirāyn), and 'connected' (muttasilayn). And if they occur such that a third can intervene between them, we call them 'separated' (mutabā‘idayn), 'apart' (muftariqayn), and 'distinct' (mutabāyinayn). As for there being another concept for meeting and contact beyond what we have mentioned, this is denied. And it is not said that sound intellect judges that when two substances exist such that no third can intervene between them, then one of them is meeting and in contact with the other. For we say: If by meeting and contact you mean the very fact that they occur such that there remains no gap between them and no distinct thing, then this is intelligible. However, on this estimation, there remains for your statement 'it is sensed by one of its two faces what is on its right, and by the second face what is on its left' no additional concept. And if you mean by it something else beyond that, it is denied. It is not denied that imagination (wahm) and fancy (khayāl) judge that an additional thing occurs. However, your doctrine is that the judgment of imagination and fancy is unacceptable. Do you not see that sound intellect judges that the one standing at the edge of the world must distinguish the side facing his face from the side facing his back? Then you mentioned that that is from the work of imagination and fancy, and that it has no consideration at all. So why is it not permissible that the matter here also be like that? And also: When you claimed that there must occur between the ascent of a stone by coercion and its descent by nature a state of rest (sukūn). So if it is said to you: Suppose that a great mountain was descending from the sky to the earth at that moment. If that rest were necessary, it would necessitate that its rest would cause the rest of that descending mountain. Then you accepted that and said this, even though imagination does not accept it and fancy does not support it, but because necessary demonstration (burhān yaqīnī) led us to it, its acceptance is obligatory. So likewise here: The proofs we have mentioned in establishing the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) are certain proofs that admit no doubt. Then these proofs necessitate affirming the negation of meeting and negation of contact. So this, even if it contradicts the judgment of imagination and fancy, yet it is necessary to adopt it and acknowledge its occurrence.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the proof for the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). The objector argues that the conclusion (that division must stop) is false, so the syllogism must contain a false premise. The author then examines two possibilities for the false premise. The first possibility is that the proof's premise that the parts are in contact (mumāssa) and meeting (mulāqāt) is false. The author responds by clarifying his doctrine: each substance occupies its own space and has no connection with another substance, neither by contact nor meeting. Rather, contact and meeting are merely terms for when two substances are so close that no third can intervene between them. If another meaning is intended, it is denied. The author then argues that the objector's own school rejects the judgment of imagination (wahm) and fancy (khayāl) when it contradicts intellect, as in the example of the man at the edge of the world. Similarly, they accepted the necessity of rest (sukūn) in the stone's motion even if imagination rejects it. Therefore, the proofs for the atom, being certain, necessitate rejecting contact and meeting in the extra-mental sense, regardless of what imagination says. The passage defends the atomist doctrine by redefining contact and meeting as mere absence of intervening space, and by appealing to the supremacy of demonstrative proof over imagination.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
المماسة
الملاقاة
الوهم
الخيال
البرهان اليقيني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contact (mumāssa) between atoms is a real, extra-mental relation.
Correction: The author argues that contact is merely a conventional term for when no third substance can intervene, and it has no additional reality.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct definition of two substances being 'in contact' (mutamāssayn)?
A) When one is adjacent to the other with overlapping surfaces.
B) When no third substance can intervene between them, and they are called so conventionally.
C) When they touch each other with some physical interaction.
D) When they are separated by a minimal distance.

Key Takeaway
Contact is a nominal term, and certain demonstrative proof should be accepted even if imagination opposes it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a belief because it 'felt right' to your imagination, even though reason and proof pointed elsewhere? What would it take for you to let certain proof overturn your comfortable assumptions?
?
The author argues that accepting truth sometimes means rejecting the immediate verdict of your inner senses. Is there a teaching of Islam that you intellectually affirm but still find yourself resisting because it seems to contradict your natural intuition? Will you trust the proof or your fancy?
?
The objector's argument seemed plausible, but it rested on a false premise. Examine your own convictions: Are any of them built on assumptions you have never questioned? Are you willing to dig deeper and let the truth reshape your worldview, even if it feels unsettling?

Can Contact Be a Mental Relation? The Objection
Opponent questions contact as mental relation; states first premise that contact is a relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's objection that contact is a mental relation and why it seemingly avoids atom division in reality.

Core Idea
If contact is a mental relation, differences in contact only cause mental division.

The atomist argument for division relies on contact being a real physical attribute. But what if contact is merely a mental relation? This lesson presents the opponent's challenge and the first premise that contact is indeed a relation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to 'فإن التماس نسبة مخصوصة' to see the opponent's question and the first premise.
Notice the objector uses 'لم لا يجوز' to challenge the atomist assumption.
2
Understand the threat
The objector claims that contact (mumāssa) and meeting (mulāqāt) are relational matters (nisab wa iḍāfāt) that exist only in minds, not externally. Therefore, any differences in contacts would only cause division in the mind, not in atoms, undermining the atomist proof.
This objection tries to avoid the conclusion that atoms must divide in reality.
3
See the first premise
The author states the first premise: contact is indeed a relation. He calls this 'apparent' (zāhir) because contact is a specific relation between two distinct things.
This premise seems uncontroversial, but it sets up the next step.

The second question: Why is it not permissible to say that contact (mumāssa) and meeting (mulāqāt) are from the category of relations (nisab) and attributive relations (iḍāfāt)? And relational, attributive matters have absolutely no existence in external objects (a‘yān), but rather in minds (adh-hān). So differences in contacts necessitate the occurrence of division in minds, not in external objects. And on this estimation, the occurrence of division in external objects does not follow. So we need, in presenting this question, to present premises: The first premise: that contact and meeting are from the category of relations and attributive relations, and it is apparent that the matter is so. For contact is a specific relation occurring between two distinct things. And the second premise: an explanation that relations and attributive relations have no existence in external objects. The proof for it is that we say: The referent (musammā) of relation has no existence in external objects. So the specific relation must have no existence in external objects. As for the explanation that the referent of relation has no existence in external objects: it is that if this referent existed in external objects, then everything that is from the category of relations and attributive relations would exist in external objects. However, relation, given its existence in external objects, would be an attribute subsisting in something other (ghayr). So its subsistence in something other and its inherence in a locus would be a relation of its essence to that locus. Thus the relation of the relation would be additional to it, and infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow. So it is established that the referent of relation has no existence in external objects. And when this is established, it must be that no type of relation has existence in external objects, any type of relation you suppose. And since the referent of relation has no existence in external objects, then the subsisting quality (kayfiyya qā’ima) and the subsisting particularity (khuṣūṣiyya qā’ima) with it are not...
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the atomist proof, along with the author's two preliminary premises. The objector argues that contact (mumāssa) and meeting (mulāqāt) are merely relational attributes (nisab wa iḍāfāt) that exist only in the mind, not in extra-mental reality (a‘yān). Therefore, the differences in contacts that the atomist uses to force division would only cause mental division, not actual physical division, and the conclusion that atoms must divide in reality would not follow. To set up his response, the author first states two premises that need to be established. The first premise is that contact and meeting are indeed relations, which is obvious since contact is a specific relation between two distinct things. The second premise is that relations have no existence in external objects. The author provides a proof: if the referent (musammā) of a relation existed externally, it would be an attribute inhering in something else (ghayr), and its inherence would itself be a relation, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Therefore, the referent of relation has no external existence, and consequently all specific relations (including contact) are only mental. The passage concludes mid-sentence, but it sets up the argument that since contact is only mental, division resulting from differences in contacts would also be mental, thus undermining the opponent's objection.

?
The atomist argues that differences in contact force division in external objects. The objector disagrees. Have you ever ignored a sound argument because it made you uncomfortable? What does that reveal about your faith in intellect as a gift from Allah?
?
This lesson presents an objection that, if true, would mean that the physical division we observe (like breaking a stone) might be just a mental event. Does that possibility shake your understanding of causality and the constancy of Allah's creation? If you answer, 'No, because it's just a hypothetical,' then ask yourself: Are you dismissing it too quickly because it threatens your worldview? The Proof That Relations Have No External Existence
Author proves second premise: relations have no external existence, using infinite regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that relations (nisab) have no existence in external objects, using the infinite regress argument.

Core Idea
If a relation existed externally, its inherence would create an infinite regress of relations.

The opponent's objection hinges on the claim that relations are only mental. In this lesson, the author provides a rigorous proof for that claim using a reductio ad absurdum: if a relation existed externally, it would lead to an infinite regress. This establishes the second premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise
Read the Arabic passage from 'والمقدمة الثانية' to the end of the chunk to see the premise and its proof.
The text is cut off, but the core argument is complete enough.
2
Follow the proof
The author argues that the referent (musammā) of relation cannot exist externally. He uses a conditional: if it did, everything relational would exist externally, but then relation itself would be an attribute of something else, and its inherence would be another relation, leading to infinite regress.
This is a classic argument that relations are mind-dependent.
3
Understand infinite regress
The infinite regress (tasalsul) arises because if relation R exists in something X, then R's inherence in X is itself a relation R', which would also need to inhere, leading to R'', etc. This is impossible, so the initial premise is false.
This argument shows that relations cannot be real properties.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why Contact Does Not Cause Real Division
Objection: contact as relative, with two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that contact is merely a relative concept and its logical structure, including the proof that relations have no external existence.

Core Idea
Contact is a mental relation, not a real cause of division.

This passage presents the second objection against the argument for the indivisible atom from contact. The objector argues that since contact is merely a relative concept with no external reality, differences in contact cannot produce actual division in atoms. We will examine the two premises that support this objection and the regress argument proving relations are mind-dependent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that this is a structured objection with two premises.
2
Identify the two premises
The first premise states that contact is a relation. The second proves relations lack external existence.
The proof uses a regress argument.
3
Analyze the regress argument
If a relation existed externally, its subsistence would itself be a relation, leading to infinite regress.
This shows relations are mind-dependent.
4
Connect premises to conclusion
Since contact is a relation, it has no external existence, so differences in contact cannot cause multiplicity in external atoms.
5
Preview the refutation
The commentary hints that the original argument does not rely on contact's external existence. Reflect on why.
The atom's nature demands two directions regardless of how contact is classified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المماسة والملاقاة من باب النسب والإضافات ؟ والأمور النسبية الإضافية ، لا وجود لها البتة في الأعيان ، بل في الأذهان . فوجب أن يكون واختلافات المماسات ، توجب وقوع القسمة في الأذهان . لا في الأعيان. وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه لا يلزم وقوع القسمة في الأعيان . فنفتقر في تقرير هذا السؤال إلى تقرير مقدمات : فالمقدمة الأولى : إن المماسة والملاقاة من باب النسب والإضافات وظاهر أن الأمر كذلك. فإن التماس نسبة مخصوصة حاصلة بين شيئين متغايرين . والمقدمة الثانية : بيان أن النسب والإضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان والدليل عليه : أن نقول : مسمى النسبة لا وجود له في الأعيان . فالنسبة المخصوصة ، وجب أن لا يحصل لها وجود في الأعيان . أما بيان أن مسمى النسبة لا وجود له في الأعيان : هو أنه لو كان [ هذا (۱) ] المسمى موجوداً في الأعيان ، لكان [ كـل (٢) ] ما كان من باب النسب والإضافات ، موجوداً في الأعيان .. إلا أن النسبة بتقدير وجودها في الأعيان ، كانت صفة قائمة في الغير. فقيامها بالغير، وحلولها في المحل ، يكون نسبة لذاتها إلى ذلك المحل . فتكون نسبة النسبة زائدة عليها . ولزم التسلسل . فثبت : أن مسمى النسبة لا وجود له في الأعيان . وإذا ثبت هذا ، وجب أن لا يحصل لنوع من أنواع النسبة وجود في الأعيان. أي نوع فرض من أنواع النسبة. وإذا كان مسمى النسبة مما لا وجود له في الأعيان ، كانت الكيفية القائمة ، والخصوصية القائمة به غير موجودة في الأعيان . وإلا لزم قيام الصفة الموجودة ، بالموصوف المعدوم . وهو محال . فثبت : أن النسبة الخاصة لا وجود لها في الأعيان . وكنا قد بينا أن التماس والتلاقي من باب النسب والإضافات. فيلزم أن يقال : إن التماس والتلاقي من الأمور التي لا وجود لها في الأعيان . وإذا لم يكن لها وجود في الأعيان ، امتنع أن يكون اختلاف المماسات موجباً وقوع الكثرة في الأعيان وعلى هذا التقدير ، فإنه لا يلزم أن يكون الجزء الموجود في الأعيان منقسماً .
The second question: Why is it not permissible to say that touch (mumāssa) and meeting (mulāqāh) are of the category of relations (nisab) and accidents of relation (iḍāfāt)? And relative accidental matters have no existence at all in external entities (aʿyān), but only in minds (adhān). So it must be that differences in touches cause division to occur in minds, not in external entities. On this estimation, it does not necessitate the occurrence of division in external entities. So in setting forth this question we need to set forth premises: The first premise: touch and meeting are of the category of relations and accidents of relation, and it is apparent that the matter is so. For touch is a specific relation obtaining between two distinct things. The second premise: the explanation that relations and accidents of relation have no existence in external entities. The proof of that: we say: the referent of relation (musammā al-nisba) has no existence in external entities. So the specific relation must not obtain existence in external entities. As for the explanation that the referent of relation has no existence in external entities: it is that if this referent existed in external entities, then everything that is of the category of relations and accidents of relation would exist in external entities. However, if relation were supposed to exist in external entities, it would be an attribute subsisting in another. Its subsistence in another and its inherence in a locus would be a relation of its essence to that locus. Thus the relation of the relation would be additional to it, and an infinite regress would follow. So it is established that the referent of relation has no existence in external entities. And when this is established, it must be that no type of relation has existence in external entities—any type of relation that is supposed. And if the referent of relation is among things that have no existence in external entities, then the quality subsisting and the specification subsisting by it are non-existent in external entities. Otherwise, the subsistence of an existing attribute in a non-existent subject would be necessary, which is absurd. So it is established that the particular relation has no existence in external entities. And we have already explained that touch and meeting are of the category of relations and accidents of relation. So it follows that it must be said that touch and meeting are among things that have no existence in external entities. And if they have no existence in external entities, it is impossible for differences of touches to necessitate the occurrence of multiplicity in external entities. On this estimation, it does not necessitate that the part existing in external entities be divided.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit (السؤال الثاني). The objector raises a challenge: why can’t we say that contact (مماسة) and meeting (ملاقاة) are merely relative matters (نسب وإضافات) that exist only in the mind, not in external reality? If so, differences in contact would cause division only mentally, not externally, and thus the argument for the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) based on contact would not hold. The author then refutes this by setting up two premises. First, it is obvious that contact is a relation between two things. Second, relations have no external existence. The proof for the second premise uses a regress argument: if a relation existed externally, it would be an attribute subsisting in something else; its subsistence would itself be a relation, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, relations are mind-dependent. Since contact is a relation, it has no external existence. Consequently, differences in contact do not produce real multiplicity in external atoms. The refutation maintains that the original argument for indivisibility does not rely on the external existence of contact; rather, it follows from the necessity of two distinct directions in a single atom, which requires division in the atom itself, not in relations.

Key Terms
المماسة
الملاقاة
النسب والإضافات
الجزء
الجوهر الفرد
الأذهان
الأعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If contact is merely mental, it cannot cause any real effect on the atom.
Correction: The refutation shows that the original argument does not depend on contact's external existence; the atom must still be divided due to the necessity of two directions.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the second objection regarding contact in the atom?
A) Contact is impossible in external reality.
B) Contact is relative and exists only in the mind, so it does not cause real division.
C) Atoms are divisible because contact requires two faces.
D) Relations have external existence and thus cause division.

Key Takeaway
The objection that contact is relative and mind-dependent does not refute the argument for the indivisible atom because the argument does not require contact to have external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If touch and meeting exist only in the mind, then your sense of closeness to Allah through the acts of worship that involve physical contact (like shaking hands in the Hajj or touching the Black Stone) is ultimately a mental construct. Does this make it less real to you? How will you deepen your awareness that such actions are meant to connect you to your Creator, even if the contact itself is relative?
?
The infinite regress argument shows that if every relation required another relation, we would never arrive at a foundation. In your own life, do you ever find yourself relying on endless justifications for your actions, never settling on a firm principle? Will you today identify one foundational belief that should be sufficient for you to act upon, without needing further reasons?
?
You have just learned that a common objection to the indivisible atom—that contact is merely mental—does not refute the argument. How often do you accept objections to your faith or to a truth you hold, simply because they seem plausible at first? Will you commit to examining the deeper logic behind what you believe, so that you are not swayed by surface-level objections?

Third Objection: Are the Two Faces Accidents or Parts?
Third objection: even if contact is external, the two faces could be accidents, but the author refutes this.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the indivisible atom argument and how the author refutes it by demonstrating that even if the faces are accidents, multiplicity in the substance is still necessitated.

Core Idea
The two faces cannot be merely accidents because multiple contacts logically force division in the substance itself.

This passage presents the third objection: even if we grant that contact between atoms creates distinct faces, those faces could just be accidents, not parts. The author refutes this by pointing out that in the opponent's own system, surfaces and points are accidents yet contact is allowed, so the multiplicity of faces implies multiplicity in the substance, not just in accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its concession
The objector concedes that contact occurs externally and that it makes one face different from the other, but then questions why the two faces cannot be accidents subsisting in the atom.
Notice the structure: the objector grants the premise to attack the conclusion.
2
Understand the objector's claim
The objector argues that multiplicity occurs only in the accidents, not in the atom's essence. The opponent might retort that accidents cannot contact each other, so the faces must be parts.
This is a potential counter-objection within the text.
3
Follow the author's refutation
The author refutes by citing the opponent's own doctrine: bodies contact through surfaces, lines, and points, which are considered accidents. Therefore, accidents can indeed have contact, and the argument that the two faces are accidents does not prevent the necessity of multiplicity in the substance.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum based on the opponent's premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : لو سلمنا أن التلاقي والتماس ، حاصل في الأعيان. وسلمنا أن ذلك يوجب أن يكون أحد وجهي الجزء ، مغايراً للوجه الثاني منه . لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : هذان الوجهان : عرضان قائمان بذلك الجزء . والتعدد إنما وقع في العرضين القائمين بذلك الجزء . فأما أن يقع التعدد في نفس ذلك الجزء في ذاته ، فهذا ممنوع؟ لا يقال : الأعراض يستحيل عليها الملاقاة والمماسة ، وذلك يوجب أن يكون الوجهان الحاصلان في الجزء المتوسط : جزءين منه . لأنا نقول : لا نسلم أن الأعراض يمتنع عليها الملاقاة والمماسة . وبيانه : وهو أن عندكم الأجسام إنما تتلاقي بالسطوح ، والسطوح إنما تتلاقي بالخطوط والخطوط إنما تتلاقى بالنقط . ثم [إن (۲)] مذهبكم : أن السطوح والخطوط والنقط أعراض . فثبت : أن المحكوم عليه بالتلاقي على مذهبكم ليس إلا الأعراض . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : دليلكم يقتضي أن يكون أحد جانبي الجوهر مغايراً للجانب الثاني منه . وعندكم : كثرة الجوانب لا معنى لها ، إلا كثرة الأعراض والصفات . فأما وقوع الكثرة في الذات . فكيف يلزم ؟
The third question: If we grant that meeting and contact occur in external entities, and we grant that this necessitates that one of the two faces of the part is different from the second face, then why is it not permissible to say: these two faces are accidents (aʿrāḍ) subsisting in that part, and multiplicity only occurs in the two accidents subsisting in that part? As for multiplicity occurring in the part itself in its essence, this is prevented. It might be objected: accidents are impossible to have meeting and contact, and that would necessitate that the two faces occurring in the middle part are two parts of it. For we say: we do not concede that accidents are impossible to have meeting and contact. Its explanation: according to you, bodies only contact by surfaces, surfaces only contact by lines, and lines only contact by points. Then your doctrine is that surfaces, lines, and points are accidents. So it is established that what is judged to have contact according to your doctrine is nothing but accidents. And when this is established, we say: your proof necessitates that one side of the substance (jawhar) is different from the second side of it. And according to you, multiplicity of sides has no meaning except multiplicity of accidents and attributes. As for the occurrence of multiplicity in the essence, how can it be necessitated?
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer (السؤال الثالث). The objector concedes that contact exists externally and that it makes one face of the atom different from the other, but argues that these two faces are merely accidents (أعراض) subsisting in the atom. Thus, multiplicity occurs in the accidents, not in the atom's essence. The opponent might then object that accidents cannot contact each other, so the faces must be parts of the atom. The author refutes this by pointing out that in the opponent's own school (likely Ashʿarism), bodies contact through surfaces, lines, and points, which are considered accidents. Therefore, they already accept that accidents can have contact. Hence, the fact that the two faces are accidents does not prevent them from contacting and thereby necessitates that the two faces are distinct, but that distinction is still in accidents, not in the substance itself. The argument ends with a rhetorical question: if multiplicity of sides is just multiplicity of accidents, how can multiplicity in the essence be necessitated?

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author argues that even if the faces are accidents, the multiplicity of sides (accidents) logically entails multiplicity in the substance itself, because the argument for division is based on contact which applies equally to accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the objector in the third question, what are the two faces of the atom?
A) Parts of the atom's substance.
B) Accidents subsisting in the atom.
C) Separate substances.
D) Mental concepts.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the third objection by showing that even if the two faces are accidents, the opponent's own acceptance of contact between accidents (surfaces, lines, points) forces the conclusion that multiplicity in the substance is necessitated.

?

?

?

A Point's Alignment Does Not Imply Its Divisibility
The author argues that a point's alignment with many points does not imply its divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a point's being aligned with many points does not prove its divisibility, based on the distinction between relational properties and essence.

Core Idea
Relations are extrinsic; many relations don't split the essence.

In this passage, the author addresses a common objection about points and divisibility. Someone might argue that if a single point is aligned with many other points, it must have parts to account for all those alignments. The author counters by clarifying that alignment is a relational property, and many relations can coexist without affecting the unity of the point's essence. This distinction is crucial in kalam for understanding how indivisible atoms can relate to multiple things.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow. Notice the key terms: نقطة (point), محاذاة (alignment), مسامتة (opposite), إضافة (relation), كثرة (multiplicity), ذات (essence), مماسة (contact).
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim and its support
Find the sentence where the author states the core argument: that a single point's alignment with many points does not imply divisibility. Then locate the reason: alignment is relational (إضافية) and multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity in the essence.
The author uses the phrase 'لا يدل' to negate the implication.
3
Understand 'relational' vs 'essential'
Reflect on the distinction between relational properties (like being to the right of something) and essential properties (like being a point). Many relations can be true of a single entity without it having parts. This is the key insight.
Think of yourself: you can be to the left of one person, to the right of another, above a third, etc., without being divided.
4
Apply the same reasoning to contact
Notice the author extends the argument to contact (مماسة). If differing contacts with many bodies do not necessitate division, then the same principle applies. This shows the broader relevance.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm you grasped the main reason given in the passage.
The answer is that multiplicity of relations does not affect the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنها تكون مسامتة لجميع النقط التي يمكن فرضها في جميع أجسام العالم . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن كون النقطة الواحدة ، محاذية لجميع النقط المفترضة في العالم، لا يدل على كون النقط منقسمة . وما ذاك ، إلا لأن المحاذاة والمسامتة أمور إضافية. وكثرة الإضافات لا توجب وقوع الكثرة في الذات .. وإذا ثبت هذا ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الحال في اختلاف المماسة واقعاً على هذا الوجه ؟ والله أعلم .
For it is opposite to all the points that can be assumed in all the bodies of the world. If this is established, we say: the fact that a single point is aligned with all the assumed points in the world does not indicate that the points are divisible. And that is only because alignment and being opposite are relational matters. And multiplicity of relations does not necessitate the occurrence of multiplicity in the essence. And if this is established, why may it not be that the situation regarding difference of contact occurs in this manner? And God knows best.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a proof against an argument that would infer the divisibility of a point from its being aligned with many other points. The author first states that a point can be opposite (مسامتة) to all points in all bodies. Then he asserts that a single point's alignment (محاذاة) with all assumed points does not prove that points are divisible. The reason is that alignment and being opposite are relational (إضافية) properties; many relations (كثرة الإضافات) do not imply multiplicity in the essence (الذات). Therefore, the same could apply to contact (مماسة) — differing contacts do not necessitate division. The author concludes with humility: 'And God knows best.'

Correction: The author clarifies that alignment is a relational property; many relations can coexist without splitting the essence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Relational multiplicity does not entail essential multiplicity; a point can be aligned with many points without being divisible.

The atomist view of slow motion as mixed motion and rest
The author explains the atomist view that slow motion is motion mixed with rest, using a color mixing analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how atomists explain slow motion as a mixture of motion and rest, and the analogy they use to support this view.

Core Idea
Slow motion is motion mixed with rest, similar to colors mixing.

The atomists had a unique way of understanding slow motion. Instead of seeing it as a continuous reduction in speed, they argued that slow motion occurs when a body moves in some atoms and rests in others, creating a mixture that our senses perceive as slow. To illustrate, they use an analogy of mixing black and white powders to produce a grey colour. This passage explains that concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own explanation come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's use of the color mixing analogy—it is central to the argument.
2
Identify the atomist definition of slow motion
Find the sentence where the atomists define slow motion. What is the key phrase?
The definition is: 'لا معنى لكون الحركة بطيئة إلا أن الجسم يتحرك في بعض الأحياز، ويسكن في بعضها'
3
Understand the color mixing analogy
The author gives an analogy with white lead and ink. Explain why the mixture appears grey even though each particle retains its original color.
This analogy shows how the sense perceives a mixture as a single quality when the components are too small to distinguish.
4
Apply the analogy to slow motion
See how the color analogy explains slow motion. Why does the sense perceive the mixture as slow motion?
Because motion and rest alternate too quickly for the senses to separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في الدلائل المذكورة في نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ المبنية على بطء الحركات وسرعتها. اعلم : أن القائلين بالجزء الذي لا يتجزأ اتفقوا : على أنه لا معنى لكون الحركة بطيئة . إلا أن الجسم يتحرك في بعض الأحياز ، ويسكن في بعضها ، فتختلط الحركات بالسكنات . فالحس يدرك أن ذلك المختلط حركة موصوفة بالبطء . كما أنا إذا سحقنا الأسفيداج ، وسحقنا المداد . وخلطنا بعض تلك الأجزاء بالبعض يابساً . فإن القوة الباصرة تدرك ذلك الجسم المخلوط ، بلون متوسط بين السواد والبياض . لا لأجل أنه حصل هناك لون متوسط . لأنا فرضنا كون تلك الأجزاء يابسة . وإذا كانت يابسة فقد بقي الجزء الأسود على سواده ، والجزء الأبيض على بياضه . إلا أن تلك الأجزاء لما كانت في غاية الصغر، عجز الحس عن الوقوف على كل واحد منها بصفته المخصوصة. وإنما حصل له الشعور بذلك المجموع . فلا جرم أدرك ذلك المجموع على لون متوسط بين السواد والبياض. فكذا ههنا لما تحرك الجسم في بعض الأحياز، وسكن في بعضها ، وعجز الحس عن الوقوف على كل واحد منها بعينه ، لا جرم أحس بالأمر المختلط من الحركة والسكون . وذلك هو الحركة البطيئة. وأما القائلون بنفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ فقد اتفقوا على أن
The second chapter on the proofs mentioned concerning the negation of the indivisible part based on slowness and speed of motions. Know that those who affirm the indivisible part agreed that there is no meaning for a motion being slow except that the body moves in some positions and rests in some of them, so motions become mixed with rests. Then the sense perceives that mixture as a motion described with slowness. Just as when we grind white lead and grind ink, and mix some of those parts with some dry, then the visual faculty perceives that mixed body as a color intermediate between black and white—not because an intermediate color has occurred there, for we assumed those parts to be dry. And if they are dry, then the black part remains on its blackness and the white part on its whiteness. However, because those parts are extremely small, the sense is incapable of standing on each one of them with its particular quality, and it only obtains awareness of that aggregate. Hence, it necessarily perceives that aggregate as an intermediate color between black and white. Similarly here, when the body moves in some positions and rests in some of them, and the sense is incapable of standing on each one of them individually, it necessarily senses the matter mixed of motion and rest. And that is slow motion. As for those who deny the indivisible part, they agreed that...
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the atomist definition of slow motion. It begins with the chapter title indicating proofs against the indivisible part (الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ) based on slowness and speed. Then it states that atomists agree: slow motion is not a continuous reduction of speed but rather the body moving in some positions and resting in others, so that motion and rest are mixed. The sense perceives this mixture as a slow motion. To illustrate, the author gives an analogy: when white lead and ink are ground into fine powder and mixed dry, the eye sees an intermediate gray, even though each particle retains its original color. Because the particles are too small to distinguish individually, the aggregate appears as a single color. Similarly, in slow motion, the rapid alternation of motion and rest is imperceptible, so the overall movement is sensed as slow. The passage then begins to mention the opposing view but is cut off. This lesson covers the atomist view and their analogy.

Key Takeaway
The atomists explain slow motion as a mixture of motion and rest, using the analogy of mixed colors to illustrate how the senses perceive the mixture.

Slowness Not Due to Intervening Rests: The Voluntary Action Argument
Author argues that slowness is not due to intervening rests based on voluntary action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam scholars argue that slowness in motion is not caused by intervening rests, based on the nature of voluntary action.

Core Idea
Slow motion lacks intervening rests because the agent is unaware of discrete stops.

This passage presents a key argument from the kalam tradition against the idea that slowness results from tiny rests inserted between movements. By examining what we know about voluntary actions, the author shows that slowness must be understood differently. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: premise, observation, conclusion.
2
Identify the key premise about voluntary action and knowledge
The author states that a voluntary act requires knowledge of the intended act and desired outcome. How does this premise support the argument about slowness?
Think about what a walker would need to know if slowness were due to rests.
3
Understand the conclusion and its connection to the 'leap' debate
The author concludes that slowness cannot be due to intervening rests, and notes that proponents of al-ṭafra (leap) use many proofs to show the same point. How does this argument fit into the larger discussion about motion?
The conclusion is that speed difference is not about rests at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفعل الحاصل بالقصد والاختيار ، لا يحصل إلا مع العلم ، بالفعل المقصود والأمر المطلوب ، فكان يجب فيمن يمشي مشياً بطيئاً ، أن يعلم بالضرورة : أنه تحرك في الحيز الفلاني ، وأنه وقف في الحيز الفلاني . لأن الفعل الذي فعله بقصده واختياره ، لا بد وأن يكون عالماً بأنه كيف اختاره ؟ وكيف أوجده ؟ ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، بل الذي يمشي مشياً بطيئاً ، يعتقد أنه متحرك في جميع صفة البطء . علمنا : أن البطء في الحركة ، لا يمكن أن يكون لأجل تخلل السكنات . واعلم : أن ههنا وجوهاً كثيرة ، يستدل بها القائلون بإثبات الطفرة . ونحن ننقلها ، ونبين أنها دالة على أنه قد توجد حركتان خاليتان عن تخلل ، مع أن إحداهما أسرع من الأخرى. وحينئذ تصير تلك الوجوه كلها دالة على أن البطء ليس لأجل تخلل السكنات . فثبت بهذه الوجوه : أن التفاوت بين الحركة السريعة والبطيئة ، ليس لأجل تخلل السكنات .
The act that occurs by intention and choice only occurs with knowledge of the intended act and the desired matter. So it would be necessary for one who walks slowly to know necessarily that he moved in such-and-such a location and that he stopped in such-and-such a location, because the act he did by his intention and choice must be such that he knows how he chose it and how he brought it about. Since this is not the case, rather the one who walks slowly believes that he is moving in the entire quality of slowness, we know that slowness in motion cannot be due to the insertion of rests. Know that there are many ways by which those who affirm al-ṭafra (leap) argue. We will transmit them and show that they indicate that there may exist two motions free from insertion, while one of them is faster than the other. Then all those ways become indicative that slowness is not due to the insertion of rests. So it is established by these ways that the difference between fast and slow motion is not due to the insertion of rests.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that slowness in motion is not caused by intervening rests, based on the nature of voluntary action. The author begins by stating that a voluntary act requires knowledge of the intended act and desired outcome. Therefore, a person walking slowly would have to know each individual movement and stop if there were intervening rests. Since the walker does not have such discrete awareness, slowness cannot be due to inserted rests. The author then notes that proponents of the 'leap' (al-ṭafra) theory use many proofs to argue for the possibility of two motions without intervening rests, one faster than the other. All these proofs lead to the conclusion that the difference between fast and slow motion is not due to intervening rests.

Key Terms

السكنات المتخللة
الطفرة

Misconception: One might think slowness is merely slower speed due to more frequent small pauses.
Correction: The passage argues that slowness is not caused by intervening rests at all; the difference in speed is fundamental.

Proof of Infinite Divisibility of Body and Time
Proof that body and time are infinitely divisible via speed comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument from differing speeds that establishes the infinite divisibility of both body (substance) and time.

Core Idea
Speed differences force matter and time to be infinitely divisible.

This passage presents a key kalam argument that uses the simple observation of fast and slow motion to prove a deep metaphysical claim: that space and time have no smallest unit. The author builds on the previous conclusion that slowness is not caused by rests, and now shows that comparing a fast and a slow mover necessitates splitting atoms and moments without end.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: two symmetrical parts—one from equal time, one from equal distance.
The argument is conditional: 'if... then impossible, so therefore division.'
2
Understand the first scenario
Focus on the first part: if a fast mover and a slow mover both cover one atom in the same time, the slow mover would be as fast as the fast—impossible. Therefore, the slow mover must cover less than an atom, so the atom is divisible.
This shows that distance (and thus the atom) must be divisible.
3
Understand the second scenario
Now consider the reverse: if a slow mover covers one atom in a given time, the fast mover must cover that atom in less time. Hence time itself must be divisible.
This shows time is divisible.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
The two scenarios together demonstrate that both space and time are infinitely divisible—the desired conclusion. Reflect on how this argument supports the kalam view against atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : وجب أن يكون الجسم قابلاً للقسمة إلى غير النهاية ، وأن يكون الزمان أيضاً قابلاً للقسمة إلى غير النهاية . والدليل عليه : أن المتحرك السريع ، إذا تحرك على جوهر واحد ، في قدر من الزمان. ففي مثل ذلك الزمان ، إذا تحرك المتحرك البطيء على جوهر واحد ، لزم أن يكون البطيء مثل السريع وهو محال، فوجب أن يتحرك المتحرك البطيء في مثل تلك المدة على أقل من الجوهر الواحد . وذلك يوجب انقسام الجوهر. وأيضاً : المتحرك البطيء إذا تحرك على جوهر واحد ، في قدر من الزمان . فالسريع إما أن يتحرك على الجوهر الواحد ، في مثل ذلك الزمان ، أو في أقل منه . والأول باطل وإلا لزم أن يكون السريع مثل البطيء . وإنه محال . بقي الثاني وهو أن السريع يتحرك على الجوهر الواحد ، في أقل من الزمان، الذي يتحرك البطيء فيه على الجوهر الواحد . فثبت : أن السريع والبطيء إذا تساويا في الزمان ، كانت مسافة البطيء أقل ، فتنقسم المسافة . وأيضاً : السريع والبطيء إذا تساويا في المسافة ، كان زمان السريع أقل ، فينقسم الزمان . وبهذا الطريق يظهر أن المسافة قابلة للقسمة أبداً وأن الزمان قابل للقسمة أبداً وهو المطلوب فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه الحجة
And when this is established, we say: it is necessary that body be divisible infinitely, and that time also be divisible infinitely. The proof of that: when a fast mover moves over one atom (jawhar) in a measure of time, then if a slow mover moves over one atom in that same measure of time, the slow would be like the fast, which is impossible. So it is necessary that the slow mover moves in that same period over less than one atom, and that necessitates the division of the atom. Also: when a slow mover moves over one atom in a measure of time, then the fast mover either moves over one atom in that same time or in less. The first is false, otherwise the fast would be like the slow, which is impossible. The second remains: that the fast mover moves over one atom in less time than the slow moves over one atom. So it is established: when the fast and slow are equal in time, the distance of the slow is less, so distance divides. Also: when the fast and slow are equal in distance, the time of the fast is less, so time divides. And in this way it becomes clear that distance is divisible ad infinitum and time is divisible ad infinitum, which is the desired conclusion. So this completes the exposition of this argument.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a proof that body (i.e., substance or atom) and time are infinitely divisible, based on the comparison of fast and slow motions. The author builds on the previous conclusion that slowness is not due to intervening rests. Now he argues: if a fast mover traverses one atom in a certain time, and a slow mover also traverses one atom in the same time, then the slow mover would be as fast as the fast, which is absurd. Therefore, the slow mover must traverse less than one atom in that time, so the atom must be divisible. Similarly, if a slow mover traverses one atom in a time, the fast mover must traverse that atom in less time, so time must be divisible. From equal time, distance divides; from equal distance, time divides. Thus, both space and time are infinitely divisible. This argument supports the theologians' view that speed difference implies infinite divisibility.

Correction: The difference in speed between a fast and slow mover over the same distance or time forces the division of both the atom (spatial unit) and the moment (temporal unit), showing infinite divisibility.

Mutakallimun's Argument for Slowness via Rests and First Response
Mutakallimun's argument for slowness via rests and initial refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument that slowness requires intervening rests and the author's initial refutation that slowness cannot occur during entry into existence.

Core Idea
Slowness is not just less speed; it requires actual rests between motions.

The mutakallimun argue that motion consists of successive occurrences in space, and slowness can only arise from rests inserted between these occurrences. But the author counters that a thing enters existence all at once, so slowness cannot happen at entry; it must occur after existence, either as rest (sukūn) or as the fastest possible motion. This sets the stage for rebutting their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the mutakallimun's claim and proof
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the mutakallimun define motion and then argue that slowness requires rests.
They use a dichotomy to force the conclusion.
2
Understand the dichotomy about slowness
The passage presents two possibilities: slowness occurs at the moment of entry into existence, or after entry. Identify why the first is rejected.
Entry into existence is instantaneous, so no slowness can occur there.
3
See the consequences after entry
If after entry the thing remains, that is rest (sukūn). If it perishes instantly and is replaced, that is the fastest motion. Thus, slowness must be due to rests.
The author begins to rebut this by pointing out the corruption of the position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المتكلمون : البطء لا يمكن حصوله إلا لأجل تخلل السكنات. والدليل عليه : إنا بينا بالدلائل الكثيرة القاهرة : أنه لا معنى للحركة إلا حصولات متعاقبة ، في أحياز متلاصقة . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : البطء . إما أن يحصل حال دخول ذلك الشيء في الوجود ، وإما أن يحصل بعد دخوله في الوجود . والأول باطل . لأنا قد ذكرنا الدلائل الكثيرة في بيان أن الشيء الواحد ، يمتنع أن يدخل في الوجود ، على سبيل المهلة والتدريج . بل إنما يدخل في الوجود دفعة واحدة. وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع حصول البطء والسرعة في هذه الحالة ، وإذا بطل هذا ، بقي أن البطء والسرعة إنما يحصل بعد دخول الشيء في الوجود . فإنه إن بقي ذلك الشيء بعد دخوله في الوجود ، فذلك هو السكون . فيكون حصول البطء لأجل حصول ذلك السكون . وإن كان ذلك الشيء بعد دخوله في الوجود : لا يبقى ، بل يغني دفعة واحدة ، ويحدث بعده شيء آخر ، بشرط أن يكون حدوثه أيضاً دفعة . فذلك هو السريع ، الذي لا يعقل وجود سريع أسرع منه ، فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن قول من يقول : إنه توجد حركة أسرع من حركة ، لا لاجل تخلل السكنات : مفرع على قول من يقول : إن الحركة عبارة عن الحدوث على سبيل التدريج ولما ثبت فساد هذا ، يجب أن نجيب عن الوجوه التي تمسكوا بها . أما الوجه الأول : وهو قوله : « لو كان بطء الحركات ، لأجل تخلل السكنات ، لوجب أن تكون حركات الفرس الذي يكون شديد العدو ، أقل
The theologians (al-mutakallimūn) said: Slowness cannot occur except due to the insertion of rests. The proof of that: we have shown with many compelling proofs that there is no meaning for motion except successive occurrences in contiguous places. When this is established, we say: slowness either occurs at the moment of that thing's entry into existence, or after its entry into existence. The first is false, because we have presented many proofs that a single thing cannot enter into existence gradually and slowly, but rather enters into existence all at once. When this is so, the occurrence of slowness and speed in this state is impossible. When this is refuted, it remains that slowness and speed only occur after the thing's entry into existence. For if that thing remains after its entry into existence, that is rest (sukūn). So the occurrence of slowness is due to the occurrence of that rest. And if that thing after its entry into existence does not remain, but perishes all at once, and another thing occurs after it, with the condition that its occurrence also be all at once, then that is the fast, than which no faster fast can be conceived. So it is established by what we have said: that the statement of one who says that there exists a motion faster than another motion not due to the insertion of rests is based on the statement that motion is gradual occurrence. When the corruption of this is established, we must answer the proofs they have clung to. As for the first proof: it is his statement: 'If the slowness of motions were due to the insertion of rests, it would be necessary that the motions of a horse that is running extremely fast be fewer...'
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents the position of the theologians (mutakallimūn) that slowness in motion can only occur due to the insertion of rests (sakanāt). Their proof relies on the definition of motion as successive occurrences in contiguous places. They then argue that slowness cannot occur at the moment of a thing's entry into existence because that entry is instantaneous; thus it must occur after existence. If the thing remains after entry, that is rest, and slowness results. If it does not remain but perishes instantly, then that is the fastest possible motion. Therefore, any motion slower than that must involve rests. The author then states that this position is corrupted (fasād) and that he must answer their proofs. He begins with the first proof, which is an argument about a fast horse, but the passage ends mid-sentence. This sets up the refutation that will continue.

Correction: The mutakallimun argue that slowness requires actual rests (sakanāt) inserted between motions; without them, motion would be at maximum speed.
