The First Argument for the Leap (al-Ṭafra)
Ibn al-Malik presents the first argument of those who affirm the leap, using a thought experiment with three contiguous parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first argument used by proponents of the leap (al-ṭafra) in Islamic atomism, including the thought experiment and the deduction that forces the conclusion of a leap.

Core Idea
The leap argument uses a thought experiment to show that motion can skip over intermediate spaces.

This lesson explores a key argument for the theory of al-ṭafra (the leap) in Islamic atomism. The proponents of this theory sought to demonstrate that motion does not require traversing every intermediate point, and they built a clever thought experiment involving contiguous parts. We will examine the setup, the assumed motion, and the logical deduction that forces the conclusion of a leap.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the leap theory
Briefly grasp what the leap theory claims: that motion can occur without the moving object passing through every intermediate point. This was a controversial position in Islamic atomism.
The leap theory is associated with atomistic conceptions of space and time.
2
Set up the thought experiment
Visualize three contiguous parts (atoms) arranged in a line, labeled alif, ba, jim. An additional part is placed above the right side (the alif side). The line is assumed to move horizontally.
The arrangement is depicted as 'EO O' in the text, likely a diagram.
3
Simulate the motion
The entire line moves such that alif enters a new space, ba takes the place of alif, and jim takes the place of ba. Simultaneously, the upper part moves to the right along with alif.
4
Analyze the two possibilities
After motion, the upper part ends up in some space. Two possibilities arise: either it ends up directly above the new place of alif, or it ends up in a space further to the right.
These are the only logical options given the geometry.
5
Eliminate the impossible possibility
The first possibility (above the new place of alif) is ruled out because it would imply the upper part never lost contiguity with alif, contradicting the assumption that it moved. Thus the second possibility must hold, meaning the upper part made a leap over an intervening space.
This conclusion supports the leap theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج القائلون بالطفرة بوجوه :
الأول : إنا إذا قدرنا ثلاثة أجزاء متماسة ، على هذه [ الصورة (١) ] : EO O ثم وضعنا [ فوق (۳) ] طرفه الأيمن جزءاً ، ثم تحرك هذا الخط بكليته ، بحيث دخل الألف مكاناً جديداً ، ودخل الباء في مكان الألف ، ودخل الجيم في مكان الباء . ثم قدرنا : أن عند حركة الألف إلى المكان الجديد ، تحرك ذلك الجزء الفوقاني ، من الألف إلى الجانب الأيمن أيضاً . فهذا الجزء بعد هذه الحركة ، حصل في حيز . فهذا الجزء . إما(۳) أن يحصل فوق المكان الجديد الذي دخل الألف فيه ، أو وصل إلى حيز آخر على يمين ذلك الحيز . والأول باطل . وإلا لزم أن يقال : إنه لم يتحرك عن مماسة الألف . لكنا قد فرضناه متحركاً عنها ، فبقي الثاني . فعلى هذا التقدير يكون الجزء الفوقاني ،
The proponents of the leap argued with [several] aspects:
The first: If we assume three contiguous parts, in this [form] (1): EO O, then we place a part above (3) its right side, then this line moves in its entirety such that alif enters a new place, ba enters the place of alif, and jim enters the place of ba. Then we assume: that at the movement of alif to the new place, that upper part moved from alif to the right side as well. So this part after this movement comes to be in a space. So this part: either it comes to be above the new place that alif entered, or it reaches another space to the right of that space. The first is false; otherwise it would be necessary to say that it did not move from contiguity with alif, but we have assumed it to be moving from it. So the second remains. According to this assumption, the upper part...
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of those who affirm the theory of *al-ṭafra* (the leap), a concept in Islamic atomism concerning motion across discontinuous space. The argument is a thought experiment involving three contiguous parts (atoms) arranged in a line, with a label 'EO O' likely indicating a diagram. An additional part is placed above the right side of the line. The entire line then moves horizontally, so that part alif enters a new space, part ba takes the place of alif, and part jim takes the place of ba. Simultaneously, the upper part moves to the right along with alif. After this movement, the upper part ends up in some spatial location. The argument then considers two possibilities for this location: either it is directly above the new place of alif, or it is further to the right. The first possibility is deemed false because it would imply that the upper part never departed from its original contiguity with alif, contradicting the assumption that it moved. Therefore, the second possibility must hold—the upper part must have moved to a space discontinuous from its initial position, thereby exemplifying the leap across an intervening void. The argument is presented as a proof of the possibility of motion without traversing every intermediate point, a key claim of the proponents of *al-ṭafra*. The author does not complete the argument here, as the passage cuts off, but the logical structure is clear: the physical arrangement forces a conclusion that validates the leap.

Key Terms
طفرة (ṭafra)
جزء (part)
متماسة (contiguous)
حيز (space)
مماسة (contiguity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument assumes a single continuous line, but it actually relies on discrete atomic parts (ajzāʾ mutamāssa) central to Islamic atomism.
Correction: The argument explicitly uses 'three contiguous parts' (thalāthat ajzāʾ mutamāssa), which are discrete atoms in contact, not a continuous substance.

Checkpoint

A) Above the new place of alif
B) In a space to the right of that new place
C) Above the original place of alif

Key Takeaway
The leap argument deduces that an upper part placed above a moving line must skip a space, thereby demonstrating the possibility of motion without traversing every intermediate point.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument uses a thought experiment to challenge a common assumption about motion. Think of a belief you hold about the world that you have never questioned. Could there be a 'leap'—a possibility you have dismissed—that might actually be true? Are you willing to examine it without prejudice?
?
The proponents of the leap were willing to accept a conclusion that seemed counterintuitive. When you read something in Islamic theology that challenges your reason, do you automatically reject it, or do you pause to consider that your reason might be limited? What would it take for you to accept a truth that 'jumps' over your intellect?
?
The upper part in the argument moved alongside alif, yet ended up in a non-contiguous space. In your spiritual life, you may feel you are moving with the community (like alif), but are you truly keeping contiguity with the truth, or have you leaped away without realizing it? How can you ensure your actions remain contiguous with sincere faith?

The First Argument for al-Ṭafrah and Its Three Implications
Al-Razi presents the first argument for atomistic leap and the philosophers' three derivations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the thought experiment used to argue for atomistic leaps and how philosophers derive three conclusions from it regarding time, distance, and motion speed.

Core Idea
A thought experiment about moving parts reveals arguments for time's divisibility and speed variation.

This lesson presents al-Razi's first argument for the theory of al-ṭafrah. Through a simple mental experiment involving three contiguous parts and an upper part, both proponents of leaps and philosophers draw different conclusions. We will examine the experiment, the leap proponents' inference, and the three philosophical implications regarding time, distance, and motion speed.

Guided Path
1
Visualize the thought experiment
Imagine three contiguous parts (alif, ba, jim) arranged in a line, with a fourth part above the rightmost end. The entire line moves rightward, each part shifting to the next position. Simultaneously, the upper part also moves rightward.
The diagram in the text uses 'EO O' to represent the initial arrangement.
2
Understand the reasoning behind the leap
After motion, the upper part must either be above alif's new position or further right. It cannot be above alif because that would mean it didn't move away from touching alif. So it occupies a locus further right, meaning it moved through two loci while the lower part moved through only one. Proponents of al-ṭafrah take this as evidence for instantaneous leaps.
This is the key step that leads to the leap inference.
3
See how philosophers reinterpret it
Philosophers use the same scenario to derive three conclusions: time is infinitely divisible, distance is infinitely divisible, and two motions free from rest can differ in speed.
They do not accept the leap but use the reasoning to support divisibility.
4
Digest the three implications
The three implications are: (1) Time divisible because the same time contains two motions (upper moves in two parts, lower in one); (2) Distance divisible because half the time yields half the distance; (3) Two motions free from rest can differ in speed because the upper covers more distance in the same time.
These are foundational for later discussions on continuity and atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنا إذا قدرنا ثلاثة أجزاء متماسة ، على هذه الصورة : EO O ثم وضعنا فوق طرفه الأيمن جزءاً ، ثم تحرك هذا الخط بكليته ، بحيث دخل الألف مكاناً جديداً ، ودخل الباء في مكان الألف ، ودخل الجيم في مكان الباء . ثم قدرنا : أن عند حركة الألف إلى المكان الجديد ، تحرك ذلك الجزء الفوقاني ، من الألف إلى الجانب الأيمن أيضاً . فهذا الجزء بعد هذه الحركة ، حصل في حيز . فهذا الجزء . إما أن يحصل فوق المكان الجديد الذي دخل الألف فيه ، أو وصل إلى حيز آخر على يمين ذلك الحيز . والأول باطل . وإلا لزم أن يقال : إنه لم يتحرك عن مماسة الألف . لكنا قد فرضناه متحركاً عنها ، فبقي الثاني . فعلى هذا التقدير يكون الجزء الفوقاني ، قد تحرك في حيزين ، حال ما تحرك الجزء السفلاني في حيز واحد وعند هذا قال القائلون بالطفرة : إن هذا يدل على القول بالطفرة . وأما الفلاسفة : فإنهم احتجوا بهذا الكلام على إثبات أمور . أحدهما : إن هذا يدل على أن الزمان قابل للقسمة أبداً وذلك لأن في الزمان الذي تحرك الجزء التحتاني في حيز واحد ، فقد تحرك الجزء الفوقاني في جزءين . فيكون ذلك الزمان منقسم إلى قسمين . وثانيها : إن هذا يدل على أن المسافة قابلة للقسمة أبداً . وذلك لأن الزمان لما كان منقسماً ، وقد تحرك فيه الجزء السفلاني ، في حيز واحد . فالواقع في نصف ذلك الزمان ، هو الحركة على نصف تلك المسافة . فيلزم كون تلك المسافة منقسمة . وثالثها : إن هذا يدل على أنه قد توجد حركتان خاليتان ، عن مخالطة . مع أنه تكون إحداهما أسرع من الأخرى . لأن ههنا حركة الجزء التحتاني ، خالية عن مخالطة السكنات. وكذلك أيضاً حركة الجزء الفوقاني خالية عن مخالطة السكنات . أن حركة الجزء الفوقاني ، أسرع من حركة الجزء التحتاني . فهذه الحجة أفادت هذه المطالب الثلاثة .
First: if we suppose three contiguous parts, in this form: EO O, then we place a part above its right end, then this line moves in its entirety, such that alif enters a new place, ba enters the place of alif, and jim enters the place of ba. Then we suppose that upon the movement of alif to the new place, that upper part moves from alif also to the right side. So this part after this motion occurs in a locus. So this part either occurs above the new place which alif entered, or it reaches another locus to the right of that locus. The first is false; otherwise it would follow that it did not move away from touching alif, but we had supposed it moving away from it, so the second remains. On this assumption, the upper part has moved in two loci, while the lower part moved in one locus. And at this the proponents of al-ṭafrah said: this indicates the doctrine of al-ṭafrah. As for the philosophers, they used this speech to prove things. One: this indicates that time is always divisible, because in the time in which the lower part moved in one locus, the upper part moved in two parts, so that time is divided into two parts. Two: this indicates that distance is always divisible, because since time is divided, and the lower part moved in it in one locus, what occurs in half of that time is motion over half of that distance, so that distance is necessarily divided. Three: this indicates that two motions can exist free from admixture, while one of them is faster than the other, because here the motion of the lower part is free from admixture of rests, and likewise the motion of the upper part is free from admixture of rests, that the motion of the upper part is faster than the motion of the lower part. So this argument yielded these three points.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of those who advocate the theory of al-ṭafrah (atomistic leap), followed by the philosophers' extraction of three metaphysical implications from it. The argument begins with a thought experiment: suppose three contiguous parts (atoms) arranged in a line, with a fourth part placed above the right end. Then the entire line moves rightward, so each part shifts to the next position. Simultaneously, the upper part also moves rightward. Now, after this motion, the upper part must either be above the new position of the first part (alif) or further right. The first alternative fails because that would mean the upper part did not leave contact with alif, contradicting the assumption that it moved. Thus the upper part occupies a locus further right, meaning it moved through two loci while the lower part moved through only one. The proponents of al-ṭafrah take this as evidence for the possibility of instantaneous leaps (al-ṭafrah), since the upper part appears to skip over intermediate positions. The philosophers, however, use the same reasoning to establish three conclusions: (1) time is infinitely divisible, because the same time segment contains two different motions (the lower part's one locus and the upper part's two loci), so the time is composed of parts; (2) spatial distance is also infinitely divisible, because if time is divisible and motion covers a certain distance in that time, then half the time corresponds to half the distance; (3) two motions can coexist without any admixture of rest yet differ in speed, because both motions are continuous (free of rests) but one covers more distance in the same time, making it faster. The passage concludes that this first argument yields these three results.

Key Terms
طفرة (ṭafrah)
جزء (juz', part)
حيز (ḥayyiz, locus)
زمان (zamān, time)
مسافة (masāfah, distance)
حركة خالية عن مخالطة السكنات (motion free from admixture of rests)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument directly proves the existence of atomistic leaps.
Correction: The philosophers use the same reasoning to argue for infinite divisibility of time and space, not for leaps. The leap proponents only claim it indicates the possibility of leaps, but the philosophers show it leads to divisibility.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what does the first argument demonstrate about time and distance?
A) That time and distance are indivisible.
B) That time and distance are both infinitely divisible.
C) That time is divisible but distance is not.
D) That both are composed of finite atoms.

Key Takeaway
This first argument for al-ṭafrah, when analyzed, yields three philosophical proofs: time and distance are infinitely divisible, and two motions free from rest can differ in speed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience time passing, do you sense it as a continuous flow or as discrete instants? The philosophers argue that time must be infinitely divisible. Does this contradict your inner feeling? What does this tension tell you about the limits of your own perception? How will you humble your assumptions before the reality that Allah has created?
?
The mutakallimun saw this argument as proof for leaps, while the philosophers saw it as proof for infinite divisibility. Both are attempting to understand the same created reality. Are you willing to hold two competing interpretations in your mind without dismissing one? Is there a danger in feeling too certain too soon? What would it cost you to honestly consider a view that challenges your current understanding?
?
This small thought experiment leads to three profound conclusions about time, space, and motion. If such depth lies in a simple movement, how much more is hidden in the ordinary events of your life? Will you commit to training your mind to see the deeper implications of everyday experiences? Or will you remain content with surface-level understanding? What will you do this week to practice such reflection?

Al-Razi begins the second argument for atomistic leap with a modified thought experiment.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
A line's upper part moving opposite to the whole forces a sequential impossibility.

Al-Razi presents a second argument for the atomistic leap (al-tafrah) by modifying the first thought experiment. This time, the upper part of the line moves in the opposite direction to the whole line. The fragment we have sets up a dilemma about which part the upper part becomes adjacent to, and eliminates one option. Let's examine the reasoning step by step, keeping in mind that the conclusion is missing from the text.

Guided Path

Understand the setup
The line is in its first state, but the upper part moves left while the whole line moves right. Notice the deliberate symmetry.
2
Consider the upper part's possible positions
After moving, the upper part can only be adjacent to either bāʼ or jīm. These are the two parts to its left and right after the whole line moved right.
3

The text states that the first possibility (adjacent to bāʼ) is false because bāʼ has entered the place of alif. This reasoning is given; the second possibility would imply a leap.

Refuting the First Two Arguments for the Indivisible Particle
Answer to the first two arguments concerning the indivisible particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how defenders of the indivisible particle refute the first two objections by denying the assumed possibility of motion during place motion.

Core Idea
An argument built on an unproven assumption can be halted by denying that assumption.

In this passage, the author presents the response of those who affirm the indivisible particle to the first two arguments against it. They argue that these arguments rely on an unsubstantiated claim about the movement of a located object. By denying this claim and demanding proof, the proponents effectively neutralize the objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the structure: a general statement, then specific response to the first two arguments.
2
Identify the denied assumption
Find the phrase that states what the arguments are based on. Identify what exactly the proponents deny (ممنوع).
The assumption is about the located object's possible motion.
3
Understand the logical move
The proponents do not prove the opposite; they simply reject the assumption and demand evidence. This shifts the burden of proof back to the opponent.
This is a common refutation strategy in kalam: denial of a premise (منع).
4
Reflect on the significance
Why is this a powerful response? Without the assumption, the first two arguments collapse. The opponent must now justify the possibility before proceeding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القائلون بإثبات الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ : فقد أجابوا عنها :
أما الحجة الأولى والثانية : فقد أجابوا عنهما : بأن قالوا : إنها مبنية على أن المتمكن يجوز أن يتحرك ، حال حركة مكانه ، إلى جهة حركة مكانه ، أو إلى خلاف تلك الجهة . وهذا الجواز ممنوع . فلا بد من إقامة الدلالة على الجواز .
As for those who affirm the indivisible particle: they have answered them: As for the first and second argument: they answered them by saying: They are based on the fact that the located [object] may possibly move, during the motion of its place, towards the direction of the motion of its place, or towards the opposite of that direction. But this permissibility is denied. So it is necessary to provide evidence for the permissibility.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents the response of those who affirm the indivisible particle (الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ) to the first two arguments against their position. The author first states that the proponents have a reply. For the first and second arguments, they argue that these arguments rely on the assumption that a located object (المتمكن) can move, while its place is moving, either in the same direction as the place's motion or in the opposite direction. They then deny this possibility, calling it (ممنوع) i.e., not granted without proof. Therefore, they demand that the opponent must provide evidence for this alleged possibility before the argument can proceed.

Response to the Third Argument: Motion and Pauses
Answer to the third argument involving the motion of a bucket rope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the kalam response refutes the third argument by showing that motion requires discrete pauses and lateral motion, supporting the indivisible particle theory.

Core Idea
Motion is not smooth; pauses and swaying prove discrete parts.

This lesson examines the response to the third argument against the indivisible particle. The opponent likely argued that continuous motion disproves atoms, but the respondent turns the tables by pointing out that observable motion, like a bucket rope, necessarily involves pauses and lateral swings. These features actually support the existence of discrete instants.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the conditional structure: if devoid of X and Y, then Z.
2
Identify the two claims about motion
The respondent asserts two necessary features of the bucket rope's motion: pauses (السكنات) and lateral movement (يميناً ويساراً). Identify these in the text.
3
Understand the conditional conclusion
The respondent says that without these two features, the bucket cannot move and the rope breaks. This turns the opponent's argument around by showing that motion itself requires discrete elements.
This supports the theory of indivisible particles (atoms).
4
Consider the broader implication
Think about how this argument defends the atomist position by using an everyday example to demonstrate that motion is not perfectly continuous.
This connects to the learning objective.

وأما الحجة الثالثة : فالجواب عنها : إن حركة المعلاق . لا بد وأن يتخللها السكنات ، وإما أن يذهب ذلك المعلاق يميناً ويساراً . فإن خلا المعلاق عن هذين النوعين ، فلا نسلم إمكان حركة الدلو ، بل ينقطع ذلك الحبل .
As for the third argument: the answer to it is that the motion of the bucket rope must be interspersed with pauses, and that the bucket rope goes right and left. If the bucket rope is devoid of these two types, then we do not grant the possibility of the motion of the bucket; rather, the rope would break.

Commentary
This passage answers the third argument against the indivisible particle. The argument presumably involved the motion of a bucket drawn by a rope (المعلاق). The respondent claims that such motion necessarily involves intermittent pauses (السكنات) and that the rope sways right and left. If these features (pauses and lateral motion) were absent, then the bucket's motion would be impossible—the rope would break. Thus, the opponent's assumption of a perfectly continuous, one-directional motion is unrealistic. The respondent turns the argument around by asserting that the observed motion actually requires discrete units (pauses), which supports the indivisible particle theory.

Response to the Fourth Argument: The Ship and the Man
Response to fourth argument: man on ship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the respondent uses the example of a man on a moving ship to refute the argument for continuous motion.

Core Idea
Continuous motion leads to absurdity: one body in two places at once.

In responding to the fourth argument, the author presents a clever reductio ad absurdum. He considers a man sitting on a ship moving continuously without pauses. By allowing the man to voluntarily move in the opposite direction, he shows that the man would have to be in two different places simultaneously—an impossibility. This demonstrates that motion cannot be continuous without discrete instants.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional proof.
2
Identify the key assumption
The opponent assumes the ship moves continuously without interspersed pauses. What does the respondent deny?
The respondent first denies this premise.
3
Follow the logical steps
The respondent supposes the man moves opposite to the ship. Why does this lead to a contradiction?
Moving to two opposite directions forces the body to be in two places.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the same body cannot be in two different places at once, so the original assumption must be false.
This supports the atomic theory of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة : فلا نسلم أن حركة السفينة ، إذا لم يتخللها السكنات . فإن الرجل الجالس فيها ، يمكنه أن يتحرك [ والدليل عليه ] : أن السفينة إذا تحركت إلى جهة تحركات خالية عن تخلل السكنات . فإذا فرضنا أن الرجل الجالس فيها [ إذا تحرك إلى ] خلاف جهة السفينة ، بحركته الاختيارية . فمن المعلوم : أن من تحرك إلى جهة ، فإنه يحصل في تلك الجهة . فإذا تحرك إلى جهتين متضادتين : إحداهما بالقصد والاختيار ، والأخرى بتبعية حركة السفينة . فحينئذ يلزم أن يحصل الجسم الواحد دفعة واحدة ، في حيزين مختلفين . وذلك محال .
As for the fourth argument: we do not grant that the motion of the ship, if it is not interspersed with pauses. For the man sitting in it can move, and the proof of that is: if the ship moves in a direction with motions free from the intercalation of pauses, then if we suppose that the man sitting in it moves towards the opposite direction of the ship by his voluntary motion, it is known that whoever moves to a direction, he arrives at that direction. So if he moves to two opposite directions—one by intention and choice, and the other by following the motion of the ship—then it would follow that the same body would be in two different spaces at once. That is impossible.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage answers the fourth argument, which likely involved a man on a moving ship. The respondent first denies the premise that the ship's motion is without interspersed pauses. Then they present a proof: assume the ship moves continuously (without pauses) in one direction. If the man voluntarily moves in the opposite direction, he would be moving toward two opposite directions simultaneously—one by his own choice, the other by being carried by the ship. Consequently, the same body would occupy two different places at the same time, which is absurd. Therefore, the original assumption of continuous motion without pauses must be false, supporting the need for discrete units (the indivisible particle and intermittent motion).

Correction: The respondent actually uses voluntary motion as a tool to show a contradiction in the opponent's assumption. They do not deny voluntary motion itself.

The Millstone Argument: Proving Celestial Indivisibility
Razi presents a proof using a millstone analogy to refute the divisibility of celestial spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the millstone argument demonstrates that celestial spheres cannot be divided or fragmented during circular motion.

Core Idea
Circular motion prevents separation: the millstone proves celestial spheres indivisible.

This lesson introduces a key kalam proof against the divisibility of the heavens. Using the analogy of a millstone with two concentric circles, the argument shows that any division during rotation leads to absurdities. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the proof.
The Arabic has some OCR issues; focus on the logical structure.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The proof considers two scenarios: either both circles move proportionally or only the large circle moves.
These are the only logical options.
3
Understand the refutations
See why each possibility leads to an impossibility: equal circumferences or disintegration.
The second impossibility is argued via two reasons.
4
Examine the two reasons against disintegration
The first reason is empirical (smooth stone doesn't become like flour); the second is metaphysical (celestial spheres are strong and cannot break).
The Quranic verse 'seven strong ones' supports the second reason.
5
Summarize the proof
Reiterate that circular motion cannot cause separation in solid bodies, especially celestial spheres.
This conclusion refutes the idea of divisibility.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع
في أنهاء أخير من الدلائل على نفي البر المعركة

الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : إذا دارت الرحى. فإما أن يقال : مهما تحرك الطوق العظيم جزءاً ، فإنه يتحرك الطوق الصغير جزءاً . وذلك محال . وإلا لزم أن يكون مدار الدائرة الصغيرة ، مساوياً لمدار الدائرة العظيمة . وإما أن يقال : إنه قد يتحرك الطوق العظيم جزءاً مع أنه لا يتحرك من الطوق الصغير شيء البتة وذلك باطل . لأن هذا يقتضي تفكك أجزاء الرحى بعضها عن البعض . وذلك باطل لوجوه :
الأول : إن الحس يدل على أن الحجر الصلب ، بل الأملس ، لا يصير عند استدارته ، كالدقيق الذي لا يتصل بعض أجزائه بالبعض
الثاني : إنا نفرض الكلام في الفلك . وحينئذ يمتنع عليه التفرق والتمزق . لأنا بينا : أن الفرق على الفلك محال . وأيضاً : فإنا نتمسك بقوله تعالى : "وبنينا فوقكم سبعاً شداد" (النبأ:12) ، وإذا كانت الأفلاك أبداً متحركة على الاستدارة ، وكانت الحركة على الاستدارة توجب التفرق والتمزق .
Chapter Four: On [?] final of the proofs for negating the proof [?]. The first proof: We say: When the millstone rotates, then either it is said: Whenever the large circle moves a part, the small circle moves a part. That is impossible, otherwise the circumference of the small circle would equal the circumference of the large circle. Or it is said: The large circle may move a part while nothing at all moves from the small circle. That is false, because this necessitates the disintegration of the parts of the millstone from one another. That is false for reasons: First: Sensation indicates that a hard, even smooth stone, when it rotates, does not become like flour whose parts are not connected to each other. Second: We are speaking about the celestial sphere. In that case, fragmentation and tearing are impossible for it, because we have shown that separation is impossible for the celestial sphere. Also we hold fast to His saying, the Most High: 'And We built above you seven strong ones' (Quran 78:12). And since the celestial spheres are perpetually moving in a circular motion, and circular motion necessitates fragmentation and tearing.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (الحجة الأولى) from the author (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) against the idea of divisibility or separation of parts in celestial spheres (الفلك). The proof uses the analogy of a millstone (الرحى) with two concentric circles (طوق عظيم وطوق صغير). The author posits two possibilities: either whenever the large circle moves a part, the small circle also moves a part; or the large circle moves a part while the small circle moves nothing at all. The first is impossible because it would make the circumferences equal; the second is impossible because it would require the parts of the millstone to separate. Then the author gives two reasons why this separation is impossible: (1) sensory observation shows that a hard, smooth stone does not become like disjointed powder when rotated; (2) the discussion is about celestial spheres (فلك), which are impervious to fragmentation—God says 'We built above you seven strong ones' (Quran 78:12), and celestial spheres are perpetually in circular motion, which would cause fragmentation if they were divisible. Thus, the argument concludes that the celestial spheres are indivisible.

Key Terms
الرحى
الطوق العظيم
الطوق الصغير
الفلك
التفكك
التفرق
الحركة الاستدارية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The millstone argument is merely a physical analogy about mechanics.
Correction: It is actually a logical proof for the indivisibility of celestial spheres, using the impossibility of separation during circular motion.

Checkpoint
In the millstone argument, what is the consequence if the large circle moves a part but the small circle does not?
A) The circumferences become equal.
B) The parts of the millstone disintegrate.
C) The small circle moves in the opposite direction.
D) The millstone stops rotating.

Key Takeaway
The millstone argument proves that celestial spheres cannot be divided because circular motion would cause fragmentation, which is impossible for the solid and strong heavens.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Razi uses both reason and revelation to prove the heavens' indivisibility. Where in your own life have you neglected one of these sources of truth? What step will you take today to integrate both intellect and scripture in your understanding of creation?
?
If the heavens are indivisible and perpetually moving without fragmentation, what does that say about Allah's preservation of His creation? How does this truth confront any doubt you have about Allah's ability to hold all things together—including your own life?

Three Positions on Simple Bodies and Their Absurdities
Classification of three views on simple bodies and their absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible doctrines concerning the composition of simple bodies and why the atomist view (finite parts) is defended as the least absurd.

Core Idea
Atomism wins because its rivals lead to even greater impossibilities.

The author presents a complete classification of all possible views on the nature of simple bodies. By systematically showing that each alternative leads to absurdities far worse than the breaking of the millstone—the objection raised against atomism—he argues that accepting atoms is rationally necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of possible doctrines
Identify the three positions on simple bodies: composed of finite parts, composed of infinite parts, or a single indivisible unity.
This sets up the argument by exhausting logical possibilities.
2
Examine the absurdity of finite parts
Notice that the finite-parts view (atomism) forces acceptance of the millstone's disintegration, which is dismissed as far-fetched but not impossible.
This is the starting point the author defends.
3
Examine the absurdity of infinite parts
The infinite-parts view leads to motion over an infinite number of parts in finite time, which is more impossible than the millstone.
This shows the opponent's position is worse.
4
Examine the absurdities of the unity view
The view that a body is a single indivisible thing leads to three specific impossibilities: annihilation of the sea by a mosquito, line segments without distinction, and a mustard seed covering all creation.
Each is claimed to be more impossible than the millstone.
5
Understand the author's argument strategy
Realize that the author is not proving atomism directly, but showing it is the least absurd option among the three possibilities.
This is a classic argument from elimination.

التزام تفكك حجر الرحى . وأما القول بأن الجسم البسيط شيء واحد في نفسه ، فهذا يلزم عليه أيضاً : أنواع من المحالات : فأحدهما : ما بينا أن على هذا القول : يلزم أن يكون تقسيم الجسم إعداماً له ، وأن البعوضة Attributes and accidents necessitate actual difference, so actual difference must occur between all those points. If the possible points in it are actually infinite, then it follows that in that circle there occur parts actually infinite. And we say: that is impossible. Because what is infinite cannot be moved from its beginning to its end in a finite time. Therefore it is necessary that that rotation not be completed in finite time. But when this rotation is completed, we know that is false. So it is established from what we mentioned: that if this proof were correct, it would yield a false conclusion, so it is necessary to judge that this proof is false and fallacious. When we examined it, we did not find in it a premise that can be objected to except their saying: 'The statement that the millstone disintegrates is false.' And what indicates that accepting this statement, despite its being improbable in imagination, is not absolutely impossible are aspects: The first: Certain division has indicated that the possible doctrines concerning simple bodies are only one of these three. That is because we say: This simple body is either in itself composed of parts, or it is not thus but is in itself one thing, as it appears to the senses. If it is composed, then it is either composed of infinite parts or of finite parts. So it is established: the possible doctrines concerning simple bodies are only these three: One of them: the statement of those who say they are composed of finite parts. The second: the statement of those who say they are composed of infinite parts. The third: the statement of those who say they are in themselves one thing, with no composition of parts and no aggregation of segments. If you understand this, then we say: As for the statement that the body is composed of finite parts, it necessitates the statement of the disintegration of the millstone, and the disintegration of the parts of the celestial sphere, which is very far-fetched. As for the statement that the body is composed of infinite parts, it necessitates that the mover move over actually infinite parts in a finite duration. That is more impossible than accepting the disintegration of the millstone. As for the statement that the simple body is one thing in itself, this also necessitates types of impossibilities: The first of them: What we explained that on this statement, the division of the body would be its annihilation, and that if a mosquito fell into the sea and pierced the sea with its needle, then it would be necessary to say that that mosquito annihilated the first sea and created this new sea. And it is known that accepting this is more impossible than accepting the disintegration of the millstone. The second of them: We explained that the segments that can occur in a line, each one of them is characterized by a specific property, which is the acceptance of half, third, quarter, and the rest of the infinite joints. With the specification of each one with a specific property impossible to occur in another, then there is no actual distinction; that is more impossible than accepting the occurrence of disintegration in the millstone. The third of them: Those who say that the body is divisible into infinite divisions accept that from a mustard seed one may take sheets that cover the face of the Throne, the Kursi, the heavens and the earths a million times. And it is known that this is more improbable than accepting the disintegration of the millstone. The fourth of them: You said that each of those infinite divisions is possible in act, and you concede that the existence of one does not prevent the occurrence of another in existence. So you must concede that those divisions which have no end...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument defending the atomist position (the existence of the indivisible atom) by showing that the alternative views lead to greater absurdities. The author begins by recounting a previous argument about infinite points in a circle, which is shown to be fallacious. He then identifies that the premise objected to by opponents is the claim that disintegration of the millstone (i.e., the separation of the celestial sphere or other bodies into atoms) is false. The author argues that this falsity is not proven by demonstration, so it must be accepted to avoid denying the decisive proofs for the indivisible atom. To show that accepting this is not absolutely impossible, he presents a first 'aspect' (wajh): a classification of the three possible doctrines concerning simple bodies (simple bodies are either composed of finite parts, infinite parts, or are a single indivisible unity). He then examines each: 1) The finite-part view leads to the disintegration of the millstone and celestial sphere, which is far-fetched. 2) The infinite-part view leads to motion over infinite parts in finite time, which is even more impossible. 3) The unity view leads to four specific impossibilities: a) division would be annihilation (the mosquito and sea example); b) points on a line have distinct properties but no actual distinction; c) infinite divisibility would allow covering all creation with a mustard seed; d) infinite divisions would be actual, which is contradictory. Each impossibility is claimed to be more severe than accepting the disintegration of the millstone. The argument uses a ranking of absurdities to show that the atomist position is the least absurd, hence must be accepted. The passage is cut off at the fourth impossibility, which is incomplete.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (indivisible atom)
جسم بسيط (simple body)
تفكك (disintegration)
حجر الرحى (millstone)
لا نهاية (infinity)
محال (impossible)
تقسيم (division)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is trying to prove that infinite division is impossible.
Correction: The author is not directly proving or disproving infinite division; he is showing that accepting atomism (finite parts) leads to a less absurd consequence than its alternatives.

Checkpoint

B) The body is composed of infinite parts
C) The body is a single indivisible thing
D) The body is an accident inhering in a substance

Key Takeaway
The atomist view (finite parts) is defended because the other two views on simple bodies lead to absurdities more impossible than accepting the millstone's disintegration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Atomists' Principle of Accepting Absurdities
The author critiques atomists by stating their method and applying it to a specific case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author critiques the method of atomists by showing that they commit to rational proofs even when they lead to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Atomists accept absurdities because they follow rational proofs blindly.

In this passage, the author presents an objection against the proponents of atomism. He points out that their method is to commit to whatever a rational proof indicates, even if it forces them to accept extremely improbable or absurd consequences. This reveals a weakness in their approach, as they cannot reject the proof even when it leads to contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the atomists' rule
The passage begins by stating a general principle: when atomists find a rational proof for something, they commit to it even if it leads to absurdities. Identify this statement in the Arabic text.
2
Observe the application
The author then applies this principle to the case of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). Certain proofs establish the atom, but it leads to consequences like the disintegration of a millstone and even more repugnant impossibilities.
The footnote marker [ على (١) ] is an editorial note; disregard.
3
Understand the necessity
The author concludes that the atomists must accept these absurdities and ignore them. This highlights the conflict between rational proof and sensible outcomes.
Consider why the author calls these consequences 'more serious and more repugnant'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن مذهبكم : أنكم إذا وجدتم برهاناً عقلياً على صحة شيء . فإذا صار ذلك الدليل معارضاً بشيء مستبعد جداً . التزمتم ذلك المستبعد ، ولا تلتفتون إليه. فكذا ههنا لما دلت الدلائل اليقينية على إثبات الجوهر الفرد ، ثم لزم على إثبات الجوهر الفرد : وقوع التفكك في حجر الرحى ولزم [ على (١) ] القول بكون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية : محالات أقطع وأشنع من هذا الإلزام. وجب التزامه وترك الالتفات إليه

So it is established by what we mentioned: that your doctrine is: that when you find a rational proof for the validity of something, and then that proof is opposed by something very far-fetched, you commit to that far-fetched thing and pay no attention to it. So also here, when the certain proofs indicate the existence of the indivisible atom, and then the existence of the atom necessitates the occurrence of disintegration in the millstone, and it necessitates [upon (1)] the statement that the body is composed of infinite parts: impossibilities more serious and more repugnant than this implication. It is necessary to commit to it and pay no attention to it. This is what I have of investigation concerning this proof.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the proponents of atomism (الجوهر الفرد). The author begins by stating a general principle: the atomists' method is that when they find a rational proof for something, even if that proof leads to something extremely improbable (مستبعد جداً), they commit to that improbable thing and ignore it. Then he applies this to the present case: the certain proofs (الدلائل اليقينية) establish the atom, but then it leads to the consequence that the millstone would disintegrate, and even more serious impossibilities if the body is composed of infinite parts. Therefore, they must accept these absurdities and not pay attention to them. He concludes that this is his investigation concerning this proof.

الجوهر الفرد (al-jawhar al-fard) - the indivisible atom
برهان عقلي (burhan 'aqli) - rational proof
دلائل يقينية (adilla yaqiniyya) - certain proofs
تفكك (tafakkuk) - disintegration

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author accepts atomism and is simply stating its consequences.
Correction: The author is critiquing the atomists' method by showing that they must accept absurd consequences, revealing a flaw in their reasoning.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The atomists' commitment to rational proof leads them to accept absurd consequences, which the author uses as a critique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Your faith may sometimes require you to accept things that seem contrary to reason or experience. How do you distinguish between a humble acceptance of divine mystery and a foolish embrace of absurdity? What safeguards do you have in your own thinking?

Refuting the Intermediate State in Atom Motion
Razi refutes the fourth argument using an analogy and a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes the objection that atom motion requires an impossible intermediate state by drawing an analogy with the transition from non-existence to existence and providing a proof about location.

Core Idea
Motion is not a continuous intermediate state but discrete leaps from one location to the next.

The objection that motion requires an intermediate state between positions seems plausible, but al-Razi shows it leads to absurdity. By comparing it to the transition from non-existence to existence, he demonstrates that such intermediate states are illusory. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: first the objector's claim, then al-Razi's response.
The objector claims motion must occur in an intermediate state between two positions.
2
Understand the analogy with existence
Al-Razi uses an analogy: if we accept the objector's logic, we would have to affirm an intermediate state between non-existence and existence, which is absurd. This shows the flaw in the objection.
The analogy reveals that the objector's reasoning leads to an impossible consequence.
3
Follow the conclusive proof
Al-Razi then provides a proof that motion is simply the first obtaining in the second location, with no intermediate state. He examines three possibilities for the atom during motion and shows each is impossible, leaving only the conclusion.
This proof establishes the discrete nature of motion in atomistic kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة : وهي قولهم : ( الجزء إذا انتقل من جزء إلى جزء آخر ، فإنه لا يكون متحركاً حال بقائه على الجزء الأول . لأنه ما دام كذلك . فإنه بعد لم توجد الحركة ، ولا يكون متحركاً حال حصوله على الجزء الثاني . لأنه إذا صار كذلك ، فقد حصلت الحركة وانقطعت وانقرضت . بل إنما يكون متحركاً فيما بين الحالتين ، فنقول : هذا باطل . وذلك لأنا نعلم : أن الصورة والصفة قد يكون معدوماً ، ثم يصير موجوداً . فنقول : إنه ما دام يكون معدوماً ، فهو بعد لم ينتقل إلى الوجود ، وإذا صار موجوداً ، فقد حصل الانتقال وانقرض وانقضى ، فوجب أن يقال : إنما يكون منتقلاً من العدم إلى الوجود في الحالة المتوسطة ، بين كونه معدومـاً وبين كونه موجوداً . فيلزم على هذا : إثبات حالة متوسطة بين كونه معدوماً وبين كونه موجوداً . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . ثم إنه لا جواب عن هذا الخيال ، إلا أن يقال : إنه كان في الآن الأول معدوماً محضاً ، وفي الآن الثاني صار موجوداً محضاً . ولا واسطة بين كونه معدوماً وبين كونه موجوداً . وأن هذا الخيال الحاصل من كونه متنقلا من العدم إلى الوجود : عمل الوهم وتصوير الخيال ، وليس له حقيقة أصلاً البتة. وإذا ثبت أنه لا بد من المصير إلى هذا الجواب ، في هذا المقام . فهو بعينه جوابنا عما ذكروه من الشبهة . فإنا نقول : الجزء كان مماساً للجزء الأول ، ثم صار مماساً للجزء الثاني . وليس بين هاتين المماستين حالة متوسطة مغايرة لهما في الحقيقة. بل هذه الحالة المتوسطة موجودة في الوهم وفي الخيال . والذي يدل على أن الأمر كذلك : أنه لو حصل بين كون ذلك الجزء مماساً للجزء الأول ، وبين كونه مماساً للجزء الثاني : حالة الحركة . فنقول : ذلك الجزء عند حصول تلك الحالة المتوسطة . إما أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز معين، أو يكون حاصلاً في حيز غير معين ، أو لا يكون حاصلاً في شيء من الأحياز أصلاً. فإن كان الأول ، وهو حال كونه متحركاً ، كان حاصلا في حيز معين ، بطل ما يقال : إن الحركة : حالة تحصل قبل حصوله في حيز معين . وأما الثاني فهو باطل . لأن الجزء المعين موجود معين . فحصوله في حيز معين في نفس الأمر محال . وأما الثالث وهو أن يقال : إنه حال كونه متخركاً ، فهو حجم ومقدار . فحصوله لا في شيء من الأحياز محال . فثبت بهذا البرهان القاهر: أنه لا معنى للحركة إلا الحصول الأول في الحيز الثاني ، وأنه إذا توالت هذه الحصـولات في الأحياز المتعاقبة ، فذاك هو الحركة. وعند هذا تبطل الشبهة المذكورة بالكلية .
As for the fourth argument: their statement: 'When the atom moves from one part to another part, it is not moving while remaining on the first part. Because as long as it is thus, motion has not yet occurred. It is not moving while obtaining on the second part. Because when it becomes thus, motion has occurred and ceased and passed away. Rather, it is only moving in between the two states.' We say: this is false. And that is because we know that a form and attribute may be non-existent, then become existent. We say: as long as it is non-existent, it has not yet transitioned to existence; when it becomes existent, the transition has occurred and perished and passed away. So it must be said that it only transitions from non-existence to existence in the intermediate state between being non-existent and being existent. This entails affirming an intermediate state between being non-existent and being existent. And it is known that this is false. Then there is no answer to this imagination except to say: it was in the first moment pure non-existence, and in the second moment it became pure existence. There is no intermediate between being non-existent and being existent. And that this imagination arising from it transitioning from non-existence to existence is the work of illusion and imagery, and has no reality whatsoever. And if it is established that one must resort to this answer in this situation, it is exactly our answer to what they mentioned of the doubt. For we say: the atom was in contact with the first part, then it became in contact with the second part. There is no intermediate state between these two contacts different from them in reality. Rather, this intermediate state exists in illusion and imagination. And what indicates that the matter is so is that if there occurred between the atom's contact with the first part and its contact with the second part a state of motion, we say: that atom at the occurrence of that intermediate state is either obtaining in a specific location, or obtaining in a non-specific location, or not obtaining in any location at all. If the first, and it is the case of it being moving, then it is obtaining in a specific location; this refutes what is said that motion is a state that occurs before its obtaining in a specific location. As for the second, it is false because the specific atom is a specific existent; its obtaining in a non-specific location is impossible in reality. As for the third, which is to say that it, while moving, is a volume and magnitude; its obtaining in no location is impossible. So it is established by this conclusive proof that there is no meaning to motion except the first obtaining in the second location, and that if these obtainings occur consecutively in successive locations, that is motion. At this point the mentioned doubt is entirely refuted.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The objector claims that if an atom moves from one part to another, it cannot be moving while on the first part (since motion hasn't started) nor after reaching the second (since motion has ended), so motion must occur in an intermediate state between the two positions. The author, al-Razi, refutes this by drawing an analogy with the transition from non-existence to existence: if we accept the objector's reasoning, we would have to affirm an intermediate state between non-existence and existence, which is absurd. The only way to avoid this absurdity is to say that the transition occurs instantaneously with no intermediate state. The author then applies the same logic to the atom: the atom goes directly from contact with the first part to contact with the second part, with no real intermediate state; the imagined intermediate is merely a product of illusion. To further prove this, he argues that if there were a real intermediate state of motion, the atom would have to be in a specific location (contradicting motion), in a non-specific location (impossible for a determinate existent), or in no location (impossible for a volume). Therefore, motion is nothing but the first obtaining in the second location, and consecutive such obtainings constitute motion. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms

الحركة
الحالة المتوسطة
الوهم
البرهان القاهر
المماسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion requires a continuous intermediate state between positions.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that such an intermediate state is impossible; motion is actually discrete obtainings in successive locations with no intermediate.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi say is the only meaning of motion?
A) A continuous state between two positions
B) The first obtaining in the second location and successive obtainings
C) The movement of the atom through an intermediate space
D) The illusion of the mind without any reality

Key Takeaway
Motion in atomist kalam is not a continuous intermediate state but consists of discrete obtainings in successive locations, with no real intermediate between them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever assume that change must be gradual and have an 'in-between' state? How does al-Razi's argument challenge that assumption, and what is one area where you might be clinging to an illusion of an intermediate step rather than facing a direct transition?
?
The text says the intermediate state is only in 'illusion and imagination.' What is something you have convinced yourself is real but is actually a product of your own mind? How can you align your perception with the truth as presented in this lesson?
?
Al-Razi's proof destroys the doubt entirely. Is there a doubt in your belief about Allah's attributes or actions that you have been entertaining as a 'possible intermediate'? What would it take for you to accept the conclusive proof and let go of that doubt, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Refuting the Argument from Shadow to Body Divisibility
Razi refutes an argument linking shadow length to body divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi dismantles the objection that a body's shadow being twice its length proves the body is divisible.

Core Idea
An inference from shadow length to body divisibility fails if the body has no half.

The fifth argument claims that if a body's shadow is twice its length, then half the shadow must correspond to half the body, implying divisibility. Razi's response is subtle: he accepts the premise but denies the inference where the body lacks a half. Let's examine this logical move closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's argument: the body has a shadow twice its length, so half that shadow equals the shadow of half the body, hence the body has a half and is divisible.
Note the logical structure: premise → intermediate step → conclusion.
2
Identify Razi's refutation
Razi distinguishes two cases: if the body has a half, the inference holds; if not, we simply say the shadow is twice the body without attributing a half.
The key is that the argument assumes the body has a half—the very point in dispute.
3
Reflect on the circularity
The objector's reasoning is circular: it uses the existence of a half to prove divisibility, but the existence of a half already implies divisibility.
This is a classic refutation that exposes a begged question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الخامسة : وهي قولهم : ( الجسم قد يكون في وقت من السنة ، ظله مثليه . وإذا كان كذلك ، فمثله من الظل ظل نصفه . فالجسم الذي تكون أجزاؤه فرداً ، وجب أن يكون منقسماً ، فجوابه : أن نقول : إذا كان ظله مثليه فإنما نقول : إن مثله من الظل ظلّ نفسه ، إذا كان له نصف . أما إذا لم يكن له نصف . فإنا نقول : ظله يساويه مرتين ، ولا نقول : إن مثله من الظل : ظل نفسه . فسقطت هذه الشبهة .
As for the fifth argument: their statement: 'The body may have, at a time of the year, a shadow twice its length. If so, then the equal of that shadow is the shadow of its half. So the body whose parts are indivisible must be divisible.' The answer is to say: if its shadow is twice its length, we only say that the equal of the shadow is the shadow of itself if it has a half. But if it does not have a half, then we say: its shadow equals it twice, and we do not say that the equal of the shadow is the shadow of itself. So this doubt falls away.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector argues that if a body's shadow is twice its length, then the shadow's equal (i.e., half the shadow) equals the shadow of half the body, implying the body has a half and is therefore divisible. The author responds by distinguishing two cases: if the body has a half, then indeed the equal of the shadow is the shadow of that half. But the body in question may be indivisible (atom), so it does not have a half. In that case, we simply say the shadow is twice the body's length, but we do not claim that half the shadow corresponds to half the body. The argument assumes what it seeks to prove—that the body has a half—so it is circular and invalid.

Key Terms
الظل
الجسم
النصف
الانقسام
الشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a body's shadow is twice its length, then the body must have a half, proving divisibility.
Correction: Razi shows that the inference only holds if the body already has a half. For an indivisible atom (no half), the shadow is simply twice the body, with no implication of a half-body.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, when does the statement 'the equal of the shadow is the shadow of itself' apply?
A) Always when the shadow is twice the body
B) Only if the body has a half
C) Only if the body is divisible into three parts
D) Never, because the shadow is an illusion

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the shadow argument by showing it assumes the existence of a half, which is precisely what it aims to prove.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen how a clever argument can fail because it assumes what it tries to prove. Think of a belief you hold strongly—perhaps about your own worth, a relationship, or a religious practice. Is it possible that your reasoning for that belief contains the same fallacy? Are you willing to examine it today and strip away any unjustified assumptions?
?
The atomist denies that the body has a half. In your life, what 'halves'—essential things you take for granted as existing—might actually be illusions? For example, do you assume you will live to complete all your plans? Will you act now to verify what you actually have proof for, rather than assuming?
?
Razi’s refutation shows that a single condition (having a half) can change the entire conclusion. Is there a condition in your life—a hidden assumption—that, if false, would collapse a major decision you’ve made? What would it cost you to test that condition honestly?

Refuting the Shadow Argument for Atomism
Refutation of the fifth argument for atomism from shadows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the shadow argument for atomism is refuted by distinguishing between a body that has a half and one that does not.

Core Idea
The shadow argument only works if atoms already have halves.

The fifth argument for atomism tries to use everyday observations about shadows to prove that bodies must be divisible. The argument sounds plausible, but a careful logical response shows it assumes what it tries to prove. In this lesson, we will examine the argument and its refutation, focusing on the key distinction that undermines the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its refutation
First, read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument is presented and then answered.
The argument is called a 'shubha' (false argument) by the author.
2
Understand the argument's premise
The argument says: a body can have a shadow twice its length (e.g., at a certain time of year). If that happens, then half of that shadow would equal the body itself. Therefore, the body has a half and is divisible.
3
Identify the refutation's key distinction
The response rejects the inference by asking: does the body have a half? If it does, then the statement 'half the shadow equals the body' is true, but that already assumes divisibility. If it does not (i.e., it is an indivisible atom), then the shadow equals the body twice over, not that half the shadow equals the body. So the argument begs the question.
This is a classic logical move: showing that the argument only works if you already accept the conclusion.
4
Reflect on why this refutation works
Consider: the argument tries to prove atoms are divisible. But the refutation shows that to move from 'shadow is twice the body' to 'body is divisible', you must assume the body has a half. That is exactly what the argument is trying to prove. So it is circular.
This type of refutation is common in kalam — pointing out an unwarranted assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الخامسة: وهي قولهم: (الجسم قد يكون في وقت من السنة، ظله مثليه. وإذا كان كذلك، فمثله من الظل ظل نصفه. فالجسم الذي تكون أجزاؤه فرداً، وجب أن يكون منقسماً). فجوابه: أن نقول: إذا كان ظله مثليه فإنما نقول: إن مثله من الظل ظل نفسه، إذا كان له نصف. أما إذا لم يكن له نصف، فإنا نقول: ظله يساويه مرتين، ولا نقول: إن مثله من الظل ظل نفسه. فسقطت هذه الشبهة.
As for the fifth argument: it is their saying: 'A body may at a time of the year have its shadow twice its length. And if that is the case, then the like of it from the shadow is half of its shadow. So a body whose parts are indivisible must be divisible.' The answer is: we say: if its shadow is twice its length, then we say that the like of it from the shadow is its own shadow, if it has a half. But if it does not have a half, then we say: its shadow equals it twice, and we do not say: the like of it from the shadow is its own shadow. So this false argument falls away.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument against atomism and its refutation. The argument claims that if a body's shadow is twice its length at a certain time, then half the shadow would equal the body's own shadow, implying the body has a half and is thus divisible. The response distinguishes two cases: if the body has a half, the statement holds; but if it does not have a half (i.e., it is an indivisible atom), then the shadow equals the body twice, not that half the shadow equals the body. Hence the argument fails to prove divisibility, as it assumes what is in question.

Key Terms
ظل
مثل
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shadow argument proves that a body with a shadow twice its length must be divisible, regardless of its constitution.
Correction: The argument only holds if the body already has a half (i.e., is divisible). For an indivisible atom, the correct relation is that the shadow equals the body twice, not that half the shadow equals the body.

Checkpoint
What key distinction does the response to the shadow argument make?
A) Between having a shadow and not having a shadow
B) Between having a half and not having a half
C) Between the body and its shadow
D) Between winter and summer

Key Takeaway
The shadow argument for atomism fails because it assumes what it tries to prove: that a body has a half.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a deeply held belief using an argument that only works if you already assume that belief? When someone pointed out the circularity, did you feel defensive or grateful? Will you now commit to examining your own reasoning for hidden assumptions before arguing?
?
This lesson shows that even clever arguments can be question-begging. What is one area of your faith where you might be relying on circular reasoning without realizing it? Are you willing to test that belief by honestly considering counterarguments?
?
The refutation here exposes a subtle logical error that many overlook. How often do you check your own thinking for similar mistakes? Choose one belief you hold strongly and spend time this week analyzing whether your reasons for it truly stand on their own or secretly assume the conclusion.

The Logical Dependence Argument against Atomism
First logical argument against atomism from dependence of knowledge on constituents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first logical argument against atomism uses the principle that knowledge of a composite quiddity depends on knowledge of its constituents to disprove indivisible parts.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a thing requires knowledge of its parts — but we know bodies without knowing atoms.

This passage introduces a key logical argument against atomism (the view that bodies are composed of indivisible parts). It begins with a principle from logic: if a quiddity is composed of constituents, then knowing that quiddity depends on knowing those constituents. The argument then applies this principle to bodies. Since people commonly know that earth and water are bodies without any concept of atoms, it follows that bodies cannot be composed of indivisible atoms. This is a powerful reductio based on common awareness.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the logical principle
Understand the dependency: knowledge of a composite depends on knowledge of its constituents. This is a fundamental logical principle.
The principle is stated in the first part of the passage.
2
Apply to body and atoms
If bodies were made of indivisible parts (atoms), then knowledge of body would require knowledge of those atoms.
This is a hypothetical application.
3
Consider the common observation
Notice that people know bodies like earth and water without having any concept of atoms. This observation contradicts the required dependence.
This is the empirical counterexample.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the consequence is false, the assumption that bodies are composed of atoms must be false.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس
في
حكاية أنواع من الدلائل النغلة [؟] جزء متعلقة بخات [؟] الجسم وبكونه متحيزا
الحجة الأولى: قالوا: قد ثبت في علم المنطق: أن الماهية إذا كانت مركبة من مقومات، فإنه يمتنع العلم بتلك الماهية إلا بعد العلم بتلك المقومات، وذلك لأنه لا معنى لتلك الماهية إلا مجموع تلك المقومات، فوجب أن يكون العلم بتلك الماهية موقوفاً على العلم بتلك المقومات. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: لو كان الجسم مؤلفاً من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ، لوجب أن يكون العلم بكون الجسم جسماً وحجماً ومقداراً موقوفاً على العلم بحصول تلك الأجزاء، لكن معلوم أنه ليس كذلك. فإن جميع الناس يعلمون كون الأرض والماء جسماً مع أنهم لا يخطر ببالهم كونها مؤلفة من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ. فثبت: أن هذه الأجسام يمتنع كونها مؤلفة من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ. فهذا تمام الدليل.
The Fifth Chapter
On
Narration of types of transmitted proofs pertaining to [?] the body and its being a substance.
The first argument: they said: It has been established in the science of logic that if a quiddity is composed of constituents, then knowledge of that quiddity is impossible except after knowledge of those constituents, because that quiddity has no meaning except the sum of those constituents. So knowledge of that quiddity must be dependent on knowledge of those constituents. If this is established, we say: if the body were composed of indivisible parts, then knowledge of the body's being a body, a volume, and a magnitude would have to be dependent on knowledge of the existence of those parts. But it is known that this is not the case. For all people know that earth and water are bodies, while it does not occur to their minds that they are composed of indivisible parts. So it is established that it is impossible for these bodies to be composed of indivisible parts. This is the complete proof.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of the fifth chapter, a logical proof against atomism. It begins with a principle from logic: if a quiddity (māhīya) is composed of constituents (muqawwimāt), then knowledge of that quiddity depends on prior knowledge of those constituents. Applying this to bodies: if bodies were composed of indivisible parts, knowledge of body would require knowledge of those parts. However, ordinary people know bodies (like earth and water) without any concept of atoms. Therefore, bodies cannot be composed of indivisible parts. This is a reductio ad absurdum based on common experience. The argument presupposes that the principle of dependence holds universally.

Key Terms
ماهية
مقومات
أجزاء لا تتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is based on empirical observation of not seeing atoms.
Correction: The argument uses a logical principle about knowledge dependence, not direct observation. The common knowledge of bodies serves as a counterexample to the logical necessity that would follow from atomism.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why can't the body be composed of indivisible parts?
A) Because atoms are too small to be perceived
B) Because people know bodies without knowing atoms
C) Because logic forbids composition
D) Because atoms have no extension

Key Takeaway
The first logical argument against atomism shows that because people know bodies without knowing atoms, the atomic composition is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Like the people in the argument who know bodies without knowing atoms, is there a truth about your faith that you accept without understanding its logical basis? Will you leave it unexamined, or will you seek to know why you believe it?
?
The argument uses a simple logical principle to upend a whole theory. Are there assumptions in your own life that seem obvious but might collapse under logical scrutiny? For instance, do you act as if this life is permanent while knowing it will end? What would it take for you to truly live consistently with that truth?
?
If knowledge of a composite requires knowledge of its parts, how does that apply to your understanding of God? God is not composite, so your knowledge of Him does not come from analyzing parts. Does your worship reflect that direct relation, or do you reduce God to manageable concepts, missing the whole?
