First Argument Against Atomism: Dependence of Knowledge
First logical argument against atomism from dependence of knowledge on parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that bodies cannot be composed of indivisible parts because knowledge of a composite depends on knowledge of its constituents.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a composite depends on knowledge of its parts.

This lesson presents the first logical argument against the atomist theory that bodies are made of indivisible parts. The argument uses a premise from logic: if a thing's essence is composed of parts, then knowing the whole requires knowing those parts. Applying this to bodies, if bodies were atoms, then everyone who knows a body would need to know atoms, but they don't. Therefore, bodies cannot be atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow, then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: premise, application, observation, conclusion.
The argument is concise—every sentence matters.
2
Identify the logical premise
The argument starts with a rule from logic: if an essence is composed of constituents, knowledge of that essence depends on knowledge of those constituents. Make sure you understand why this is true—the essence is just the sum of its parts.
This premise is accepted by both sides of the debate.
3
Apply the premise to bodies
Assume bodies are composed of indivisible parts (atoms). Then, by the premise, knowledge of a body as a body would require knowledge of those atomic parts. But is that true? The argument says no.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Ordinary people know bodies (like earth and water) without ever thinking of atoms. This contradicts what the premise would require. Therefore, the assumption that bodies are atomic is false. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The conclusion is that bodies cannot be composed of indivisible parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : قالوا : قد ثبت في علم المنطق : أن الماهية إذا كانت مركبة من مقومات . فإنه يمتنع العلم بتلك الماهية ، إلا بعد العلم بتلك المقومات . وذلك لأنه لا معنى لتلك الماهية إلا مجموع تلك المقومات . فوجب أن يكون العلم بتلك الماهية ، موقوفاً على العلم بتلك المقومات . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لو كان الجسم مؤلفاً من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ ، لوجب أن يكون العلم بكون الجسم جسماً وحجماً ومقداراً : موقوفاً على العلم بحصول تلك الأجزاء ، لكن معلوم أنه ليس كذلك . فإن جميع الناس يعلمون كون الأرض والماء جسماً . مع أنهم لا يخطر ببالهم كونها مؤلفة من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ . فثبت : أن هذه الأجسام يمتنع كونها مؤلفة من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ فهذا تمام هذا الدليل.
The first proof: They said: It has been established in the science of logic that if an essence is composed of constituents, then knowledge of that essence is impossible except after knowledge of those constituents. That is because the meaning of that essence is nothing but the aggregate of those constituents. Thus knowledge of that essence must depend upon knowledge of those constituents. Once this is established, we say: If the body were composed of parts that are indivisible, then knowledge of the body's being a body, volume, and magnitude would have to depend upon knowledge of the existence of those parts. But it is known that this is not the case. For all people know that earth and water are bodies, even though it never occurs to their minds that they are composed of indivisible parts. Thus it is established that these bodies cannot be composed of indivisible parts. This is the complete argument.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument (حجة) against the atomist position that bodies are composed of indivisible parts (al-ajza' allati la tatajazza). The argument begins with a premise from logic: if an essence (mahiyya) is composite (murakkaba) from constituents (muqawwimat), then knowledge of that essence depends on prior knowledge of those constituents. This is because the essence is nothing but the sum of its parts. Applying this to bodies, if a body were composed of atoms, then our knowledge of the body as a body, a volume, and a magnitude would require knowledge of those atoms. However, we find that ordinary people know earth and water as bodies without any thought of atoms. Thus the premise fails—body cannot be composed of atoms. The argument is structured as a modus tollens: if atoms, then dependence; but no dependence, so no atoms. The conclusion is that bodies are not composed of indivisible parts.

Key Terms
الماهية
المقومات
الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ
الجسم
الحجم
المقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the argument assumes what it tries to prove (begging the question) by already assuming bodies are not atomic.
Correction: The argument does not assume that; it uses a logical premise about knowledge of composites, which the opponent accepts, to show that the atomist view leads to an absurd consequence—namely that common knowledge of bodies would require knowledge of atoms, which is false.

Checkpoint
What is the logical premise used in the first argument against atomism?
A) If an essence is simple, knowledge of it is impossible.
B) If an essence is composed of constituents, knowledge of it depends on knowledge of those constituents.
C) Knowledge of a body requires knowledge of its color and shape.
D) Atoms have no dimensions.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against atomism shows that if bodies were composed of atoms, knowledge of bodies would require knowledge of atoms, but ordinary people know bodies without such knowledge, so bodies cannot be atomic.

?
The argument shows that people know bodies without knowing their parts. In your own life, are there things you think you understand but actually you have not examined the foundations? What are they?
?
This argument uses a logical premise that knowledge of a whole depends on knowledge of its parts. How does this principle apply to your understanding of your own self? Do you truly know yourself by knowing its parts?

Second Argument on the Atom and Its Refutation
Dilemma on atom dimensions and dialectical response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma posed against atomism regarding the atom's dimensions and how the response neutralizes it by showing it applies equally to the opponent's theory.

Core Idea
The atom's dimensions dilemma backfires on the opponent's view.

The second argument against atomism presents a clever dilemma: if atoms have size they are divisible; if not, they cannot combine to produce extension. The response, however, shows that the same logic applies to the opponent's theory of body composed of matter and form, thus the argument fails to disprove atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the argument (a dilemma), then the response.
The argument has two main horns, each leading to a contradiction.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The first horn: if the atom has dimensions, it is divisible. The second horn: if it has no dimensions, then combining atoms cannot produce extension.
The second horn is further split into two sub-cases.
3
Analyze the sub-cases of the second horn
If upon joining each atom gains dimensions, then each becomes divisible (same problem). If no dimensions arise, the aggregate has no size, which is false.
Both sub-cases lead to impossibility.
4
Understand the response's dialectical strategy
The response does not directly defend atomism but shows that the same dilemma applies to the opponent's theory (body = matter + form). Thus the argument is neutralized.
This is a common technique in kalam: turning the argument back.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : قالوا : الجوهر الفرد إما أن يكون له قدر من الطول والعرض ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، فإن كان الأول كان منقسماً لا محالة . وإن كان الثاني فعند ضم بعضها إلى البعض ، وجب أن لا يحصل الطول والمقدار . لأن عند انضمام بعضها إلى البعض إن حدث الطول والعرض فيها ، صار عند الانضمام ، كل واحد منها طويلاً عريضاً ، فيعود الإلزام . وإن لم يحدث الطول والعرض عند الانضمام ، فحينئذ لم يحدث المقدار ، ولم يحدث الطول والعرض البتة . فوجب أن لا يحصل للمجموع حجم ومقدار . وذلك باطل . والجواب عنه : إن هذا أيضاً وارد عليكم . لأن عندكم : الجسم مركب من الهيولي والصورة . ثم إن الناس يتصورون كون الأرض جسماً ، وكون الماء جسماً مع الشك في كونها مركبة من الهيولي والصورة . فثبت : أن الذي أوردوه علينا ، وارد عليهم أيضاً.
The second proof: They said: The indivisible atom either has a measure of length and width or it does not. If the first, then it is necessarily divisible. If the second, then when some of them are joined together, length and magnitude must not arise. Because when some are joined together, if length and width occur in them, then upon joining each one becomes long and wide, so the implication returns. And if length and width do not occur upon joining, then magnitude does not arise, and length and width do not arise at all. Thus the aggregate must have no volume and magnitude. That is false. The response to it: This also applies to you (the opponents). Because according to you, the body is composed of matter (hayula) and form (sura). Yet people conceive of earth as a body and water as a body while doubting whether they are composed of matter and form. Thus it is established that what they brought against us also applies to them.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage contains the second argument against atomism and the response that turns the argument against the opponents. The argument presents a dilemma concerning the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). If the atom has dimensions (length and width), then it is divisible, contradicting its definition. If it has no dimensions, then combining atoms cannot produce extension—because if upon combination each atom gains dimensions, then each becomes divisible (the original problem returns); if no dimensions are gained, then the aggregate has no size, which is false (since bodies clearly have size). Thus composition from atoms is impossible. The response (al-jawab) cleverly turns this against the opponents who hold that body is composed of matter (hayula) and form (sura). The same reasoning applies: people know bodies without knowing this composition, so the opponents' theory also fails by the same logic.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
الطول
العرض
المقدار
الهيولي
الصورة
الانضمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The response concedes that atomism is false.
Correction: The response does not concede; it shows that the opponent's view suffers from the same defect, so the argument is neutralized.

Checkpoint
What is the second argument's dilemma about the indivisible atom?
A) Whether it is material or spiritual.
B) Whether it has dimensions or not; both lead to absurdity.
C) Whether it is created or eternal.
D) Whether it is perceived by sense or intellect.

Key Takeaway
The second argument's dilemma is neutralized by showing it applies equally to the opponent's theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a 'you too' argument to deflect a criticism without truly addressing it? How does this lesson challenge you to respond to objections honestly?
?
When someone points out a flaw in your reasoning, do you immediately look for a way to turn it back on them, or do you first examine whether your own position is sound? Which approach does this lesson illustrate?
?
This passage shows a logical dilemma that seems powerful but is neutralized. Are you willing to accept that even strong logical arguments can be neutralized by showing they apply to the critic as well? What does that mean for your trust in logic?

The Opponent's Argument for Intuitive Composition
Presents opponent's argument for intuitive hyle-form composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's claim that knowledge of body's composition from hyle and form is intuitive, and their proof based on the definition of body.

Core Idea
The opponent argues that the very definition of body implies matter and form.

The passage begins by presenting a common argument from those who hold the hyle-form composition theory. They claim that knowledge of this composition is intuitive and necessary for all rational people. Understanding this argument is essential before seeing its refutation in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Read the passage from the beginning up to 'الدليل عليه' to get the main claim that knowledge of composition is intuitive.
Notice the strong language: 'بديهي ضروري' (intuitive, necessary).
2
Identify the proof
Read the proof that begins with 'أن كل من أراد أن يخبر...'. The proof is based on a definition of body.
The definition used is: 'الجوهر الذي يمكن فرض الأبعاد الثلاثة فيه'.
3
Analyze the two intelligibles
The author says the statement indicates two intelligibles: the substance receptive of dimensions and the dimensions themselves. Identify these in the text.
The words 'قابل' and 'مقبول' are key.
4
Map to matter and form
The passage explicitly equates the recipient with matter (الهيولى) and the accepted dimensions with form (الصورة). Understand how this mapping works.
Matter is the receptive principle, form is the dimensional determination.
5
Reflect on the intuition claim
The opponent concludes that pure reason rules composition. Think about why they consider this knowledge intuitive: because the definition seems self-evident to all rational people.
This sets up the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : العلم بكون الجسم مركباً من الهيولى والصورة : علم بديهي ضروري ، حاصل لجميع العقلاء. والدليل عليه : أن كل من أراد أن يخبر عن ماهية الجسم ، وأن يذكره بصفته الحقيقية . قال : إنه الجوهر الذي يمكن فرض الأبعاد الثلاثة فيه . ولا شك أن هذا الكلام إشارة إلى معقولين : أحدهما : الجوهر القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة . والثاني : نفس تلك الأبعاد الثلاثة . فثبت : صريح العقل قضى ههنا بوجود قابل ، وبوجود مقبول : فالقابل هو الجوهر ، والمقبول هو الأبعاد الثلاثة . ولا معنى للهيولى إلا القابل ، ولا معنى للصورة إلا الأبعاد الثلاثة المقبولة . فثبت : أن صريح العقل حاكم بأن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة . قلنا : هذا مدفوع . لأن تعريف الجسم بأنه الجوهر القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة : كلام ذكره بعض الفلاسفة . وأما الباقون فإنهم قالوا : ماهية الجسم ماهية متصورة بنفسها ، غنية عن التعريف ، وليس لها حقيقة إلا هذا الحجم، وإلا هذا الامتداد. فثبت : أن على هذا القول فالكلام الذي عولوا عليه ساقط.
For if they say: Knowledge that the body is composed of matter and form is intuitive, necessary knowledge, possessed by all rational people. The proof for it is that everyone who wishes to inform about the essence of the body and to describe it by its true attribute says: It is the substance in which three dimensions can be assumed. There is no doubt that this statement indicates two intelligibles: one is the substance receptive of the three dimensions, and the second is the three dimensions themselves. Thus it is established that pure reason has judged here the existence of a recipient and an accepted thing: the recipient is the substance, and the accepted thing is the three dimensions. Matter means nothing but the recipient, and form means nothing but the accepted three dimensions. Thus it is established that pure reason rules that the body is composed of matter and form. We say: This is refuted. Because the definition of body as the substance receptive of the three dimensions is a statement mentioned by some philosophers. As for the rest, they said: The essence of body is an essence conceived by itself, independent of definition, and it has no reality except this volume and except this extension. Thus it is established that on this opinion, the statement they relied on is null.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the opponent (those who hold the hyle-form composition theory) and the response. The opponent claims that knowledge of body's composition from matter (hayula) and form (sura) is intuitive (badihi) and necessary (daruri), and they provide a proof: the definition of body as 'substance in which three dimensions can be assumed' implies two things: a substance that receives dimensions, and the dimensions themselves. The recipient is matter, the received is form, so reason necessitates composition. The response (qulna) refutes this by pointing out that this definition is not universally accepted. Only some philosophers define body this way. Others, including many theologians, hold that the essence (mahiyya) of body is self-evident and needs no definition—it is simply volume and extension. If the body is self-evident, then the argument based on that definition fails. Thus the opponent's claim of intuitive knowledge is not established.

Key Terms
بديهي
ضروري
الجوهر
الأبعاد الثلاثة
القابل
المقبول
الهيولى
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the opponent, the definition of body as 'the substance in which three dimensions can be assumed' indicates how many intelligibles?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
The opponent claims that the very definition of body reveals its composition from matter and form, suggesting this knowledge is intuitive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Pure reason is invoked to prove that body is composed of matter and form. If your reasoning leads you to a conclusion that contradicts what Allah has revealed, would you still trust your reasoning? What is the ultimate arbiter in your heart?

The Refutation: Definition Not Universally Accepted
Provides refutation by highlighting that the definition is not universally accepted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the response refutes the opponent's claim by showing that the definition of body is not universally accepted.

Core Idea
The response undermines the opponent's argument by revealing its definitional foundation is contested.

Having seen the opponent's argument, we now turn to the refutation (qulna). The response points out that the definition of body as 'substance receptive of dimensions' is only held by some philosophers. Others define body simply as volume or extension, which renders the opponent's proof irrelevant. This shows that the knowledge is not intuitive.

Guided Path
1
Read the response
Read the passage from 'قلنا: هذا مدفوع' to the end. This is the direct refutation.
Note the opening words 'هذا مدفوع' (This is refuted).
2
Understand the refutation strategy
The response does not deny the logical connection, but challenges the premise: the definition itself is not universally accepted.
The key word is 'بعض الفلاسفة' (some philosophers).
3
Learn the alternative definition
The response presents another view: body is self-evident and defined simply as volume or extension.
Look for 'ماهية الجسم ماهية متصورة بنفسها'.
4
Conclude the argument
If the alternative definition is accepted, the opponent's proof collapses because it relied on a specific definition.
The final statement: 'فالكلام الذي عولوا عليه ساقط'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Objection on Body's Magnitude and Its Refutation
Objection about magnitude of parts and its tu quoque refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a kalam objection about the magnitude of a body composed of parts is countered by a tu quoque argument that turns the objection against the opponent's own metaphysical commitments.

Core Idea
A tu quoque argument defuses an objection about body's magnitude.

This passage presents a classic kalam-style objection about the magnitude of a body composed of parts: if the parts individually lack dimensions, how can the whole have magnitude? The objection is sharp, but the author disarms it by turning it against the opponent's own metaphysical commitments. Instead of defending the atomic theory directly, he shows the objection is a double-edged sword.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent argues that if parts individually lack length and width, joining them cannot produce these dimensions without contradiction. Read the first part of the passage carefully to grasp the two possible scenarios.
The objection is conditional: either dimensions appear upon joining (causing regress) or they don't (making magnitude impossible).
2
Follow the two options
The objection examines both possibilities: if parts acquire length and width when joined, then each part becomes long and wide, which regenerates the problem for the parts. If not, then the whole has no magnitude at all, which is false.
3
Grasp the refutation
The author answers by pointing out that the same reasoning applies to the opponent's own view that body is composed of matter (hayulā) and form (ṣūra). People conceive of everyday objects like earth and water as bodies without knowing about that composition, so the objection also undermines their theory.
This is a tu quoque (you too) argument.
4
Recognize the strategy
Notice how the kalam theologian does not need to disprove the objection outright; instead, he shows that if it were valid, it would equally damage the opponent's position. This is a common dialectical move in Islamic theology.
This strategy relies on the opponent's own commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقدار. لأن عند انضمام بعضها إلى البعض إن حدث الطول والعرض فيها، صار عند الانضمام كل واحد منها طويلاً عريضاً، فيعود الإلزام. وإن لم يحدث الطول والعرض عند الانضمام، فحينئذ لم يحدث المقدار، ولم يحدث الطول والعرض البتة. فوجب أن لا يحصل للمجموع حجم ومقدار. وذلك باطل. والجواب عنه: إن هذا أيضاً وارد عليكم. لأن عندكم: الجسم مركب من الهيولي والصورة. ثم إن الناس يتصورون كون الأرض جسماً، وكون الماء جسماً مع الشك في كونها مركبة من الهيولي والصورة. فثبت: أن الذي أوردوه علينا، وارد عليهم أيضاً.
And the magnitude. Because when some of them are joined to others, if length and width occur in them, then upon joining, each one of them becomes long and wide, so the objection returns. And if length and width do not occur upon joining, then magnitude did not occur, nor did length and width at all. So it must be that the totality does not obtain volume and magnitude. And that is false. The answer to it: This also applies to you. Because according to you, the body is composed of matter (hayulā) and form (ṣūra). Then people conceive of the earth as a body, and water as a body, despite doubting whether they are composed of matter and form. So it is established that what they brought against us also applies to them.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objection argues about the magnitude of a body composed of parts: if the parts individually lack length and width, when joined they either become long and wide (and the problem recurs) or not (so no magnitude at all), leading to a contradiction. The answer turns the argument against the opponents (likely philosophers who believe in matter-form composition): they hold that body is composed of matter (hayulā) and form (ṣūra), yet ordinary people conceive of everyday objects like earth and water as bodies without knowing about that composition. Therefore, the same objection could be raised against their theory. The argument is thus a tu quoque.

Key Terms
مقدار
انضمام
طول
عرض
هيولي
صورة
جسم
حجم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the objection is a specific flaw of the atomic theory of bodies.
Correction: The objection applies equally to the alternative theory of matter and form (hayulā and ṣūra), as shown by the fact that ordinary people conceive of bodies without knowledge of their composition.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection about magnitude of parts?
A) By denying that parts exist in a body.
B) By showing the objection also applies to the opponent's theory of matter and form.
C) By claiming that length and width are essential to each part individually.
D) By arguing that magnitude is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
An objection based on the impossibility of magnitude emerging from parts can be deflected if the opponent's own theory faces the same problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reject a criticism of your belief, do you ever check whether the same criticism applies to your own position? What would it take for you to apply the same standard to yourself that you demand of others?
?
The objection was deflected but not answered. In what areas of your life do you deflect uncomfortable truths about yourself rather than facing them? Will you sit with the question today, or turn away again?
?
If you were the opponent in this debate, would you accept the tu quoque or press further? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to being right rather than seeking truth?

The Self-Evidence Claim Refuted
Objection and initial refutation about body's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that body's composition from matter and form is self-evident is not universally accepted, as the definition of body is disputed among philosophers.

Core Idea
The definition of body is not self-evident; only some philosophers accept it.

In this passage, the author presents an objection from those who argue that the composition of body from matter and form is self-evident, based on a common definition. However, he quickly refutes this by pointing out that this definition is not accepted by all; many philosophers consider body's quiddity to be simple extension. Let's examine the objection and its initial rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to 'فثبت: أن على هذا القول فالكلام الذي عولوا عليه ساقط.' Follow along with the translation to understand the objection's reasoning.
The objection claims self-evidence based on the definition of body.
2
Identify the key definition
Notice the definition: 'substance receptive of three dimensions.' The objection argues this implies a receptor (matter) and received (form).
3
Understand the refutation
The author refutes by noting that this definition is only held by some philosophers. Others see body as simple extension, needing no definition.
This shows the objection's premise is not universally accepted.
4
Ask: Why does this matter?
Reflect on how the author uses disagreement among philosophers to undermine the claim of self-evidence. This is a common dialectical strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: العلم بكون الجسم مركباً من الهيولى والصورة: علم بديهي ضروري، حاصل لجميع العقلاء. والدليل عليه: أن كل من أراد أن يخبر عن ماهية الجسم، وأن يذكره بصفته الحقيقية. قال: إنه الجوهر الذي يمكن فرض الأبعاد الثلاثة فيه. ولا شك أن هذا الكلام إشارة إلى معقولين: أحدهما: الجوهر القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة والثاني: نفس تلك الأبعاد الثلاثة. فثبت: صريح العقل قضى ههنا بوجود قابل، ووجود مقبول: فالقابل هو الجوهر، والمقبول هو الأبعاد الثلاثة. ولا معنى للهيولى إلا القابل، ولا معنى للصورة إلا الأبعاد الثلاثة المقبولة. فثبت: أن صريح العقل حاكم بأن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة. قلنا: هذا مدفوع. لأن تعريف الجسم بأنه الجوهر القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة: كلام ذكره بعض الفلاسفة. وأما الباقون فإنهم قالوا: ماهية الجسم ماهية متصورة بنفسها، غنية عن التعريف، وليس لها حقيقة إلا هذا الحجم، وإلا هذا الامتداد. فثبت: أن على هذا القول فالكلام الذي عولوا عليه ساقط. ثم نقول: الجواب عنه: إن كل واحدة منها مقوم لماهية الجسم، من حيث إنه شيء له حجم ومقدار. وأما إذا اعتبرناه من حيث إنه شيء مغاير للجزء الآخر، فهو بهذا الاعتبار ليس مقوماً لماهية الجسم، ولا يمتنع في العقل أن يكون شيء له اعتباران. وهو بأحد الاعتبارين مقوم لماهية الشيء، وبالاعتبار الثاني لا يكون مقوماً لتلك الماهية. وإذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: إذا اعتقدنا في جسم: كونه جسماً. فنحن نعرف أنه جسم من أوله إلى آخره. فأما الأجزاء الحاصلة فيه: مئة، أو مثنا ألف. فهذا الاعتبار غير داخل في تقويم ماهية الجسم. فلا يجب من العلم بماهية الجسم: العلم بكمية هذا العدد. والجواب عن الحجة الثانية: إن كل واحد من الأجزاء وإن لم يكن في نفسه طويلاً إلا أنه إذا انضم إليه غيره، صار طويلا. كما أن كل واحد من حبات الحنطة وإن لم يكن في نفسه منا واحداً إلا أن مجموعها يصير منا. فكذلك ههنا. والله أعلم.
Then they say: Knowledge that the body is composed of matter (hayulā) and form (ṣūra) is self-evident necessary knowledge, possessed by all rational persons. The evidence for it is that everyone who wishes to inform about the quiddity (māhiyya) of the body and mention it by its true description says: It is the substance (jawhar) in which the three dimensions can be posited. There is no doubt that this speech refers to two intelligibles: one of them is the substance receptive of the three dimensions, and the second is those three dimensions themselves. Thus it is established that clear reason judges here the existence of a receptor and the existence of a received: the receptor is the substance, and the received is the three dimensions. And there is no meaning of matter (hayulā) except a receptor, and no meaning of form (ṣūra) except the three dimensions received. So it is established that clear reason rules that the body is composed of matter and form. We say: This is refuted. Because the definition of body as the substance receptive of the three dimensions is a statement mentioned by some philosophers. As for the rest, they said: The quiddity of the body is a quiddity conceived by itself, in no need of definition, and it has no reality except this volume, except this extension. So it is established that on this view, the statement they relied on collapses. Then we say: The answer to it is that each one of them (the parts) is constitutive of the quiddity of the body, inasmuch as it is something having volume and magnitude. But if we consider it inasmuch as it is something other than the other part, it is in this consideration not constitutive of the quiddity of the body. There is no impossibility in reason that a thing has two aspects. It is in one aspect constitutive of the quiddity of the thing, and in the second aspect not constitutive of that quiddity. And when this is established, we say: If we believe concerning a body that it is a body, then we know that it is a body from its beginning to its end. As for the parts occurring in it: a hundred, or two hundred thousand, this consideration is not included in constituting the quiddity of the body. So knowledge of the quiddity of the body does not necessitate knowledge of the quantity of this number. And the answer to the second argument: Each one of the parts, even if it is not in itself long, yet when another is joined to it, it becomes long. Just as each grain of wheat, even if it is not in itself a mann, yet their sum becomes a mann. So likewise here. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage contains a second objection and its detailed answer. The objection asserts that body's composition from matter and form is self-evident, citing the common definition of body as substance receptive of three dimensions, which implies a receptor (matter) and received (form). The answer first refutes this by noting that this definition is not universally accepted; many philosophers hold that body's quiddity is simple extension, not requiring definition. Then the author elaborates on the nature of parts: a part can be considered constitutive of the body's quiddity when viewed as having volume, but not when viewed as distinct from another part—this distinction is valid. Thus, knowledge of the body's essence does not require knowing the number of parts. Finally, a second argument about parts not being individually long but becoming long when joined is answered with the analogy of grains of wheat: each grain is not a mann, but together they make a mann. The discussion ends with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
هيولى
صورة
جوهر
أبعاد ثلاثية
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of body as 'substance receptive of three dimensions' is universally accepted and self-evident.
Correction: The author shows that this definition is only held by some philosophers; others consider body's quiddity to be simple extension, needing no definition.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that body's composition from matter and form is self-evident?
A) Because the definition of body is contradictory.
B) Because the definition is only accepted by some philosophers, not all.
C) Because the three dimensions cannot be conceived separately.
D) Because reason cannot judge about composition.

Key Takeaway
The claim that body's composition from matter and form is self-evident fails because its premise—the definition of body—is not universally accepted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief simply because it seemed 'self-evident' to everyone around you, without examining whether it is truly universally agreed upon? What would it cost you to question such beliefs today?
?
This lesson shows that even philosophers disagree about the simplest definitions. Which of your own core beliefs about existence or the material world might be based on assumptions you've never examined? Will you examine them now?

Answering the Arguments for Composition
Detailed answer to arguments for matter-form composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's detailed response to arguments for matter-form composition, including the distinction between constitutive and non-constitutive aspects and the grain analogy.

Core Idea
A part can be constitutive of body's quiddity in one aspect but not another.

Having refuted the self-evidence claim, the author now provides a deeper answer. He explains that parts can be considered constitutive of body's quiddity when viewed as having volume, but not when viewed as distinct from each other. He also answers a second argument about parts not being individually long, using the analogy of grains of wheat that together make a larger unit. Let's explore these nuanced points.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed answer
Read the Arabic passage from 'ثم نقول: الجواب عنه' to the end. Follow the translation to understand the logic.
This part is more technical.
2
Grasp the two aspects
Note the distinction: a part is constitutive of the body's quiddity when considered as having volume, but not when considered as distinct from another part.
This shows how a thing can have two aspects.
3
Understand the consequence
Knowing the body's quiddity does not require knowing the number of parts. This counters the opponent's implication.
4
Examine the second argument and analogy
The opponent argues parts are not individually long. The author answers with the grain analogy: individual grains are not a mann, but together they become one.
This shows that the sum of non-long parts can be long.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how the author systematically dismantles both the self-evidence claim and the supporting arguments, leaving no ground for the composition theory.

Answering the Objection: Two Considerations of Parts
The author answers an objection by distinguishing two considerations of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an object can have multiple considerations, and why knowledge of a body's essence does not require knowledge of its specific parts.

Core Idea
A thing can be considered in different ways; what constitutes its essence is only one consideration.

This passage addresses a key objection against the claim that parts constitute the essence of a body. The author clarifies that a part can be a constituent in one respect but not in another, resolving the objection without contradiction. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how we can know a body without knowing its exact composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then follow with the literal translation. Notice the structure: the author first states the objection, then gives the answer by distinguishing two considerations.
Pay attention to the terms 'muqawwim' and 'iʿtibār'.
2
Identify the two considerations
The author distinguishes: (1) a part as having volume and magnitude (constituent of essence), (2) a part as distinct from another part (not constituent). Write these down in your own words.
Consider why something can have two valid considerations without contradiction.
3
Apply the distinction to the body example
The author gives an example: knowing a body as a body does not require knowing the exact number of parts (100 or 2000). Explain why this follows from the distinction.
This shows that essence does not include specific quantities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : الجواب عنه : إن كل واحدة منها مقوم لماهية الجسم ، من حيث إنه شيء له حجم ومقدار وأما إذا اعتبرناه من حيث إنه شيء مغاير للجزء الآخر ، فهو بهذا الاعتبار ليس مقوماً لماهية الجسم ، ولا يمتنع في العقل أن يكون شيء له اعتباران . وهو بأحد الاعتبارين مقوم لماهية الشيء ، وبالاعتبار الثاني لا يكون مقوماً لتلك الماهية. وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إذا اعتقدنا في جسم : كونه جسماً . فنحن نعرف أنه جسم من أوله إلى آخره . فأما الأجزاء الحاصلة فيه : مئة ، أو مثنا ألف. فهذا الاعتبار غير داخل في تقويم ماهية الجسم . فلا يجب من العلم بماهية الجسم : العلم بكمية هذا العدد
Then we say: The answer to it is: Indeed, each one of them is a constituent (muqawwim) of the essence (mahiyya) of the body (jism), from the perspective that it is a thing having volume and magnitude. But if we consider it from the perspective that it is a thing distinct from the other part, then in this consideration it is not a constituent of the essence of the body, and it is not impossible in the intellect for something to have two considerations. In one of the two considerations it is a constituent of the essence of the thing, and in the second consideration it is not a constituent of that essence. And if this is established, then we say: If we believe concerning a body that it is a body, then we know that it is a body from its beginning to its end. As for the parts that are present in it, whether one hundred or two thousand, this consideration is not included in constituting the essence of the body. So it is not necessary, from the knowledge of the essence of the body, to know the quantity of this number.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The author states the objection that each part is a constituent of the essence of a body. He then clarifies by distinguishing two considerations: from the perspective that a part has volume and magnitude, it is a constituent; but from the perspective that it is distinct from another part, it is not a constituent. He explains that a thing can have two considerations without contradiction. Applying this, when we know a body as a body, we know its essence without needing to know the exact number of parts. Therefore, knowledge of the essence does not entail knowledge of the quantity of parts.

Key Terms
مقوم (muqawwim)
ماهية (mahiyya)
جسم (jism)
اعتبار (iʿtibār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowledge of a body requires knowing its specific atomic composition.
Correction: The author shows that knowing a body as a body (its essence) does not require knowing the precise number of parts, because the parts are only constituents in one consideration (as having volume), not in the consideration of being distinct. The essence is known without that detail.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why is it not necessary to know the number of parts when knowing the essence of a body?
A) Because the essence of a body is defined by its parts.
B) Because the consideration of being a part that constitutes the essence is different from considering it as separate from another part.
C) Because the parts are infinite.
D) Because the body is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The essence of a body is known without knowing the number of its parts, because parts are only constituents in a specific consideration (as having volume), not in the consideration of being distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you define yourself by your constituent parts—your body, your achievements, your relationships—or by the essence that Allah sees in you? Which consideration do you prioritize in your daily actions?
?
You know you are a believer, but do you feel you must know every detail of your faith before you can act? How does this lesson challenge the paralysis of needing complete knowledge before taking a step of obedience?
?
Think of a person you find difficult to deal with. Do you reduce them to one consideration—their mistake or difference—ignoring other perspectives? How can you apply the principle of multiple considerations to see them as Allah sees them?

Answer to the Objection: Parts Become Long When Joined
Answer to objection using grain analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses an analogy of grains of wheat to refute the claim that individual parts cannot be long, demonstrating that combination yields length.

Core Idea
Parts gain length through combination, just as grains of wheat collectively weigh a mann.

The author addresses a second argument against his position. The objection claimed that if parts are not long individually, they cannot produce a long whole. Here, he presents a crisp response using a simple analogy from everyday measurement. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the answer.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the objection being answered
The passage starts with 'And the answer to the second argument'. What was the second argument? (Not given here, but infer that it claimed parts individually are not long.)
The objection likely said that if each part is not long, the whole cannot be long.
3
Understand the analogy
The author compares parts to grains of wheat. Each grain is not one mann (a unit of weight), but together they become one mann. Similarly, each part may not be long, but together they become long.
This is an analogy of aggregation: the property emerges from the sum.
4
Reflect on the logical principle
The key insight is that a property can be absent in each individual part yet present in the collective whole. This refutes the objection that parts must individually possess the property.
Consider other examples: individual water molecules are not wet, but a body of water is.
5
Summarize the takeaway

This will help solidify your understanding.

Summary of geometrical arguments proving atomism false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how geometrical properties of circles and spheres are used to refute the doctrine of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard).

Core Idea
The infinite divisibility of angles and circles disproves atomism.

This passage from al-Matālib al-‘Āliyya presents a concise summary of geometrical arguments that were used to disprove the atomist theory. By examining properties of circles and spheres, the author shows that the atomist claim is rationally untenable. In this lesson, we will unpack the geometric reasoning and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.

The first angle and the second circle are referenced.

The passage states that if the degrees of widening of the first angle are infinite, then likewise the degrees of narrowing of the second circle are infinite. Reflect on why this presents a challenge to atomism.

Enumeration of four geometric primitives and their proofs by motion.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Geometry rests on four primitives: circle, sphere, cone, cylinder, proven by motion.

Geometry is not just about definitions; it requires proof. In this passage, the author identifies the four foundational shapes from which all geometric investigations branch and shows how engineers (geometers) establish their validity through specific motions. Pay attention to how each shape is generated.

Guided Path

3

Why the Indivisible Atom Has No Shape
Refutes the claim that the indivisible atom must be spherical or polygonal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the indivisible atom cannot be described as having a shape, according to kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
An indivisible atom has no shape because shape requires parts.

In kalam, the concept of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is a fundamental building block of matter. Opponents often argue that such an atom must have some shape, such as being spherical or polygonal. Our author refutes this claim by showing that shape is only conceivable for composite bodies, not for simple, partless entities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Opponent's Claim
The opponent asserts that the indivisible atom must have a shape, either spherical or polygonal. Recognize that this is an attempted limitation on the atom's nature.
This claim is based on the assumption that every physical thing has a shape.
2
Read the Author's Refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author begins by stating the truth: the atom is neither sphere nor polygon.
3
Analyze the Logical Reasoning
Identify the key reason: shape is only conceivable in something composed of sides and parts. Since the atom is indivisible and partless, it cannot have shape.
This is a clear argument from the nature of compositeness.
4
Apply the Conclusion
Understand the broader implication: the indivisible atom cannot be described by any shape attribute. This refutes the opponent's dichotomy.
This illustrates a core principle of kalam physics: simplicity precedes shape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة المبنية على قولهم: ( شكل الجوهر الفرد. إما أن يكون هو الكرة أو المضلع، فنقول: الحق: أن الجوهر الفرد. لا كرة ولا مضلع. لأن هذا إنما يعقل فيما يكون مؤلفاً من الجوانب والأبعاض. والجوهر الفرد ليس كذلك. فلا يمكن وصفه بشيء من هذه الصفات.

Because this is only conceivable in something composed of sides and parts. And the indivisible atom is not like that. So it cannot be described by any of these attributes.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage refutes a claim that the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) must have a shape—either a sphere or a polygon. The author asserts that shape is only conceivable for composite bodies that have sides (جوانب) and parts (أبعاض). Since the indivisible atom is simple and lacks parts, it cannot possess any shape. Therefore, the question of whether it is spherical or polygonal is invalid.

Objection and Answer on the Triangle Argument for Divisibility
Response to an objection against the triangle argument for infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
A single triangle forces indivisible parts to be divisible.

In this lesson, we examine a specific objection to the triangle argument for infinite divisibility. The author has shown that if a body is composed of indivisible atoms, then constructing triangles from those atoms forces a part to connect two sides, implying divisibility. An objector claims this point has already been discussed, but the author insists the argument stands. We will analyze this exchange and understand its logical force.

The triangle argument is meant to show that indivisible atoms lead to contradictions when applied to geometry.
This helps grasp why the objection is raised.

3
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says the author's statement about division has been discussed before, implying it is not decisive.
Consider whether this is a valid objection or a mere dismissal.
4
Analyze the author's response
The author simply acknowledges the point was discussed, implying it was resolved in his favor. The objection does not introduce new information.
The response is brief but shows confidence in the argument.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Think about whether the objection truly undermines the triangle argument or if the author's logic remains intact.

Third Proof: Triangle Side Lengths and Infinite Divisibility
Sanusi constructs a proof from geometry to infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of certain triangles and the progression of triangle types leads to the conclusion that magnitudes are infinitely divisible.

Core Idea
Triangles from increasing numbers show angles shrink without limit, proving infinite divisibility.

This passage presents the third proof for infinite divisibility, based on geometric construction from arithmetic sequences. Sanusi demonstrates that as the side lengths increase, the triangle types progress from obtuse to right to acute, and the acute angles become progressively narrower. Since numbers can increase without bound, the angles can become arbitrarily small, implying that points on a line can be approached without end — hence magnitudes are infinitely divisible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting impossibility
Read the opening statement about triangle (1,2,3) and why it is impossible. Note the condition that the sum of any two sides must exceed the third.
This failure sets the stage for the progression that follows.
2
Observe the progression of triangle types
Identify the three cases: (2,3,4) gives obtuse, (3,4,5) gives right, (4,5,6) gives acute. Note the pattern of side lengths increasing by one each time.
The sequence is consecutive integers starting from 2.
3
Note the narrowing of acute angles
Compare the acute triangles from (4,5,6) and (5,6,7). The author states that the angle opposite the largest side is smaller in the second triangle, showing a trend of decreasing angle size.
This narrowing continues as numbers increase.
4
Connect to the conclusion of infinite divisibility
Since numbers can increase without limit, the angles can become infinitely small. The author argues that this implies magnitudes are divisible into infinitely many parts.
This is the key link between geometry and the atomic theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : قالوا : إذا أردنا أن نعمل مثلثاً من الواحد والاثنين والثلاثة ، امتنع . وإلا لزم أن يكون مجموع الضلعين مساوياً للضلع الثالث . وهو محال : أما إذا أردنا أن نعمل مثلثاً من الاثنين والثلاثة والأربعة ، حصل منه مثلث منفرج الزواية . لأن مربع الأربعة، أزيد من مجموع مربع الاثنين الثلاثة . وبهذا البيان . يظهر أن المثلث الذي يكون منفرج الزواية ، أقدم أنواع المثلثات. أما إذا أردنا أن نعمل مثلثاً من الثلاثة والأربعة والخمسة . فإنه يحصل منه مثلث قائم الزاوية ، لأن مربع الخمسة يساوي مجموع مربع الأربعة والثلاثة . أما إذا أردنا عمله من الأربعة والخمسة والستة ، حصل منه مثلث حاد الزوايا . لأن مربع الخمسة والستة أقل من مجموع مربع الأربعة . إلا أن هذا المثلث - وإن كان حاد الزوايا - إلا أنه مختلف الزوايا . وأعظمها هي الزاوية التي بوترها الضلع الأعظم - وهو الستة . وأما إذا أردنا عمل المثلث من الخمسة والستة والسبعة ، حصل أيضاً مثلث حاد الزوايا . إلا أن الزاوية التي كان بوترها الضلع الأعظم من المثلث السابق ، أعظم من الزاوية التي بوترها الضلع الأعظم من هذا المثلث . فإنا بينا : أن أول تلك الزوايا هي المنفرجة ثم القائمة ثم الحادة. وعلى هذا الطريق فلا يزال يزداد ضيق تلك الزوايا . إذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : إنه لا نهاية لمراتب تزايد الأعداد، فوجب القطع بأنه لا نهاية المراتب تصاغر تلك الحادة وتضايقها . وذلك يوجب القطع يكون المقادير قابلة للقسمة إلى غير النهاية
The third proof: They said: If we want to make a triangle from one, two, and three, it is impossible. Otherwise, the sum of two sides would equal the third side, which is impossible. But if we want to make a triangle from two, three, and four, an obtuse triangle results, because the square of four is greater than the sum of the squares of two and three. By this explanation, it appears that the obtuse triangle is the oldest type of triangles. If we want to make a triangle from three, four, and five, a right triangle results, because the square of five equals the sum of the squares of four and three. If we want to make it from four, five, and six, an acute triangle results, because the square of five and six is less than the sum of the squares of four. However, this triangle, although acute, has unequal angles. The greatest of them is the angle subtended by the greatest side, which is six. And if we want to make the triangle from five, six, and seven, also an acute triangle results, except that the angle subtended by the greatest side of the previous triangle is greater than the angle subtended by the greatest side of this triangle. For we showed that the first of those angles is obtuse, then right, then acute. And in this way, those angles keep becoming narrower. If you understand this, we say: the degrees of increase of numbers are infinite, so it must be that the degrees of diminution and constriction of those acute angles are infinite. And that necessitates that magnitudes are divisible infinitely.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is the third proof for infinite divisibility. The argument uses geometric principles: for a triangle to be constructed, the sum of any two sides must be greater than the third. Starting from side lengths (1,2,3) which fail, the author shows that as numbers increase, triangles progress from obtuse to right to acute, and within acute types, the angles become progressively smaller. Since numbers increase without limit, the angles can become infinitely small, implying that points on a line can be approached indefinitely—hence magnitudes are infinitely divisible.

Key Terms
المثلث المنفرج
المثلث القائم
المثلث الحاد
القسمة إلى غير النهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of a triangle from (1,2,3) proves that magnitudes have a finite limit of divisibility.
Correction: The impossibility shows the need for three numbers to form a triangle, but the progression of triangle types and narrowing angles demonstrates that angles can decrease infinitely, implying infinite divisibility.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what does the progression from obtuse to acute triangles demonstrate?
A) That all triangles are equally old.
B) That angles can become infinitely narrow.
C) That side lengths must be whole numbers.
D) That only right triangles are possible.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that since angles in acute triangles can become arbitrarily small as numbers increase, magnitudes must be infinitely divisible.

Fourth Proof: Equilateral Triangle and Perpendicular Line Suggest Divisibility
Fourth proof using an equilateral triangle divided by a perpendicular to argue for divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the geometry of an equilateral triangle demonstrates the necessity of divisibility in lines composed of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
The perpendicular's length must be between whole parts, proving divisibility.

This proof uses a geometric construction to challenge the idea that lines are composed of indivisible parts. By assuming an equilateral triangle with sides of three parts and dropping a perpendicular, the author derives a contradiction if the perpendicular has an integral length. This forces the conclusion that the perpendicular line must be divisible, thereby undermining the notion of indivisible parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the geometric setup
Start by understanding the initial assumption: an equilateral triangle with each side composed of three indivisible parts, and a perpendicular dropped from one vertex to the opposite side.
Visualize the triangle as having sides of length 3 units.
2
Identify the resulting triangles and angles
The perpendicular divides the equilateral triangle into two right triangles. Notice the types of angles: right, acute (from the original triangle), and half-acute (the remaining angle at the base).
The acute angle is 60°, the half-acute is 30°.
3
Analyze the hypotenuse lengths
The text assigns lengths to the hypotenuses: the hypotenuse of the right angle (the original side) is 3 parts; the hypotenuse of the half-acute angle is 2 parts. Then consider the remaining hypotenuse (the perpendicular).
These lengths are derived from the geometry.
4
Deduce the necessary length of the perpendicular
The author argues that the perpendicular cannot be 3 (would equal the side) nor 2 (would equal the other hypotenuse), so it must be between 2 and 3. This implies that the perpendicular is divisible, contradicting the assumption of indivisible parts.
This is the key step: the impossibility of integer lengths forces divisibility.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this forces the rejection of indivisible parts: if the perpendicular had an indivisible length, it would have to be an integer multiple of the assumed part size, but both integer options lead to contradictions.
The proof does not depend on the specific numbers; any integer parts would lead to similar contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : فرضنا مثلثاً متساوي الأضلاع على خط مركب من ثلاثة أجزاء.. هكذا . ولنفرض أنه نزل من إحدى زواياه خط إلى الضلع الذي بوتر تلك الزاوية . فحينئذ ينقسم ذلك المثلث بمثلثين متساويين . كل واحد منهما قائم الزاوية . فيحصل في ذلك المثلث زاوية قائمة ، وأخرى حادة ، وهي التي كانت زاوية المثلث الأول . وأخرى نصف الحادة، التي كانت حاصلة في المثلث الأول . إذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : وتر القائمة : هو الذي كان أحد أضلاع المثلث الأول . وهو ثلاثة أجزاء. ووتر نصف الحادة : جزءان . بقي الكلام في وتر الحادة التي كانت حاصلة في المثلث ، وهو الخط العمودي النازل من زاوية المثلث الأول . فإن قلنا : إنه ثلاثة أجزاء، لزم أن يكون وتر الزاوية الحادة ، مساوياً لوتر الزاوية القائمة. وإن قلنا : إنه جزءان ، لزم أن يكون وتر الحادة مساوياً لوتر نصف الحادة . وهو محال . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : إنه أزيد من الجزءين ، وأقل من الثلاثة . وذلك يوجب القسمة .
The fourth proof: We assume an equilateral triangle on a line composed of three parts. And let us assume that from one of its angles a line descends to the side which subtends that angle. Then that triangle is divided into two equal right triangles. In that triangle, a right angle appears, another acute angle which was the angle of the first triangle, and a half-acute angle that was present in the first triangle. If you understand this, we say: the hypotenuse of the right angle is one of the sides of the first triangle, and it is three parts. The hypotenuse of the half-acute angle is two parts. There remains the hypotenuse of the acute angle present in the triangle, which is the perpendicular line descending from the angle of the first triangle. If we say it is three parts, then the hypotenuse of the acute angle would equal the hypotenuse of the right angle. If we say it is two parts, then the hypotenuse of the acute angle would equal the hypotenuse of the half-acute angle, which is impossible. So it must be said that it is more than two parts and less than three parts. That necessitates division.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is the fourth proof. It starts by assuming an equilateral triangle with each side of length three (indivisible) parts. Dropping a perpendicular from one vertex to the opposite base creates two right triangles. The author then considers the hypotenuses: the original side is 3 parts, the base segment from the foot to one vertex is 2 parts (since the base is split 2+1? Actually the text says 'وتر نصف الحادة جزءان' meaning the hypotenuse of the half-acute angle is two parts. The half-acute angle is at the base? Then the perpendicular's length must be something between 2 and 3, because if it were 3, a triangle with sides (3,3,3) would be equilateral but the perpendicular cannot equal the side; if it were 2, it would match the smaller hypotenuse, which is impossible. Therefore, the perpendicular must have a length that is not an integer multiple of the assumed indivisible parts, implying that the line can be divided further.

D) Because the parts are indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The perpendicular's length must be fractional, disproving the notion that lines consist of indivisible integer parts.

Fifth Proof: Pythagorean Theorem Refutes Indivisible Atom
Sanusi presents the first aspect of the fifth proof using the Pythagorean theorem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Pythagorean theorem is used to demonstrate the impossibility of indivisible parts (atoms).

Core Idea
The hypotenuse of a right triangle proves atoms cannot exist.

The fifth proof employs the famous Pythagorean theorem (known as 'شكل العروس') to argue against atomism. By constructing a right triangle whose legs consist of two indivisible parts each, the hypotenuse becomes the irrational √8, which cannot be measured by any number of indivisible parts. This shows the logical inconsistency of the doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with 'الحجة الخامسة' indicating a new proof.
2
Identify the Pythagorean theorem
The author invokes 'شكل العروس' (the Bride's Shape), which is the Pythagorean theorem. Understand that it relates the sides of a right triangle.
3
Understand the triangle construction
Imagine a right triangle where each leg is composed of two indivisible parts (atoms). The author says: 'we compose a line from two parts, and place upon one of these two parts another part' — so the legs are each two atoms long.
4
Calculate the hypotenuse
Using the theorem, the hypotenuse squared equals sum of squares of legs: 2^2 + 2^2 = 8. So the hypotenuse is √8, which is an irrational number.
The author says it is 'أصم' (deaf), meaning irrational.
5
Reflect on the contradiction
If atoms are indivisible, any length must be a whole number of atoms. But √8 cannot be expressed as a whole number. Therefore atomism is false.
The passage cuts off but the argument is complete for this aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : قالوا : ثبت بشكل العروس ، أن وتر الزاوية القائمة لا بد وأن يكون جذراً . لمجموع مربعي الضلعين المحيطين بتلك القائمة . وإذا ثبت هذا لزم فساد القول بالجوهر الفرد ، من وجوه : الأول : إنا إذا ركبنا خطاً من جزءين ، ووضعنا فوق أحد هذين الجزءين جزءاً آخر ، حتى حصل هناك مثلث قائم الزاوية ، كل واحد من ضلعيـه جزءان . فههنا يجب أن يكون وتر هذه القائمة جذر الثمانية . لكنه أصم
The fifth proof: They said: It is established by the Bride's Shape (Pythagorean theorem) that the hypotenuse of a right angle must be the square root of the sum of the squares of the two sides surrounding that right angle. And if this is established, the falsity of the doctrine of the indivisible atom follows from several aspects: First: If we compose a line from two parts, and we place upon one of these two parts another part, so that there results a right triangle with each of its two sides being two parts, then the hypotenuse of this right angle must be the square root of eight, but it is an irrational number (asamm).
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof. It invokes the Pythagorean theorem (called 'Bride's Shape' or 'شكل العروس'). The author argues that if we construct a right triangle with each leg consisting of two indivisible parts, the hypotenuse would be √8, an irrational number. Since an indivisible part cannot be split to measure such a length, this shows the impossibility of indivisible atoms. The argument begins with the first of several aspects; the text cuts off but the point is clear.

Key Terms

جذر
أصم
الجوهر الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Indivisible atoms can compose any length.
Correction: The Pythagorean theorem shows that the hypotenuse of a right triangle with legs composed of integer parts would be irrational, impossible to measure with indivisible atoms.

Checkpoint

B) Because the hypotenuse would be √8, an irrational number, impossible with indivisible parts.
C) Because the triangle could not be formed.
D) Because the two sides are equal.

Key Takeaway
The Pythagorean theorem, when applied to a triangle made of indivisible parts, yields an irrational hypotenuse, refuting the possibility of indivisible atoms.

The Diagonal Argument Against Atomism: Second Division
Proof by contradiction: second division of the diagonal argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from the diagonal and side of a square refutes the theory of the indivisible atom by showing that both outcomes of the second possibility lead to contradictions.

Core Idea
The diagonal argument shows that atomism leads to geometric contradictions.

This passage continues a proof against the indivisible atom. It examines the second possibility: parts along the diagonal do not meet, creating gaps. The passage then shows that both possible cases for the size of those gaps lead to contradictions, thereby refuting atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice that the passage starts mid-argument, so the first division was already covered.
2
Identify the Two Sub-Cases
Under the second division, the text considers two possibilities for the gaps: either they can accommodate an atom or they cannot. Find these two cases in the text.
The first case leads to the diagonal equaling the sum of the two sides; the second leads to something smaller than an atom.
3
Understand Why Each Case is a Contradiction
For each sub-case, the author shows it leads to a 'khalaf' (contradiction). The first violates the known geometric fact that the diagonal is longer than the side; the second implies the atom is divisible.
4
Appreciate the Overall Conclusion
The passage concludes that all divisions under the assumption of atomism are false, so the theory of the indivisible atom must be false.
This is a powerful reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعرض متلاقية. فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون القطر مساوياً للضلع. وهذا باطل محال. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: إن هذه الأجزاء غير متلاقية من جانب القطر. بل تكون متباعدة، فحينئذ حصل فيما بين تلك الأجزاء الأربعة: فرج ثلاثة من جانب القطر. فكل واحد من تلك الفرج. إما أن يتسع لجوهر فرد، أو لا يتسع له. فإن كان الأول. فحينئذ يكون مقدار القطر مثل سبعة أجزاء، ومجموع الضلعين أيضاً سبعة أجزاء. فيكون القطر مساوياً للضلعين. هذا خلف. وإن كان الثاني وهو أن كل واحد من تلك الفرج أصغر من الجوهر، فحينئذ قد وجد شيء أصغر حجماً من الجوهر الفرد فيكون الجوهر الفرد منقسماً. هذا خلف. فظهر أن على تقدير إثبات الجوهر الفرد تكون جميع هذه الأقسام باطلة. فوجب أن يكون القول بالجوهر الفرد باطلاً.
And the width [they] meet. Then it follows that the diagonal equals the side. This is false and impossible. As for the second division: that it is said: these parts do not meet from the side of the diagonal. Rather they separate, then occurs between those four parts: three gaps from the side of the diagonal. So each one of those gaps: either it accommodates an indivisible atom, or it does not accommodate it. If the first, then the measure of the diagonal is like seven parts, and the sum of the two sides also seven parts. So the diagonal equals the two sides. This is a contradiction. And if the second, that each one of those gaps is smaller than the atom, then something smaller in volume than the indivisible atom exists, so the indivisible atom is divisible. This is a contradiction. So it appears that on the hypothesis of affirming the indivisible atom, all these divisions are false. So it is necessary that the statement of the indivisible atom be false.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the theory of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) based on the relationship between the diagonal and the side of a square. The argument assumes that the side of a square is composed of a certain number of indivisible atoms arranged in a line, and similarly the diagonal. The argument explores two possibilities regarding the arrangement of parts along the diagonal. The first possibility (which was discussed before the text begins) is that the parts meet at the diagonal; that leads to the diagonal being equal to the side, which is false. The second possibility (detailed here) is that the parts do not meet, creating gaps. The gaps are then analyzed: each gap either accommodates an atom or does not. If it accommodates an atom, the diagonal becomes longer (seven parts vs. the sum of two sides also seven? Actually the argument says the diagonal equals the two sides, not the side—this seems to be a specific configuration). If it does not accommodate, then there is something smaller than an atom, implying divisibility. Both lead to contradictions, so the premise of the indivisible atom is false. The argument is technical and relies on geometrical intuition.

ضلع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes a specific number of atoms (4 per side) and is not general.
Correction: The argument is general: any number of atoms on the side leads to similar contradictions; the specific case illustrates the logical impossibility.

Checkpoint

B) They accommodate an indivisible atom

Key Takeaway

Euclid's Proof and the Indivisible Atom
Euclid's proposition that any rectilinear surface can be squared is incompatible with indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's theorem about squaring any rectilinear surface disproves the theory of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
Euclid's geometry proves atoms cannot explain surface area.

In this lesson, we examine the second argument used by mutakallimun against the existence of indivisible atoms. The argument leverages a well-known theorem from Euclid's Elements: any polygonal surface can be converted into an equal square. If surfaces were composed of atoms, that conversion would be impossible without breaking atoms, which contradicts the theorem. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the logical structure: premise, contradiction, examples, conclusion.
Pay attention to the phrase 'يُبطل ذلك' – this signals the contradiction.
2
Understand Euclid's theorem
The argument begins with Euclid's proven proposition: any rectilinear surface can be squared. This is an established geometric fact.
The 'last figure of the second article' likely refers to Elements Book II, Proposition 14.
3
See the contradiction
If surfaces were made of indivisible atoms, squaring would require cutting atoms, which is impossible. This directly contradicts Euclid's theorem.
The key point: denial of squaring feasibility under atomism.
4
Examine the examples
Two examples are given: a triangle of three parts, and a triangle with sides of three atoms each. Both cannot be squared without dividing atoms.
The examples illustrate why atomism fails for even simple triangles.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The argument concludes that the atomist doctrine is false because it conflicts with a proven geometric theorem.
This is a direct refutation of the indivisible atom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: إن (أقليدس) برهن في الشكل الأخير من المقالة الثانية: على أنه يمكن عمل مربع مساوي لأي سطح مستقيم الخطوط. لكن القول بأن السطوح إنما تتألف من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ يبطل ذلك. لأن المثلث المعمول من ثلاثة أجزاء لا يمكن البتة عمل مربع مساوي له. وأيضاً: إذا عملنا مثلثاً، بحيث يكون كل واحد من أضلاعه: ثلاثة ثلاثة. فإنه يكون مجموع أجزائه. ولا يمكن عمل مربع مساوي له إلا بقطع الأجزاء. وهذا القول في سائر مراتب المثلثات. فعلمنا: أن هذا الشكل يبطل القول بإثبات الجوهر الفرد.
The second argument: Euclid proved in the last figure of the second article that it is possible to construct a square equal to any rectilinear surface. But the statement that surfaces are composed of indivisible parts invalidates that. Because a triangle constructed from three parts cannot possibly have a square equal to it constructed. Also: if we construct a triangle such that each of its sides is three, then it is the sum of its parts, and a square equal to it cannot be constructed except by cutting the parts. And this statement applies to all types of triangles. So we know that this figure invalidates the affirmation of the indivisible atom.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This is the second argument against the indivisible atom, drawing on Euclid's Elements. Euclid proved that for any polygonal surface, one can construct a square of equal area. But if surfaces are composed of indivisible atoms, then a triangle made of three atoms (e.g., each side three atoms) cannot be squared without cutting the atoms, which is impossible because atoms are indivisible. Thus the theory contradicts a proven theorem.

Key Terms
أقليدس
مربع
سطح مستقيم الخطوط
مثلث
جزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Indivisible atoms can be used to construct any geometric figure.
Correction: Euclid's theorem shows that squaring a rectilinear surface is always possible, but if surfaces were atomic, it would be impossible for many triangles, thus atoms cannot exist.

Checkpoint
What does Euclid's proposition state?
A) A square can be constructed equal to any triangle
B) A square can be constructed equal to any rectilinear surface
C) A triangle can be squared only if its sides are equal
D) Indivisible atoms are necessary for geometry

Key Takeaway
Euclid's theorem on squaring rectilinear surfaces offers a powerful geometric proof against the existence of indivisible atoms.

Geometric Proof Against Atomism
The diagonal and side are incommensurable, but if composed of atoms, they would be commensurable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a geometric fact about incommensurability is used in kalam to disprove atomism.

Core Idea
Geometry proves diagonal and side are incommensurable; atomism would make them commensurable, which is impossible.

The third argument employs a well-known geometric truth: the diagonal and side of a square have no common measure. If the world were made of indivisible atoms, then every length would be a multiple of a tiny unit, making the diagonal and side commensurable—contradicting geometric certainty. This argument powerfully refutes the atomist claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the geometric premise
Recognize that the diagonal and side of a square are incommensurable: their ratio is irrational, a proven geometric fact.
2
Consider the atomist hypothesis
If both were composed of indivisible atoms, their lengths would be whole-number multiples of the atom, so their ratio would be rational.
3
Recognize the contradiction
Rational ratio implies commensurability, contradicting the geometric proof. Hence atomism cannot account for this simple geometric figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: ثبت بالبراهين الهندسية: أن القطر مباين للضلع. ولو كان القطر مركباً من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ والضلع أيضاً مركب من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ فحينئذ تكون نسبة القطر إلى الضلع كنسبة إلى عدد آخر وحينئذ يكونان مشتركين لا متباينين. فثبت بهذا: أن تركيب المربع من الجوهر الفرد محال والله أعلم.
The third argument: It is established by geometrical proofs that the diagonal is incommensurable with the side. But if the diagonal were composed of indivisible parts, and the side also composed of indivisible parts, then the ratio of the diagonal to the side would be as one number to another number, and then they would be commensurable, not incommensurable. So it is established by this that the composition of a square from the indivisible atom is impossible, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This argument uses the well-known fact from geometry that the diagonal of a square is incommensurable with the side (i.e., their ratio is irrational). If both were composed of a finite number of indivisible atoms, then their lengths would be in a rational ratio (as whole numbers), contradicting the proven incommensurability. Therefore, the square cannot be composed of atoms.

Euclid's Parallel Lines Argument Against Atomism
Euclid's theorem on parallelograms, argument against atoms, objection, and partial answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's theorem on parallelogram equality is used to argue against the existence of indivisible atoms, and how an objection based on infinite length is addressed.

Core Idea
Euclid's geometry disproves atomism: equal areas must have equal atom counts, but slanted shapes require more atoms.

This lesson presents the fourth kalam argument against atomism, drawn from Euclid's Elements. The argument uses the geometrical fact that parallelograms on the same base and between parallels are equal in area. When applied to atomic composition, it generates a contradiction. You will also see a classic objection and the beginning of its reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Note that the answer to the objection is incomplete in the source.
The Arabic text cuts off at 'بقدر'.
2
Understand Euclid's theorem
Euclid proved that parallelograms on the same base and between the same parallels are equal in area. This is used as a premise.
3
Follow the argument against atoms
Suppose one parallelogram is a square of side 10 (area 100). Another parallelogram on the same base with same area, but slanted, would require the same number of atoms. But due to the slant, it would need more rows, leading to impossibility.
The exact reasoning: if composed of atoms, the slanted shape would have a different number of atoms in each row, but the total must equal 100, which is impossible if atoms are indivisible.
4
Examine the objection
The objection says: Euclid's theorem itself seems impossible because a 1-cubit parallelogram could equal one extending from east to west. How can that be rational?
5
Analyze the partial answer
The answer states that as the slanted surface increases in length, it decreases in width proportionally. The impossibility would only arise if the width remained constant. However, the answer is cut off, so the full resolution is unknown.
The text breaks off at 'بقدر'.
