⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: إن (أقليدس) برهن في المقالة الأولى: على أن السطوح المتوازية الأضلاع، التي تكون على قاعدة واحدة، وفي جهة واحدة، وفيها بين خطوط بأعيانها متوازية فإنه يجب أن يساوي بعضها بعضاً. وإذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: إن هذا يبطل القول بالجوهر الفرد لأنا إذا قدرنا أحد السطحين عشرة في عشرة حتى كان مجموعه مائة، وكان السطح الآخر مائة. يلزم أن يكون مجموع الأجزاء الحاصلة في ذلك السطح، مساوية لمائة جزء. وذلك محال. فإن قالوا: ( هذا الإشكال أيضاً لازم على (أقليدس)، لأن أحد السطحين، إذا كان ذراعاً، والآخر طوله من المشرق إلى المغرب. فكيف يعقل كون أحدهما مساوياً للآخر؟ ) قلنا: السطحان المتوازيان. إذا كان أحدهما قائماً على قاعدته، وكان الآخر مائلاً، وكانا جميعاً على قاعدة واحدة، فيما بين خطين متوازيين، فإن بمقدار ما يزداد السطح الماثل في الطول، فإنه ينتقص من العرض. والمحال إنما كان يلزم لو كان عرض السطح المائل. بقدر
The fourth argument: Euclid proved in the first article that parallelograms which are on the same base and in the same direction and between the same parallels must be equal to each other. And that is impossible. For if they say: 'This difficulty is also binding on Euclid, because if one of the two surfaces is a cubit, and the other extends from east to west, how can it be rational that one equals the other?' We say: The two parallelograms, if one stands on its base and the other is slanted, and both are on one base between two parallel lines, then to the extent that the slanted surface increases in length, it decreases in width. The impossibility would only follow if the width of the slanted surface were as much...

Commentary
This argument uses Euclid's proposition that parallelograms on the same base and between the same parallels are equal in area. Suppose a square of side 10 (area 100). Consider another parallelogram on the same base but slanted, also with area 100. If composed of atoms, the slanted one would require the same number of atoms, but due to its slant it would have more rows? The argument is that it is impossible. Then an objection is raised: Euclid's theorem itself seems to allow a very long rectangle to equal a small square, but the answer is that as the length increases, the width decreases proportionally, so the impossibility arises only if the width were something else. Proof from Diagonal and Side Ratio
A concise geometrical refutation of atomism using the incommensurability of diagonal and side.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the incommensurability of the diagonal and side of a square refutes the existence of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
Atomism is self-contradictory because it forces the irrational ratio √2 to be rational.

This short but powerful argument uses basic geometry to challenge a cornerstone of atomism. If matter is composed of indivisible atoms, then the diagonal and side of a square would have to be commensurable (a rational ratio). But we know they are incommensurable, so the assumption is false. The author uses this to conclude that a square cannot be made of atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation to get a feel for the flow of the argument.
Notice the concise style and the key terms.
2
Identify the key claim about the ratio
The passage states that if atoms exist, the diagonal-to-side ratio becomes like a ratio to another number, i.e., rational.
This is the 'commensurable' condition.
3
Understand why atomism forces commensurability
If a square is made of discrete atoms, the diagonal and side can be measured in integer multiples of the atom, so their ratio is rational.
Imagine counting the number of atoms along each side.
4
Recognize the known incommensurability
Geometry tells us that the diagonal and side of a square are incommensurable; their ratio is the irrational number √2.
This is a well-established mathematical fact.
5
See the contradiction and conclusion
Atomism leads to a contradiction with geometry, so the composition of a square from indivisible atoms is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ فحينئذ تكون نسبة القطر إلى الضلع كنسبة إلى عدد آخر وحينئذ يكونان مشتركين لا متباينين. فثبت بهذا أن تركيب المربع من الجوهر الفرد محال. والله أعلم.
The indivisible parts. Then the ratio of the diagonal to the side is like the ratio to another number. Then they are common, not distinct. So it is established by this that the composition of a square from the indivisible atom is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This is a concise geometrical proof that refutes the doctrine of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). The author argues that if a square is composed of atoms, then its diagonal and side would have a ratio expressible as a ratio of two integers (like the ratio to another number). This would mean that the diagonal and side are commensurable—that is, they share a common measure (mutasharikayn) rather than being incommensurable (mutabayinayn). But it is a known fact in geometry that the diagonal and side of a square are incommensurable (their ratio is irrational, √2). Therefore, the assumption that the square is composed of atoms leads to a contradiction, proving that such composition is impossible. The author concludes with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
القطر
الضلع
نسبة
مشتركين
متباينين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular and relies on assuming incommensurability.
Correction: The proof relies on the known geometrical fact of incommensurability, which is independent of atomism. It shows that atomism contradicts established geometry, not that geometry is based on atomism.

Checkpoint

B) The diagonal and side are incommensurable.
C) The composition of a square from atoms is impossible.
D) The atoms must be divisible.

Key Takeaway
The incommensurability of diagonal and side refutes the atomic composition of a square, showing that atomism leads to a geometric contradiction.

Euclid's Parallelogram Theorem Refutes Atomism
Euclid's theorem is used to refute atomism, then an objection is answered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's theorem on equal-area parallelograms is used to argue against the existence of indivisible atoms, and how a geometric objection is resolved.

Core Idea
Same base, same parallels, same area — impossible for atoms.

In this fourth proof, the author leverages a well-known theorem from Euclid's Elements to challenge the doctrine of atomism. The argument is subtle: it shows that if surfaces are made of indivisible atoms, then two very differently shaped parallelograms would have to contain the same number of atoms, which leads to absurdity. An objection is raised and answered, strengthening the proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand Euclid's theorem
Recall that parallelograms on the same base and between parallel lines have equal areas. This is the key geometric fact used.
This is a classical Euclidean result from Book I.
2
Apply the theorem to atomism
Assume a 10×10 square (100 area) and another parallelogram on the same base and between parallels with the same area. If surfaces are atoms, both must have 100 atoms. But the elongated shape would require an impossible arrangement of atoms.
3
Examine the objection
The objector argues: if one shape is a cubit and the other is extremely long, how can they equal in area? This seems to break Euclid's theorem.
The objection is meant to discredit the proof.
4
Understand the answer
The author explains that in an inclined parallelogram, the width decreases as length increases, so area is conserved. The absurdity would only arise if the width remained the same, which it does not.
The answer relies on the geometric properties of parallelograms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: إن أقليدس برهن في المقالة الأولى: على أن السطوح المتوازية الأضلاع، التي تكون على قاعدة واحدة، وفي جهة واحدة، وفيها بين خطوط بأعيانها متوازية إنه يجب أن يساوي بعضها بعضاً. وإذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: إن هذا يبطل القول بالجوهر الفرد لأنا إذا قدرنا أحد السطحين عشرة في عشرة حتى كان مجموعه مائة، وكان السطح الآخر مائة. يلزم أن يكون مجموع الأجزاء الحاصلة في ذلك السطح، مساوية لمائة جزء. وذلك محال. فإن قالوا: هذا الإشكال أيضاً لازم على أقليدس، لأن أحد السطحين إذا كان ذراعاً، والآخر طوله من المشرق إلى المغرب، فكيف يعقل كون أحدهما مساوياً للآخر؟ قلنا: السطحان المتوازيان إذا كان أحدهما قائماً على قاعدته، وكان الآخر مائلاً، وكانا جميعاً على قاعدة واحدة، فيما بين خطين متوازيين، فإن بمقدار ما يزداد السطح المائل في الطول، فإنه ينتقص من العرض. والمحال إنما كان يلزم لو كان عرض السطح المائل بقدر القاعدة المشتركة. لكنه ليس الأمر كذلك، بمقدار ما ازداد في الطول انتقص عن العرض. فزال الإشكال. والله أعلم.
The fourth proof: That Euclid proved in the first book: that parallelograms which are on the same base and in the same direction and between specific parallel lines must equal each other. And if this is established, we say: This invalidates the doctrine of the indivisible atom because if we suppose one of the surfaces to be ten by ten so that its total is one hundred, and the other surface is one hundred, it would be necessary that the total of the parts occurring in that surface be equal to one hundred parts. That is impossible. If they say: This difficulty is also incumbent upon Euclid, because if one of the surfaces is one cubit and the other is its length from east to west, how can it be conceived that one equals the other? We say: The two parallel surfaces, if one of them is upright on its base and the other is inclined, and both are on the same base, between two parallel lines, then to the extent that the inclined surface increases in length, it decreases in breadth. And the impossibility would follow only if the breadth of the inclined surface were equal to the common base. But it is not so; to the extent that it increased in length, it decreased in breadth. So the difficulty is removed. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This is the fourth proof against atomism, based on Euclid's theorem (Book I) that parallelograms on the same base and between the same parallels have equal area. The author applies this to the atomic theory: suppose one surface (a square) is 10×10 = 100 square units; another surface, a parallelogram with the same base and between the same parallels, also has area 100. If surfaces are composed of atoms, then the number of atoms in each must be equal—100 each. But this leads to contradiction because the parallelogram could be extremely elongated yet still have area 100, implying an enormous number of atoms compressed into a thin strip, which is impossible if atoms are indivisible. An objection is raised: this reasoning seems to fail in the extreme case where one surface is one cubit square and the other is a line-like strip from east to west; how can they have equal area? The author answers by invoking Euclid's own demonstration: the inclined surface gains length but loses width proportionally, so the area is preserved only if the width is less than the base. The impossibility would occur only if the width equaled the base, which is not the case. Thus the objection is resolved, and the proof stands: atomism forces an impossible equality of atomic count in very different shapes, so atomism is false.

Key Terms
الحجة الرابعة
اقليدس
السطوح المتوازية الأضلاع
القاعدة
السطح المائل
الإشكال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the objection shows a real flaw in Euclid's theorem or that the proof fails in extreme cases.
Correction: The author shows that the objection misunderstands the geometry: in an inclined parallelogram, the width decreases proportionally with increasing length, so the area remains equal. The proof is sound.

Checkpoint
What is the key principle from Euclid used in this proof?
A) The sum of angles in a triangle is 180 degrees.
B) Parallelograms on the same base and between parallels have equal areas.
C) The diagonal of a square is irrational.
D) A circle can be drawn with any center and radius.

Key Takeaway
Euclid's theorem on equal-area parallelograms provides a geometric argument against atomism: shapes with vastly different dimensions cannot be composed of the same number of indivisible atoms.

First Proof: Infinite Polygons and Interior Angles
First proof: infinite polygon ranks imply infinite divisibility of magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the infinite increase in polygon sides demonstrates that magnitude is infinitely divisible, refuting the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Polygon sides increase infinitely, so interior angles never reach two right angles, proving infinite divisibility.

The author begins by establishing the first of three geometrical proofs against the atom. He shows that polygons correspond to numbers, and as sides increase, interior angles expand but never become a straight line. This continuous expansion proves that the sum of two right angles is infinitely divisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the Arabic passage for the first proof carefully. Notice how the author links polygon ranks to number ranks.
2
Follow the reasoning
The interior angles increase as sides increase but never reach two right angles. Why? Because that would make the sides form a straight line, impossible for a polygon.
This is the crux of the proof.
3
Conclude the divisibility
Since the interior angle expansion is infinite, the two-right-angle sum must be infinitely divisible. Thus magnitude is not made of indivisible parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذه المقدمات. فنقول : اعتبار حال هذه المضلعات يدل على بطلان القول بالجوهر الفرد من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : إنه لا نهاية لمراتب المضلعات . فإن مراتبها بحسب مراتب الأعداد . فأولها : الذي له ثلاثة أضلاع وثانيها : الذي له أربعة أضلاع . وثالثها : الذي له خمسة أضلاع. وإذا كان لا نهاية المراتب الزيادة في الأعداد فكذلك لا نهاية لمراتب الأضلاع. وقد عرفت أنه كلما كانت الأضلاع أكثر عدداً ، كان اتساع الزوايا الداخلة أكثر. مع أنها البتة لا ننتهي إلى حد القائمتين . وإلا لزم أن يتصل أحد الضلعين بالآخر على الاستقامة وذلك محال . فثبت : أن مجموع القائمتين ، أولى أن يقبل القسمة إلى غير النهاية . والثاني : إنا بينا : أنه كلما صارت الزوايا الداخلة أوسع ، صارت الزوايا الخارجة أضيق . ولما كان لا نهاية لمراتب ذلك الاتساع ، فكذلك لا نهاية لمراتب هذا الضيق . وعند هذا يظهر أن القائمة الثابتة تبتدي بالانقسام من وقت عمل المخمس ، وتمر في قبول القسمة إلى غير النهاية . أما في داخل المضلع فبسبب الاتساع وأما في الخارج فبسبب التضايق . والثالث : إن كل نقطة في السطح، فإنه يفرض حولها أربع قوائم . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لما كانت الأضلاع قابلة للتزايد إلى غير النهاية ، كانت المثلثات الواقعة في المضلعات قابلة للتزايد إلى غير النهاية . وإذا كانت رؤوس تلك المثلثات الواقعة عند المركز قابلة للتزايد إلى غير النهاية كانت القوائم الأربع الحاصلة عند مركز المضلع ، قابلة للقسمة إلى غير النهاية . فهذه وجوه ثلاثة دالة على كون المقدار قابلاً للقسمة إلى غير النهاية : مستنبطة من هذه المباحث المفرعة على هذه المضلعات .
And when you have known these preliminaries, we say: The consideration of the state of these polygons indicates the invalidity of the statement of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) from three aspects: First: There is no end to the ranks of polygons. For their ranks are according to the ranks of numbers. The first is that which has three sides, the second is that which has four sides, and the third is that which has five sides. And if there is no end to the ranks of increase in numbers, likewise there is no end to the ranks of sides. And you have known that whenever the sides are more numerous, the expansion of the interior angles is greater. Yet they absolutely do not terminate at the limit of two right angles. Otherwise it would necessitate that one of the two sides joins the other in a straight line, and that is impossible. So it is established that the sum of two right angles is more worthy of being divisible infinitely. Second: We have shown that whenever the interior angles become wider, the exterior angles become narrower. And since there is no end to the ranks of that expansion, likewise there is no end to the ranks of this narrowing. And at this point it becomes apparent that the fixed right angle begins to divide from the time of constructing the pentagon, and continues in accepting division infinitely. As for inside the polygon, it is due to expansion, and as for outside, it is due to narrowing. Third: Every point on a surface has four right angles assumed around it. When this is established, we say: Since the sides are capable of increase without end, the triangles falling in the polygons are capable of increase without end. And when the vertices of those triangles falling at the center are capable of increase without end, the four right angles occurring at the center of the polygon are capable of division without end. So these are three aspects indicating that magnitude is capable of division without end, derived from these investigations branching from these polygons.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents three geometrical proofs against the concept of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). The author first notes that the series of polygons (triangles, quadrilaterals, pentagons, etc.) is infinite, corresponding to the infinite series of numbers. As the number of sides increases, the interior angles expand but never reach two right angles (180°), because that would make the sides form a straight line, which is impossible for a polygon. Therefore, the two-right-angle sum must be infinitely divisible, implying that magnitude itself is infinitely divisible. The second proof considers that as interior angles widen, exterior angles narrow, and since the expansion is infinite, the narrowing is also infinite, showing that a right angle begins to divide from the construction of a pentagon and continues to be divisible infinitely—both internally and externally. The third proof considers a point on a surface; around it are four right angles. As sides increase without end, the triangles in the polygon and thus the vertices at the center increase without end, so the four right angles at the center are also infinitely divisible. Together, these three arguments demonstrate that magnitude (al-miqdar) is infinitely divisible, refuting atomism.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
المضلعات
القائمتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that polygonal angles can eventually become a straight line if the polygon has infinite sides.
Correction: The author argues that even with infinite sides, interior angles never reach two right angles because that would break the polygon shape.

Checkpoint
Why can the interior angles of a polygon never reach two right angles?
A) Because polygons always have at least three sides.
B) Because that would cause the sides to connect in a straight line, which is not a polygon.
C) Because the exterior angles become too narrow.
D) Because polygons cannot have more than 180 sides.

Key Takeaway
The infinite increase in polygon sides proves that the sum of two right angles is infinitely divisible, undermining atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof shows that reason alone can demonstrate limits of what is possible. Where in your life have you assumed something is absolutely small or simple without considering that it might be infinitely complex? Will you investigate that assumption today?
?
The polygonal series never reaches two right angles—it approaches but never arrives. Is there a goal or ideal in your life you are endlessly approaching but never achieving? What does this geometric truth teach you about patience and the nature of perfection?

Second Proof: The Narrowing Exterior Angles
Second proof: interior-exterior angle relationship implies infinite divisibility of right angles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relationship between interior and exterior angles in polygons demonstrates infinite divisibility.

Core Idea
As interior angles widen, exterior angles narrow infinitely, proving right angles are infinitely divisible.

The second proof examines the inverse relationship between interior and exterior angles. As interior angles expand without limit, exterior angles contract without limit, showing that a fixed right angle divides infinitely from both inside and outside.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic passage for the second proof. Pay attention to the correlation between interior and exterior angles.
2
Follow the relationship
Interior angles expand; exterior angles narrow. Both have no end.
This is key.
3
Notice the right angle
The right angle begins to divide from the construction of the pentagon and continues infinitely.
The pentagon is just a starting point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذه المقدمات. فنقول : اعتبار حال هذه المضلعات يدل على بطلان القول بالجوهر الفرد من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : إنه لا نهاية لمراتب المضلعات . فإن مراتبها بحسب مراتب الأعداد . فأولها : الذي له ثلاثة أضلاع وثانيها : الذي له أربعة أضلاع . وثالثها : الذي له خمسة أضلاع. وإذا كان لا نهاية المراتب الزيادة في الأعداد فكذلك لا نهاية لمراتب الأضلاع. وقد عرفت أنه كلما كانت الأضلاع أكثر عدداً ، كان اتساع الزوايا الداخلة أكثر. مع أنها البتة لا ننتهي إلى حد القائمتين . وإلا لزم أن يتصل أحد الضلعين بالآخر على الاستقامة وذلك محال . فثبت : أن مجموع القائمتين ، أولى أن يقبل القسمة إلى غير النهاية . والثاني : إنا بينا : أنه كلما صارت الزوايا الداخلة أوسع ، صارت الزوايا الخارجة أضيق . ولما كان لا نهاية لمراتب ذلك الاتساع ، فكذلك لا نهاية لمراتب هذا الضيق . وعند هذا يظهر أن القائمة الثابتة تبتدي بالانقسام من وقت عمل المخمس ، وتمر في قبول القسمة إلى غير النهاية . أما في داخل المضلع فبسبب الاتساع وأما في الخارج فبسبب التضايق . والثالث : إن كل نقطة في السطح، فإنه يفرض حولها أربع قوائم . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لما كانت الأضلاع قابلة للتزايد إلى غير النهاية ، كانت المثلثات الواقعة في المضلعات قابلة للتزايد إلى غير النهاية . وإذا كانت رؤوس تلك المثلثات الواقعة عند المركز قابلة للتزايد إلى غير النهاية كانت القوائم الأربع الحاصلة عند مركز المضلع ، قابلة للقسمة إلى غير النهاية . فهذه وجوه ثلاثة دالة على كون المقدار قابلاً للقسمة إلى غير النهاية : مستنبطة من هذه المباحث المفرعة على هذه المضلعات .
And when you have known these preliminaries, we say: The consideration of the state of these polygons indicates the invalidity of the statement of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) from three aspects: First: There is no end to the ranks of polygons. For their ranks are according to the ranks of numbers. The first is that which has three sides, the second is that which has four sides, and the third is that which has five sides. And if there is no end to the ranks of increase in numbers, likewise there is no end to the ranks of sides. And you have known that whenever the sides are more numerous, the expansion of the interior angles is greater. Yet they absolutely do not terminate at the limit of two right angles. Otherwise it would necessitate that one of the two sides joins the other in a straight line, and that is impossible. So it is established that the sum of two right angles is more worthy of being divisible infinitely. Second: We have shown that whenever the interior angles become wider, the exterior angles become narrower. And since there is no end to the ranks of that expansion, likewise there is no end to the ranks of this narrowing. And at this point it becomes apparent that the fixed right angle begins to divide from the time of constructing the pentagon, and continues in accepting division infinitely. As for inside the polygon, it is due to expansion, and as for outside, it is due to narrowing. Third: Every point on a surface has four right angles assumed around it. When this is established, we say: Since the sides are capable of increase without end, the triangles falling in the polygons are capable of increase without end. And when the vertices of those triangles falling at the center are capable of increase without end, the four right angles occurring at the center of the polygon are capable of division without end. So these are three aspects indicating that magnitude is capable of division without end, derived from these investigations branching from these polygons.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents three geometrical proofs against the concept of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). The author first notes that the series of polygons (triangles, quadrilaterals, pentagons, etc.) is infinite, corresponding to the infinite series of numbers. As the number of sides increases, the interior angles expand but never reach two right angles (180°), because that would make the sides form a straight line, which is impossible for a polygon. Therefore, the two-right-angle sum must be infinitely divisible, implying that magnitude itself is infinitely divisible. The second proof considers that as interior angles widen, exterior angles narrow, and since the expansion is infinite, the narrowing is also infinite, showing that a right angle begins to divide from the construction of a pentagon and continues to be divisible infinitely—both internally and externally. The third proof considers a point on a surface; around it are four right angles. As sides increase without end, the triangles in the polygon and thus the vertices at the center increase without end, so the four right angles at the center are also infinitely divisible. Together, these three arguments demonstrate that magnitude (al-miqdar) is infinitely divisible, refuting atomism.

Third Proof: Triangles and the Center of a Polygon
Third proof: infinite triangles at polygon center imply infinite divisibility of central right angles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the infinite increase in triangles within a polygon leads to infinite divisibility of four right angles at the center.

Core Idea
Infinite increase in polygon sides creates infinite triangles at the center, making central right angles infinitely divisible.

The third proof uses the concept of triangles formed from the center of a polygon to the sides. As the number of polygon sides increases without end, so does the number of these triangles, and thus the four right angles at the center become infinitely divisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic passage for the third proof. Notice how it uses triangles and the center.
2
Understand the relation
As polygon sides increase, the triangles from the center to sides increase. The vertices of these triangles at the center also increase.
3
Apply to right angles
Since the number of vertices increases infinitely, the four right angles at the center are divisible infinitely. Thus magnitude is not atomic.

Objection to the Polygonal Construction Proof
An objection and response about constructing polygons with many sides.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the geometrical proofs assume the possibility of constructing polygons with arbitrarily many sides, and the author's brief response by reference to the circle.

Core Idea
Every argument rests on assumptions — this objection exposes one hidden assumption.

Sanusi presents a powerful objection to the three proofs he has just given. The proofs rely on the idea that we can construct polygons with any number of sides. But is that actually possible? The objector doubts it, and Sanusi responds by pointing to an earlier discussion on the circle. Let's examine this exchange carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the passage once to grasp the objector's challenge. Notice the question: 'Why do you say that is possible? What is the evidence for it?'
The objector is questioning a key assumption, not providing a counterexample.
2
Identify the hidden assumption
The objection points out that the proofs assume one can construct a polygon with arbitrarily many sides. Identify why this might be problematic: as the number of sides increases, the angle disappears, and the sides become a straight line.
This challenges the very possibility of the figure used in the proofs.
3
Understand the response
The author responds briefly by saying that if one attempts to prove the possibility via the circle, that discussion has already preceded. Consider why the circle is relevant: polygons are often constructed by inscribing in a circle, so if circles are established, polygons become constructible.
The response assumes the reader remembers the earlier proof about the circle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنها مباحث قوية. والذي يمكن أن يقال في دفعها : إن هذا الكلام مبني على أنه يمكن تركيب المضلع، بأي عدد أزيد . فلم قلتم : إن ذلك ممكن ؟ وما الدليل عليه ؟ فإن المضلع إذا اتسع جداً، لم تبق الزاوية البتة . وحينئذ لا يمكن أن يتصل أحد الخطين بالآخر، إلا على الاستقامة . فإن أرادوا إقامة البرهان عليه بالبناء على الدائرة . فإن الكلام على إثبات الدائرة قد سبق والله أعلم .
And know: they are strong investigations. What can be said in refutation of them is: This speech is based on the possibility of constructing a polygon with any greater number [of sides]. So why do you say that is possible? And what is the evidence for it? For if the polygon becomes extremely wide, there remains no angle at all. And then it is not possible for one of the two lines to join the other except in a straight line. If they intend to establish a proof for it by building on the circle, then the discussion on establishing the circle has already preceded. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous three proofs and a brief response. The author first states that the above geometrical investigations are strong. Then he introduces a possible objection: the entire argument assumes that one can construct a polygon with any larger number of sides, but this is not obviously possible. The objector asks: why do you claim it is possible, and what is the proof? For if a polygon becomes very wide (i.e., has an extremely large number of sides), the interior angle essentially disappears, and the sides become virtually a straight line, so the figure ceases to be a polygon. The objector suggests that the proofs rely on an unproven assumption. The author's response is very brief: if the opponent tries to prove the possibility by appealing to the circle (i.e., constructing polygons from circles), then the discussion on establishing the circle has already preceded (presumably earlier in the text, and the author implies that the circle's possibility has already been established or refuted in a way that satisfies this objection). The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

الاستقامة (straight line)

البرهان (proof)

Correction: The author responds by referring to an earlier discussion on the circle.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Euclid's Bisection Argument Against Atomism
Euclid's bisection argument against the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's geometric bisection argument refutes the concept of an indivisible atom.

Core Idea
If a line can be bisected, so can any supposed 'indivisible' atom.

This lesson introduces the first geometrical proof against atomism, attributed to Euclid. The argument uses the universal property of lines being bisectable to deduce that atoms, if they exist as parts of a line, must themselves be divisible. This challenges the foundation of the atomist theory in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: statement of Euclid's theorem, application to composed line, conclusion.
The passage is dense—focus on the conditional logic.
2
Identify the key claim
Euclid's theorem that every line accepts bisection is taken as a given. Understand that this is not debated within the argument.
3
Follow the logical deduction
From the premise that any line is bisectable, the argument forces the division of the indivisible atom. Reflect on why this is a reductio ad absurdum against atomism.
This conclusion is necessary: if a line of atoms is bisectable, then the atom itself must be divided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه وجوه : الفصل العاشر في الدلائل الحالة على نفي الجوهر الفرد المستنبطة من قسمة الخطوط الأول : إن أقليدس بين أن كل خط يقبل التنصيف . فالخط المركب من الأجزاء الفردة ، يقبل التنصيف . فوجب أن ينقسم الجوهر الفرد .
And it contains aspects: The tenth chapter on the state proofs for negating the indivisible atom derived from the division of lines. First: Euclid demonstrated that every line accepts bisection. So the line composed of indivisible parts accepts bisection. So it necessitated that the indivisible atom divides.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter and presents the first geometrical proof against atomism. The author states that the chapter contains proofs derived from the division of lines to refute the existence of the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد). The first proof is attributed to Euclid: any line can be bisected. If a line were composed of indivisible parts (atoms), it would still be bisectable, which would require an atom to be split—contradicting its indivisibility. This argument uses the property of lines to deduce the necessary divisibility of atoms.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
أقليدس
التنصيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of Euclid's bisection argument regarding the indivisible atom?
A) The atom exists
B) The atom must be divisible
C) The atom is composite
D) The argument is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Euclid's bisection argument shows that any line must be divisible, and if it were made of atoms, those atoms would also have to be divisible, thus refuting the concept of an indivisible atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Euclid used a simple logical property to force an uncomfortable conclusion about something people believed indivisible. Is there a belief you hold that you have never allowed yourself to examine logically because it feels sacred or untouchable? Will you honestly test it today, even if it might force you to change?
?
The argument shows that a property of a whole (divisibility) must apply to its parts. Are there moral or spiritual principles you apply to others but refuse to apply to your own life? How can Euclid's rigorous consistency challenge you to be consistent in your own conduct?
?
If you can be convinced by a logical proof, such as this one, that something you thought was indivisible cannot be, are you willing to follow arguments where they lead, even if they challenge long-held assumptions? What assumption in your faith or worldview are you most afraid to question?

Second Proof: Trisection of Lines
Presents Ibn al-Haytham's trisection argument against the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn al-Haytham's trisection argument refutes the existence of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
If a line of atoms can be trisected, then the atom must be divisible.

Ibn al-Haytham, a renowned mathematician and physicist, offered a geometric argument against atomism. He showed that any line, even one composed of minimal atomic parts, must be trisectable. This forces the atom to be divisible, contradicting its definition. Let's examine how this works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the proof come through.
Notice that the argument is presented concisely.
2
Break down the premises
Identify the key claims: every line admits trisection; the line composed of atoms is a line; therefore it must be trisectable.
Trisection means dividing into three equal parts.
3
Draw the conclusion
Understand why trisection of an atomic line forces division of the atom itself. This refutes indivisibility.
This is the core of the argument against atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن أبا علي بن الهيثم بين في كتاب حل شكوك أقليدس : أن كل خط فإنه يقبل التثليث . فالخط المركب من الأربعة والخمسة ، وجب أن يقبل التثليث . وذلك يوجب القسمة .
Second: Abu Ali ibn al-Haytham demonstrated in his book 'Resolving Doubts about Euclid' that every line admits trisection. So the line composed of four and five [parts] must admit trisection. And that necessitates division [of the atom].
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof, from Ibn al-Haytham. He showed that any line can be divided into three equal parts (trisection). Consider a line composed of a finite number of atomic parts, such as four or five parts. Such a line must still be trisectable, which would require dividing an atomic part into fractions, contradicting its indivisibility. Thus, atomism is false.

Key Terms
ابن الهيثم
التثليث
حل شكوك أقليدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The indivisible atom is the ultimate building block and cannot be divided further.
Correction: Ibn al-Haytham shows that any line of atoms must be trisectable, meaning the atom must be divisible, thus it cannot be truly indivisible.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn al-Haytham's trisection argument imply about the atomic line?
A) The line cannot be trisected
B) The line is not made of atoms
C) The atom must accept division
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Haytham's trisection argument shows that any line composed of atoms must be divisible, thus undermining the concept of indivisible atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ibn al-Haytham used a simple geometric fact to argue about the nature of reality. What simple truths (from revelation, logic, or observation) have you neglected that could correct a deeply held but mistaken belief? Will you commit to examining them today?

Third Proof: Division in Extreme and Mean Ratio
Uses Euclid's golden ratio division to argue against atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's golden ratio division provides a geometric argument against atomism by establishing incommensurability.

Core Idea
Golden ratio division reveals that atoms would force rational ratios, but incommensurability shows they cannot exist.

This lesson presents a third proof against atomism, drawn from Euclid's geometry. The argument uses the division of a line in extreme and mean ratio (the golden ratio) to show that atomic composition would lead to a contradiction. By understanding this proof, you will see how ancient geometry was used to challenge the atomic hypothesis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the golden ratio division
Read the passage to grasp Euclid's definition: a line divided so that the whole line times one part equals the square of the other part.
2
See the atomic composition issue
Notice that a line composed of a finite number of atomic parts cannot be divided in this way without splitting an atom.
3
Learn the incommensurability conclusion
Euclid shows that the two segments from such a division are incommensurable (no common unit), which contradicts the idea that they are made of countable atoms.
4
Reflect on the argument's scope
Understand that this argument applies to any line composed of finite atoms, not just the examples of 3 or 4 parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن أقليدس بين أن كل خط ، فإنه يمكن قسمته ، بحيث يكون ضرب كل الخط في أحد قسميه ، مساوياً لمربع القسم الثاني . وهذا النوع من التقسيم ، يسمى بنسبة ذات وسط وطرفين . والخط المركب من ثلاثة أجزاء ، ومن أربعة أجزاء. لا يقبل القسمة ، إلا عند قسمة الجزء وأيضاً : فقد بين أقليدس ، في المقالة الثالثة عشر : أن كل خط ، قسم على نسبة ذات وسط وطرفين. فإن القسمين جميعاً منفصلان . وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع كون كل واحد منهما مركباً من أجزاء معدودة بعدد معين . وإلا لكان منقط . وهذا لا يتم إلا مع القول بقسمة الجزء
Third: Euclid demonstrated that every line can be divided such that the product of the whole line and one of its parts equals the square of the other part. This type of division is called division in extreme and mean ratio. The line composed of three parts and of four parts does not admit division except when the part is divided. Also, Euclid showed in the thirteenth book that any line divided in extreme and mean ratio has its two parts separated [incommensurable]. If that is the case, it is impossible for each of them to be composed of a finite number of parts; otherwise it would be rational. And this is only accomplished if one admits the division of the part.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument from Euclid's geometry. First, it describes division in extreme and mean ratio (the golden ratio), where the whole line and its parts satisfy a precise proportionality. A line composed of atomic parts (e.g., 3 or 4 atoms) cannot be divided in this way without splitting an atom. Additionally, Euclid's 13th book shows that such a division yields two incommensurable segments (منفصلان), meaning they are not rational multiples of each other. If they were composed of finite atomic parts, their ratio would be rational (a ratio of integers). Since they are incommensurable, they cannot be composed of atoms. Therefore, atomism is refuted.

Key Terms
نسبة ذات وسط وطرفين
منفقصلان
منقط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument only applies to lines of 3 or 4 parts, not to all atomic lines.
Correction: The argument generalizes: any line composed of a finite number of atoms would have a rational ratio between its parts, but golden ratio division yields incommensurable segments, so no atomic line can be divided in this way.

Checkpoint
Why does division in extreme and mean ratio refute atomism?
A) It requires splitting an atom
B) It produces incommensurable segments that cannot be atomic
C) Both A and B
D) It proves atoms are composite

Key Takeaway
Golden ratio division demonstrates that atomic composition would lead to rational ratios, but Euclid's geometry shows the segments are incommensurable, thus refuting atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that a seemingly intuitive assumption (atoms) leads to a logical contradiction when examined closely. What is one belief you hold about your own life or faith that you have never critically examined because it seems 'obvious'? Will you commit to testing it against revelation and reason today?
?
Euclid's geometry reveals hidden incommensurability—things that cannot be measured by a common unit. Where in your spiritual life might you be trying to measure your relationship with Allah by worldly standards (e.g., rituals, achievements) that are incommensurable with the true nature of sincerity? What would it look like to abandon that false measure?

Fourth Proof: Commensurable and Incommensurable Lines
Euclid's proof against atomism using line classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's distinction between commensurable and incommensurable lines serves as a geometric refutation of atomism.

Core Idea
Incommensurable lines prove that lines cannot be made of countable points.

This lesson presents the fourth proof against atomism, drawn from Euclidean geometry. The key insight is that if lines were composed of discrete atoms, all line lengths would be rational multiples of the atom. But geometry shows that some line pairs are incommensurable—their ratio is irrational—so atomic composition is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice the key terms: متباينة (incommensurable) and مشتركة (commensurable).
2
Understand the terms
Ensure you understand 'commensurable' (having a common measure) and 'incommensurable' (no common measure). The diagonal and side of a square are classic incommensurables.
3
Follow the logical structure
The proof follows an if-then-but-therefore structure: If lines are composed of atoms, then all lines are commensurable. But some lines are incommensurable. Therefore, lines are not composed of atoms.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this proof is strong: it uses established geometric facts (incommensurability) to challenge a physical theory (atomism). It shows that external evidence can refute a metaphysical claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن اقليدس بين أن الخطوط . بعضها متباينة ، وبعضها مشتركة . ولو كانت الخطوط مؤلفة من نقط معدودة متناهية ، لكانت نسبة كل خط إلى خط ، كنسبة عدد إلى عدد . فكانت الخطوط بأسرها مشتركة وذلك باطل . لما ثبت : أن بعضها مشتركة ، وبعضها متباينة
Fourth: Euclid demonstrated that lines are some incommensurable (متباينة) and some commensurable (مشتركة). If lines were composed of a finite number of points, then the ratio of every line to a line would be as a ratio of number to number. Then all lines would be commensurable, and that is false, because it is established that some are incommensurable and some commensurable.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, based on Euclid's distinction between commensurable (مشتركة) and incommensurable (متباينة) lines. If lines were composed of a finite number of atomic points, then any two lines would have lengths expressible as whole-number multiples of the atom, hence their ratio would be rational. But geometry establishes that some lines (e.g., the diagonal and side of a square) are incommensurable: their ratio is irrational. Therefore, lines cannot be composed of atoms, because atomic composition would eliminate incommensurability.

Key Terms
متباينة
مشتركة
نقطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'commensurable' means 'equal' rather than 'having a common measure'.
Correction: Commensurable lines have a common unit that measures both exactly, like 3 and 4 are commensurable (common measure 1). Incommensurable lines have no such unit; their ratio is irrational.

Checkpoint
What does the existence of incommensurable lines prove about atomic composition?
A) Atomic composition is possible
B) Atomic composition would make all lines commensurable
C) Atoms must be divisible
D) Lines are not made of points

Key Takeaway
The existence of incommensurable lines in geometry refutes the atomic composition of lines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Euclid's proof shows that a seemingly simple assumption (atoms making up lines) leads to a contradiction with observable reality. What is one assumption you hold about your own life that you have never tested against evidence? Are you willing to let it be challenged?

The existence of incommensurable lines means some things in creation cannot be fully measured by human standards. In what area of your relationship with Allah do you try to 'measure' your deeds or worth, and how does this proof remind you that His knowledge and measure surpass all?

Commit to investigating at least one such belief this week.

Rational and Irrational Lines Against Atomism
Uses line classification to argue against atomism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of irrational lines disproves the atomic theory of matter.

Core Idea
Irrational lines cannot be built from indivisible atoms.

This proof draws on Euclidean geometry to argue against atomism. It points out that lines come in two types: rational and irrational. If matter were composed of atoms, all lines would be rational multiples of some unit, but irrational lines exist—so atomism must be false. Let's examine the classification and the logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two categories of lines and the mention of composite divisions.
The Arabic is fragmentary; focus on the core classification.
2
Understand the two types of lines
Rational lines have lengths expressible as a ratio of two integers; irrational lines do not. The passage calls rational 'منقطة' and irrational 'صم'.
'منقطة' literally means 'pointed' or 'dotted', but here it refers to rational lines.
3
Grasp the argument against atomism
If space is composed of indivisible atoms, any line would be a whole-number multiple of the atomic unit, making all lines rational. But irrational lines exist, contradicting atomism.
This is an indirect proof: atomism leads to a false conclusion, so atomism is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إنه بين أن الخطوط منها منقطة . ومنها صم . وفرع على هذا الأصل : تقسيم الخطوط المركبة إلى ستة أقسام في ذي الأسمين ، وإلى ستة أخرى .
Fifth: He demonstrated that lines are some rational (منقطة) and some irrational (صم). And based on this principle: the division of composite lines into six divisions in two-named [binomials] and into six others.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, though the text is incomplete. It references a classification of lines into rational (منقطة) and irrational (صم), and mentions a further division of composite lines into six categories (likely binomial and apotome types from Euclid Book X). The implied argument is that irrational lines (those not expressible as a rational multiple of a unit) cannot be composed of a finite number of atomic units, because atomic composition would force all lengths to be rational. Thus, atomism is false. The missing portions would elaborate on the classification.

Key Terms
منقطة
صم
ذي الأسمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All lengths are rational numbers (commensurable with any given unit).
Correction: Irrational lines (like the diagonal of a square) are incommensurable with the side, demonstrating that not all lengths are rational.

Checkpoint
What property of irrational lines (صم) contradicts atomic composition?
A) They are shorter than rational lines
B) They cannot be expressed as a ratio of integers
C) They are not straight
D) They are infinite

Key Takeaway
The existence of irrational lines disproves atomism because atoms would force all lengths to be rational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that even a mathematical truth can dismantle an entire worldview. Irrational Square Roots Refute Atomism
Classifies roots and uses conditional refutation of atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of irrational square roots demonstrates that magnitudes cannot be composed of indivisible atomic units.

Core Idea
Irrational roots prove magnitudes are not atomic.

In this short but powerful proof, the author classifies square roots into rational and irrational, then shows that if magnitudes were atomic, irrational roots could not exist. Since geometry confirms irrational roots like √2 are real, the atomic hypothesis fails. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice that the proof has two parts: first a classification of roots, then a conditional inference.
Even though it's short, every word matters.
2
Understand the classification of roots
Identify what 'rational' (منقطة) and 'irrational' (صم) mean. Rational roots are those that can be expressed as a ratio of integers; irrational ones cannot (e.g., √2).
The terms منقطة (literally 'pointed') and صم (literally 'deaf') are technical terms for rational and irrational.
3
Analyze the conditional statement
Notice the author's logic: If magnitudes were composite of indivisible units, then every magnitude would be an integer multiple of that unit, so all square roots would be rational. But we know irrational roots exist; therefore the premise is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof does not require elaborate mathematics—it uses a simple geometric fact to refute a metaphysical theory. Consider why this proof is effective against atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إن الجذور منها منقطة ، ومنها صم . ولو كانت المقادير مركبة من وحدات ، كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة ، لكان القول بإثبات الجذور الصم باطلاً .
Sixth: Roots are some rational (منقطة) and some irrational (صم). If magnitudes were composed of units each of which does not accept division, then the statement affirming irrational roots would be false.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, focusing on square roots. There exist rational roots (e.g., √4 = 2) and irrational roots (e.g., √2). If all magnitudes were composed of indivisible units (atoms), then every magnitude would be a rational multiple of the atom, and all roots would be rational. But geometry demonstrates the existence of irrational roots like √2. Therefore, magnitudes cannot be composed of atoms.

Key Terms
جذور
منقطة
صم
وحدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'roots' here means plant roots; it refers to square roots (جذور).
Correction: In this context, 'roots' (جذور) are square roots, not plant roots. The terms 'rational' and 'irrational' describe whether the root can be expressed as a ratio.

Checkpoint
What do irrational square roots imply about atomic units?
A) They exist
B) They cannot exist if magnitudes are atomic
C) They are not real
D) They are rational

Key Takeaway
The existence of irrational square roots like √2 proves that magnitudes cannot be composed of indivisible atomic units.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof uses a simple geometric fact to dismantle a grand metaphysical theory. Have you ever held a belief that, when honestly examined, collapsed under the weight of a simple truth you had overlooked? What is one belief about reality that you hold today that you have not subjected to such logical scrutiny? Will you challenge it this week?

The author calls irrational roots 'ṣumm' (deaf). They do not yield to neat rational explanations. Are you willing to let those facts reshape your understanding, or do you force them into a rational box?
?

Where in your faith or daily life have you mistaken cultural or philosophical assumptions for revealed truth? Will you set aside one such assumption today and re-examine it in the light of clear evidence?

The Asymptote Argument for Infinite Divisibility
Seventh proof using Apollonius's asymptotes for infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the geometric phenomenon of asymptotes demonstrates that physical space must be infinitely divisible, thereby refuting the atomist position.

Core Idea
Asymptotes never meet, so space cannot be atomic.

This lesson presents a geometric proof from the kalam tradition against atomism, using the behavior of certain curves that approach each other indefinitely without ever meeting. The argument relies on the work of Apollonius of Perga, the ancient Greek mathematician, and shows that if space were composed of indivisible atoms, such curves would be forced to intersect, contradicting their established geometric properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the structure: first a reference to Apollonius, then the author's own geometric demonstration, and finally the logical conclusion.
The passage is concise; each sentence carries a distinct role in the argument.
2
Understand the geometric concept of asymptotes
An asymptote is a line that a curve approaches arbitrarily closely but never touches or crosses. Apollonius proved in his work 'Conics' that such curves exist (specifically, hyperbolas and their asymptotes).
Think of two lines getting closer and closer without ever meeting—like the branches of a hyperbola.
3
Grasp the logical link to divisibility
If space were made of finite, indivisible atoms, then the distance between two approaching curves would eventually become smaller than an atom, forcing them to meet. Since they never meet, the premise of finite divisibility must be false.
This implies that space is infinitely divisible, a key doctrine in kalam against atomism.
4
Reflect on the refutation of atomism
The conclusion is that matter (or space) cannot be composed of indivisible parts; it must be continuous and infinitely divisible. This supports the kalām position that atomism is rationally untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : إن أبلونيوس بين في كتاب المخروطات ، وجود خطين يتقاربان أبداً ، ولا يلتقيان . ونحن قد بينا بوجوه أخرى مبنية على أصول الهندسة : أن ذلك ممكن . ولو كان قبول القسمة متناهياً ، لامتنع ذلك قطعاً
Seventh: Apollonius demonstrated in his book 'Conics' the existence of two lines that approach each other forever and never meet. And we have shown by other ways based on principles of geometry that that is possible. And if the acceptance of division were finite, that would be absolutely impossible.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof, using Apollonius's conics. Apollonius showed that certain curves (hyperbola and its asymptotes) approach each other indefinitely but never intersect. The author adds that he himself has demonstrated this by other geometric principles. If space were only finitely divisible (i.e., composed of atoms), the distance between the curves would eventually become less than an atom, forcing them to meet. Since they never meet, space must be infinitely divisible, refuting atomism.

Key Terms
أبلونيوس
المخروطات
يتقاربان
قبول القسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do asymptotic curves disprove finite divisibility?
A) They are straight lines
B) They would have to meet if division were finite
C) They are infinite in length
D) They are not real

Key Takeaway
The existence of asymptotes proves that space is infinitely divisible, refuting atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof requires you to accept a conclusion based on reason even though you cannot see it with your eyes. Is there an area of your creed where you rely more on tradition than on rational evidence? What would it cost you to follow the argument where it leads?
?

Geometry's Universal Refutation of Atomism
Author concludes geometry entirely invalidates atomism, proofs are sample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the entire field of geometry is fundamentally incompatible with the concept of the indivisible atom, and that the presented proofs are merely a sample of a much larger body of evidence.

Core Idea
Geometry uniformly invalidates atomism; to affirm the atom is to reject all geometry.

This passage serves as the culmination of a series of geometrical arguments against atomism. The author delivers a powerful conclusion: the science of geometry from start to finish stands against the notion of an indivisible atom. He emphasizes that the proofs offered are only a fraction of the available evidence, implying that the case against atomism is overwhelming. This sets up a stark choice for the atomist: accept the entire edifice of geometry and abandon atomism, or reject geometry itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the emphatic opening 'واعلم أن' (And know that) which signals a conclusive assertion.
The author is summarizing the overall argument.
2
Understand the claim about geometry
The author states that geometry 'from its beginning to its end' refutes atomism. This is a sweeping claim that the entire discipline contradicts the indivisible atom.
Consider what it means for a whole science to refute a concept.
3
Grasp the rhetorical strategy
He then says 'what we have explained is a little from much'. This indicates that the proofs provided are just a sample; the full weight of geometry is against atomism.
This is a common argumentative move: showing that even a small part contradicts, so the whole must.
4
Recognize the implied challenge
The final sentence presents a dilemma: anyone who maintains atomism must 'impugn the sciences of geometry' – meaning they must reject all geometrical knowledge, which is implausible.
Think about why rejecting all of geometry is considered an absurd consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن علم الهندسة من أوله إلى آخره، يبطل القول بالجوهر الفرد . والذي شرحناه في هذا الموضع ، هو قليل من كثير . فمن أثبت الجوهر الفرد ، وجب عليه الطعن في علوم الهندسة .
And know that the science of geometry from its beginning to its end refutes the claim of the indivisible atom. And what we have explained in this place is a little from much. So he who affirms the indivisible atom must impugn the sciences of geometry.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage concludes the series of proofs with a strong statement: the entire body of geometry uniformly contradicts atomism. The author emphasizes that the proofs presented here are only a small sample; geometry as a whole is at odds with the concept of an indivisible atom. Therefore, anyone who insists on atomism must reject all of geometry, a position he considers untenable.

Key Terms
علم الهندسة
جوهر فرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this passage itself is a geometric proof or that it provides new evidence.
Correction: This passage is a meta-argument summarizing that geometry as a whole contradicts atomism; it does not present new geometric reasoning but asserts a consensus.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about geometry and atomism?
A) Geometry partially supports atomism
B) Geometry entirely refutes atomism
C) Geometry is silent on atomism
D) Geometry proves atomism

Key Takeaway
Geometry as a whole refutes atomism; affirming the atom forces one to reject all geometry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Affirming atomism forces one to impugn geometry. Have you ever realized that holding onto a false belief requires you to attack or dismiss entire fields of knowledge or wisdom? What is that costing you, and will you repent from that stubbornness?

The Fourth Argument: Euclidean Incommensurability
Euclid's proof of incommensurable lines refutes atomism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Euclid's demonstration of commensurability and how it refutes the theory of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
If lines were made of points, all lines would be commensurable—but they are not.

The fourth argument draws on Euclid’s finding that lines can be either commensurable or incommensurable. If atomism were true, all lines would be commensurable, which contradicts established geometry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note the conditional structure: 'if... then'.
Euclid is the authority.
2
Identify the conditional reasoning
The argument assumes lines composed of finite points, deduces commensurability, and contrasts with reality.
3
Understand the contradiction
Since some lines are incommensurable, the assumption must be false. This refutes atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن (اقليدس) بين أن الخطوط بعضها متباينة، وبعضها مشتركة. ولو كانت الخطوط مؤلفة من نقط معدودة متناهية، لكانت نسبة كل خط إلى خط، كنسبة عدد إلى عدد. فكانت الخطوط بأسرها مشتركة وذلك باطل. لما ثبت: أن بعضها مشتركة، وبعضها متباينة.

الخامس : إنه بين أن الخطوط منها منقطة، ومنها صم. وفرع على هذا الأصل: تقسيم الخطوط المركبة إلى ستة أقسام في ذي الأسمين، وإلى ستة أخرى (...). السادس : إن الجذور منها منقطة، ومنها صم. ولو كانت المقادير مركبة من وحدات، كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة، لكان القول بإثبات الجذور الصم باطلاً.

السابع : إن (أبلونيوس) بين في كتاب المخروطات، وجود خطين يتقاربان أبداً، ولا يلتقيان. ونحن قد بينا بوجوه أخرى مبنية على أصول الهندسة: أن ذلك ممكن. ولو كان قبول القسمة متناهياً، لامتنع ذلك قطعاً.

واعلم أن علم الهندسة من أوله إلى آخره، يبطل القول بالجوهر الفرد. والذي شرحناه في هذا الموضع، هو قليل من كثير. فمن أثبت الجوهر الفرد، وجب عليه الطعن في علوم الهندسة.

وليكن ههنا آخر الكلام في هذه المسألة. وبالله التوفيق.
The fourth: Euclid (Uqlīdis) showed that lines are sometimes divergent and sometimes convergent. If lines were composed of countable finite points, then the ratio of each line to another would be like the ratio of a number to a number. Then all lines would be commensurable, and that is false, because it is established that some lines are commensurable and some incommensurable.

The fifth: He showed that lines include rational (munqaṭa) and irrational (ṣumm). And based on this principle he derived the division of compound lines into six types in the case of binomial (dhū al-ismayn) and six others (...). The sixth: Roots include rational and irrational. If magnitudes were composed of units each of which is indivisible, then the affirmation of irrational roots would be false.

The seventh: Apollonius (Ablūniyūs) showed in his book on conics the existence of two lines that continually approach each other but never meet. And we have shown by other ways based on geometric principles that this is possible. If divisibility were finite, that would be absolutely impossible.

Know that the science of geometry from beginning to end refutes the doctrine of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). What we have explained in this place is a small part of much. Whoever affirms the indivisible atom must attack the sciences of geometry.

Let this be the end of the discourse on this problem. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents four specific geometric proofs against the theory of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). It begins with the fourth argument, implying three earlier ones. The first argument (fourth) uses Euclid's work on commensurability of lines: if lines were composed of finite indivisible points, all lines would be commensurable, but some are incommensurable. The second argument (fifth) distinguishes rational and irrational lines, and derives a classification of compound lines; the third (sixth) applies the same to roots—irrational roots would be impossible if magnitudes were composed of indivisible units. The fourth argument (seventh) cites Apollonius's conics on asymptotic lines that approach but never meet; the author adds that he has proven this possibility through other geometric methods, while finite divisibility would prevent it. The passage concludes by asserting that the entire science of geometry refutes atomism, and that anyone who upholds the atom must reject geometry itself. The author then ends the discussion.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
اقليدس
الخطوط
النقط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that atomism is compatible with geometry.
Correction: This argument shows that atomism leads to the false conclusion that all lines are commensurable, contradicting Euclidean geometry.

Checkpoint

A) They would be incommensurable
B) They would be commensurable
C) They would be both
D) They would be irrational

Key Takeaway
Euclid's incommensurable lines disprove the atomist claim that lines are made of finite points.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Euclid's argument shows that a false premise leads to a contradiction. When you discover a contradiction in your own thinking—about your purpose, your worship, or your character—do you ignore it, or do you uproot the false premise? Ask yourself honestly right now.
?
If all lines were commensurable, geometry would be simpler but false. Have you ever preferred a simpler but false explanation of your own life over a complex but true one? What is one truth you have been avoiding because facing it would force you to change?

The Fifth Argument: Rational and Irrational Lines
Euclid's classification of lines into rational and irrational refutes atomism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between rational and irrational lines refutes the indivisible atom doctrine.

Core Idea
Euclid’s classification of lines into rational and irrational contradicts atomic composition.

The fifth argument builds on Euclid's work showing that lines can be rational (munqaṭa) or irrational (ṣumm). The classification of compound lines into six types depends on this distinction, which atomism cannot accommodate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the classification terms.
The text has a lacuna where the sixth types are mentioned.
2
Understand the classification
Euclid divided lines into rational and irrational. From this, he derived six types of compound lines (binomials).
3
Link to atomism
This classification presupposes irrational magnitudes, which cannot exist if all magnitudes are composed of indivisible units.
Irrational lines cannot be expressed as ratios of integers.

The Sixth Argument: Irrational Roots
Irrational roots refute atomic composition of magnitudes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of irrational roots (jadhūr ṣumm) disproves the indivisible atom theory.

Core Idea
Irrational roots prove that magnitudes are not composed of indivisible units.

The sixth argument applies the same logic to roots: some roots are rational, some irrational. If magnitudes were made of indivisible atoms, irrational roots would be impossible, yet they exist in geometry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the short Arabic text and translation.
2
Identify the conditional
The argument says: if magnitudes were composed of indivisible units, irrational roots would be false.
3
Recognize the conclusion
Since irrational roots are established in mathematics, atomism is false.

The Seventh Argument: Asymptotic Lines and Geometry’s Overall Refutation
Apollonius's asymptotes and geometry's comprehensive refutation of atomism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Apollonius’s conics and the general body of geometry disprove the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Asymptotic lines that never meet would be impossible if space were atomized.

The seventh argument uses Apollonius's work on conics, which demonstrates the existence of lines that approach each other infinitely but never intersect. The author adds that this is possible only if divisibility is infinite. The passage concludes by declaring that the entire science of geometry refutes atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic covering the seventh argument and the concluding remark.
This argument refers to Apollonius and the author's own geometric proofs.
2
Understand the concept of asymptotes
Two lines can approach each other indefinitely without meeting, which implies infinite divisibility.
3
Grasp the general conclusion
The author asserts that geometry in its entirety refutes atomism, and those who uphold it must reject geometry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Doubling Infinity: A Mental Relation, Not a Real Number
The author refutes the objection about doubling infinity by denying the real existence of infinite numbers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite numbers are considered mental constructs rather than real existents in classical kalam.

Core Idea
Infinite numbers exist only as mental relations, not as actual entities.

This passage addresses a philosophical puzzle: if you double an infinite number, do you get a larger infinity? The author argues that infinite numbers have no external reality; they are merely relations between concepts. Understanding this distinction is crucial for kalam arguments that rely on causal series.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style and the logical progression from objection to answer to counter-objection.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection about doubling an infinite number. What challenge does it pose to the author's framework?
The objection is that doubling an infinite set could produce a larger infinity, which seems paradoxical.
3
Analyze the answer
The author responds by denying the external existence of infinite numbers. Instead, they are only mental additions of meanings. Compare this with physical bodies and causes, which do exist externally.
This answer preserves the consistency of kalam arguments about causal series.
4
Consider the counter-objection
The passage ends with a potential counter-argument: numbers have their own reality independent of mental consideration. Note that the author does not answer this here.
This shows the author is aware of realist views on numbers.

Its answer: that these numbers have no existence externally, nor in the mind. Rather, what is present in the intellect is the addition of the meaning of infinity to the meaning of doubling. And that is nothing but the addition of a meaning to a meaning, unlike bodies and causes, for they exist externally. And someone may say: we have mentioned that the ranks and types of numbers subsist in themselves, before mental supposition and intellectual consideration.

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage about the concept of doubling infinity. The objector likely challenges that if you can double an infinite set, you get a larger infinity, which seems contradictory. The author responds that infinite numbers do not have actual existence in the external world or even as complete mental representations; rather, we only have a mental relation between the concepts of infinity and doubling. This is unlike physical bodies and causal series, which exist externally. The author then notes a potential counter-objection: numbers have their own reality independent of mental consideration. This objection is stated but not answered in this passage.

Refuting Infinite Multiplication: Numbers as Mental Relations
The author answers that infinitely multiplied numbers exist only as conceptual relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinitely multiplied numbers have no external or mental existence, but are merely relations of concepts in the intellect.

Core Idea
Infinite numbers are not real things—they are only relations between ideas.

The objection of infinite multiplication of numbers might seem to pose a problem for the finitude of the world. However, the author argues that such numbers do not exist in reality or even in the mind as entities. Instead, they are merely intellectual relations between the concepts of infinity and multiplication. This lesson unpacks that argument and the counter-objection that numbers have independent existence.

Objection to the Proof of Composition and the Annihilation Response
Objection against atomist proof and first annihilation response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a key objection to the argument from indivisible atoms is raised and the first response that posits annihilation and generation of bodies, requiring a material substrate.

Core Idea
Division does not prove essential composition; it only changes accidents unless the body is annihilated.

In this passage, the author presents a sophisticated objection against the kalām argument that bodies are composed of indivisible atoms. The objection argues that the proof conflates accidental change with essential composition. The author then offers a first response: upon division, the original body is annihilated and two new bodies arise, which requires a material substrate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's core claim
The objector argues that the proof only shows that unity and multiplicity are accidents, not that the body's essence is composed. Read the objection carefully to grasp this distinction.
2
See the distinction between accidental and essential change
Notice how the objector uses the continuity of parts after division to argue that only the accident of unity changes, not the body's substance.
The objector says each part remains continuous as before.
3
Follow the first response's two-premise structure
The author responds that upon division the original body is annihilated and two new bodies are generated. He proves this by showing that the two resulting bodies could not have existed before, so the original must cease. Then he argues that anything subject to generation and corruption requires matter.
The second premise refers to the author's own work for full proof.
4
Reflect on the implications for atomism and matter
Consider how this response defends the need for a material substrate (hayula) and challenges the objector's assumption that only accidents change.
This response is one of three possible counterarguments the author found.
